*Postscript to Xu Xian’s Elegant Compositions* #1 *(Note: This catalog originally contained omissions and errors, which have now been corrected according to the main text without further annotation.)*

**Xu Xian’s Elegant Compositions**
*Scripture Title: Xu Xian Hanzao*
Compiled by Chen Menggen of the Yuan dynasty.
Fourteen scrolls.
Source text: *Wanli Continuation of the Daoist Canon*

**Scroll One of Xu Xian’s Elegant Compositions**

**Record of the Lingji Ancestral Temple**
The term "ancestor" speaks of origins. My surname is Xu, descendant of King Yan of Xu, a lineage that served the Tang dynasty. When the Five Dynasties fell into chaos, I received the Son of Heaven’s command to guard Jinling. In the second year of Kaiyun of the Jin (945 CE), the Min king Wang’s internal strife erupted into war. I then led cavalry commanders Zha, Bian, and ten thousand riders to defect to Jianzhou. The following year, I seized Fuzhou, took the western path through the Xi gorge, scaled the Deer Ridge, and advanced from Nantai County to capture Zhang and Quan. A villager surnamed Hu knelt before my horse and said: "Just now, routed soldiers wrought havoc, becoming the people’s unforeseen enemy. Today, we behold the royal army once more. If Your Lordship pities their innocence and revives them with a breath, sparing them from calamity, no virtue could be greater."

The troops halted and formed ranks. I instructed my sons Shang and Bo to join forces and, by military discipline, blunt the enemy’s assault. The rebels perished in battle, and the people found respite. When the army withdrew, the elders still harbored the lingering affection of *tiepao*. I pondered Li Yushi’s counsel on leniency and, from horseback, devised a plan, knowing the time had come to act with them. The following year, I indeed escaped transformation amid the snares of the ranks. My spirit, undiminished, descended to become a deity upon the southern slope of Biefeng. Initially, there was no temple—only a thatched hut and a wooden incense burner. Yet during the seasonal sacrifices of *Fu* and *La*, the rites were never neglected.

In the spring of the eighth year of Taiping Xingguo in the Song Dynasty (the year *guiwei*), no rain fell, and seeds remained unplanted in the earth, causing the stewards deep concern. In the second month, on the auspicious day *wuz*i, divination was performed before me. Before the old auspicious shrine lay a deep pool, its circumference spanning four or five *zhang*, its depths unfathomable. Suddenly, clouds and *qi* surged upward, lingering without dispersal the entire day. At dawn the following day, fierce winds, thunder, lightning, and hail arrived in unison, and the people rejoiced greatly.

Three days later, at first light, Zai Shengming came to the shrine to seek an answer. He beheld the shrine swept away, reduced to a ruin by floodwaters. Filled with astonishment and doubt, he prepared incense and submerged it in the pool, offering prayers. Suddenly, a wooden incense burner emerged from the water’s center, its ash unsullied by moisture—sure proof of a divine presence. In an instant, twin dragons coiled in the distance, bearing the shrine westward upon their wings, until they reached the islet where the mountains barred the stream’s flow. This islet was formerly called Incense Burner Peak. Crows and magpies took flight, calling as if to welcome something. Paper money marked the boundary, and a rare fragrance suffused the air. By night, the auspicious glow of heavenly lamps appeared; by day, the sound of metal and stone resounded. The villagers marveled and, seizing the opportunity, built a temple upon the site. Later, the temple produced the auspicious sign of *lingzhi*, and so it was named the Ancestral Temple of Zhi Islet.

In the first month of the first year of Yongxi, on the day *dingmao*, the construction was completed and the matter reported. The benefactor was a man of Henan surnamed Fang, named Jue, who oversaw the work.

In the second year of Qiandao, *bingxu* (1166), Fang Jue’s grandsons—Xun, Gan, and Zhong—presented the matter to Prefect Feng Li of the prefecture, who inscribed the plaque with the four characters *Hujing Ganying* ("Protecting the Realm, Responding to Devotion"). In the third year of Xining, *gengxu* (1070), Zhang Guan of Hejian, Chen Pengchu of Yingchuan, Lin Huaixian of Jinan, and Huang Ban of Jiangxia reported the affair to Cheng Shimeng, Minister of the Court of Imperial Sacrifices, who memorialized the throne. At the time, the Wang clan of Banshan held power in the court, and the people were burdened by the *Green Sprouts* corvée, so the matter was not addressed.

In the eighth year, *yimao* (1075), when Liu Yi, a native of the prefecture, governed Guizhou, Jiaozhi raiders invaded the border and seized Qin and Lian prefectures. Liu Shu, then serving alongside Zhou Ruli in the autumn bureau, prayed to me, and I manifested the numinous power of Mount Gong. The raiders indeed retreated. Liu Shu then submitted a full report. During the Yanshao era, my hidden merits became most conspicuous: the victory of Han Zhongwu at Dayi Garrison and the achievements of Monk Yuan of Wu’an. Xue Bi, the Pacification Commissioner of the circuit, memorialized the throne during the Duanping era.

In the first year of Duanping, *jiawu* (1234), Zhou Shuo, Supervisor of the Left Treasury, and Zhou Sui, Prefect of Chaozhou, detailed the matter and submitted it to the chief minister Li Zongmian. In the third year, *bingshen* (1236), in the tenth month, He Cong, the military commissioner of the circuit, was appointed Vice Minister of the Ministry of Revenue, and the imperial decree was finally obtained. In the second year of Jiaxi, *wuxu* (1238), the edict was issued, bestowing the title *Lingji* ("Numinous Aid") and adding honorifics. The gods rejoiced, the people celebrated, and their virtue was thus made manifest.

Since the dynasty began selecting officials through the examination system, I have been enrolled in the register of successful candidates. Those from my hometown who passed the examinations soared like meteors, surpassing all other counties south of the river. Thereafter, the literary flourishing grew ever greater: those listed on the *Huangjia* (imperial examination roster) were numerous, as were those selected in the highest ranks of the Southern Palace. Their reputations resounded, their ranks were lofty—some held office in the autumn court, some oversaw the imperial treasury, some governed flowery domains, some instructed the floating lords. Even those with minor ranks could be counted by the cartload or measured by the bushel.

Later, Fang Ce of the Henan Fang clan, styled Zhu, and Wang Jie, styled Lang, each passed the imperial examinations through their literary talents. Both had divined their success through dreams at my shrine and received their reward. Such is the breadth of my merit among the people—far beyond the hearsay of idle gossip, achieved not in a single morning or evening.

Since the temple’s founding in the Kaiyun era of the Later Jin, counting the *jiazi* cycles, over two thousand four hundred years have passed. Initially dismantled to become a village altar, its foundation lay by the banks of Qingbu Stream. It was later relocated to the east of the lane, where the present Lingji Palace now stands. The entire household, encircled by devotion, received celestial favor and titles:

I, *Zhizheng*, was canonized as *Jiutian Jinque Mingdao Dade Dashian Xianling Puji Zhenren* ("Perceiver of Truth, Great Immortal of the Ninefold Golden Towers, Illuminating the Dao and Manifesting Virtue, Numinously Efficacious and Universally Beneficent Perfected One"), and enfeoffed as Jiang Wang.

Zhong, *Zhie*, was canonized as *Jiutian Yuque Xuanhua Fujiao Shangxian Zhaoling Bojie Zhenren* ("Perceiver of Righteousness, Supreme Immortal of the Ninefold Jade Towers, Propagating Transformation and Upholding the Teaching, Illustriously Numinous and Vastly Beneficent Perfected One"), and enfeoffed as Rao Wang.

Our father, named Wen, was enfeoffed as Qi Wang and canonized as *Zhongwu Zhenren* ("Loyal and Martial Perfected One").

Our mother, née Tian, was canonized as *Renshou Xianfei* ("Benevolent and Longevous Immortal Consort").

My wife, née Tao, was enfeoffed as *Jingguo Furen* ("Lady of Jing State") and canonized as *Shunzhu Renhui Xianfei* ("Compliant and Assisting, Benevolent and Kind Immortal Consort").

Zhong’s wife, née Xu, was enfeoffed as *Weiguo Furen* ("Lady of Wei State") and canonized as *Shanzhu Ciyi Xianfei* ("Kindly Assisting, Compassionate and Virtuous Immortal Consort").

Our five sons:
- **Chawen Hui**, *Yong’an Marshal and Guardian*, canonized as *Yuanshuai*.
- **Bian Hao**, *Wu’an Marshal and Guardian*, canonized as *Yuanshuai*.
- **Wei Cen**, *Military Commissioner and Left Gate Marshal*.
- **Zhao Zhong**, *Commander-in-Chief and Right Gate Marshal*.
- **Diao Yanneng**, *Commander of the Vanguard and Marshal of the Army*.

Our four elder brothers—*Zhigao*, *Zhixun*, *Zhijian*, and *Zhixun*—were all enfeoffed as kings and received sacrifices in the temples of Jinling. Their origins and generations are fully recorded in the *History of the Five Dynasties*. This temple is the progenitor of all such shrines. *Zu* ("ancestor") signifies a beginning—how could one forget the origin and obscure its source?

The founding and history of the Ancestral Temple have been recounted above. All things decay with time, and without repair, they decay further. Thus, we built halls of wood and chambers of stone, planning for a hundred thousand years. The merits were vast, the expenses great, and the names and contributions of the benefactors remain on record. This occurred in the year *dinghai* (1287), the twenty-fourth year of the Zhiyuan era, on the day *dingwei* of the eleventh month.

Twelve years later, the grand gate was restored, and the plaque was inscribed with the name *Zhiyu Zumiao* ("Ancestral Temple of Zhi Islet"). As the old stele had eroded, I descended this stele and inscribed it at the request of the gentlemen of the Henan Fang clan—Zhuangyou, Fengwu, and Sixi.

**Inscription:**
The waters flow—trace their source.
The leaves of trees—seek their root.
When the source runs deep, the streams diverge.
When the root stands firm, the leaves flourish.
This is the Ancestral Temple, revered shrine of this land.
Here it has stood for a hundred thousand years.
Its merit rivals the sun and moon, its virtue the heavens and earth.
Its names and titles are preserved in history.
Of old, its temple was called *Ming* ("Illustrious"), and the gods were divine.
Now, its celestial rank is that of immortal and transcendent.
Its blessings have been bestowed upon the people—
How shall we repay them? With offerings of orchids and ferns.
In the year *dinghai*, the temple was renewed.
Twelve years later, our gates were grandly restored.
Inscribed upon this stele, carved into stone,
Its origins recounted, its sacrifices transmitted.
Like the sacred peaks, its fame resounds thrice.
For ten thousand myriad years, we pray for the sagely lord.

**Record of Lingji Palace**
My surname is Xu, descended from the Ying clan. The physician Boyi of the Xia dynasty was enfeoffed in Xu, in the land of Xiapei Tong County. The state flourished through the Xia, Yin, and Zhou dynasties, each era marked by great merit. King Yan of Xu governed with such renown for benevolence and righteousness that he received the auspicious omen of a crimson bow and vermilion arrows. When King Mu of Zhou heard of this, he attacked Xu. Unwilling to let his people suffer, King Yan retreated to the foot of Wuyuan Mountain in Pengcheng, naming the mountain after himself. There he dwelled. For over three thousand years, generations of descendants—eminent figures and towering scholars whose deeds are recorded in history—all trace their lineage to King Yan, my ancestor.

My family served the Tang dynasty for generations, all holding high office. During the chaos of the Five Dynasties, heroes and warlords arose in swarms, carving up the land and plundering one another. By the Son of Heaven’s decree, I was left to guard Jinling. Overseeing more than twenty prefectures south of the Yangtze, I eased taxes and relaxed levies, ensuring the army was well-provisioned. The tribes of the forests and caves adhered to the laws, holding fast to their oaths even unto death. The elderly and children played and wandered, joining hands to sing in celebration. Above, none bore grievance; below, none harbored enmity. The people of the Jiangnan region owed their lives to me. My merit and virtue shone as brightly as the sun and moon—though ages may pass, they cannot be erased.

In the second year of the *Kaiyun* era of the Jin, the King of Min, surnamed Wang, lost his state and sought my aid. I led an army of ten thousand, advancing by both ship and foot. The prefectures of Min each surrendered their cities. The following year, I took Fuzhou and captured it. As the army reached the southern bank of the Xiajiang, we passed through Qingbu. The elders of the village came forth with jars of wine to welcome my troops, yet they lamented: "Just now, several hundred routed soldiers hid atop Biefeng Peak. Arrogant and violent, they were on the verge of rebellion. Had your royal army not arrived today, they would have overthrown our neighbors—how many of us would have remained?" I then turned my forces southward, scaling the peak, blunting their vanguard, breaking their strength, and annihilating their ringleaders. All their followers were captured. In the end, the rebels cast aside their armor and cheered in surrender, yielding without battle. The villagers bore no grudge; markets carried on as usual. Not a single trace of disturbance marred the fields or their harvests.

Thus, to this day, the mountainside is called "Battle Gate," marking the beginning of this event. When my army returned, the elders clung to me in admiration and reluctance, unwilling to let me depart. They cut thatch to build a hut north of Biefeng’s Dragon Gorge, where they painted my likeness and enshrined me in a temple. Morning incense and evening lamps were offered to pray for my longevity. My heart stirred with sorrow, and I swore to the people: "Next year, I shall depart this world and dwell here, bound in friendship with you all." When the time came, I manifested in dreams to reveal my spirit. No year brought famine, no people suffered premature death or plague. Divinations for rain and harvests were answered as swiftly as echo follows sound. In life, I could not fully bless my people; in death, to be venerated in their land and sustained by their offerings—this is no deceit against them.

As years passed and months turned, the temple fell into disrepair, its roof leaking and walls battered by wind, leaving no shelter. Yet suddenly, a towering cloud of qi arose, and a divine dragon coiled in protection. The wooden censer remained unbroken, my likeness stood firm and unyielding—all who witnessed it marveled. A villager named Fang Jue first established a temple in the wilds of Chuanyan, honoring me with the title "Guardian of the Realm, Moved by Devotion." This is the ancestral temple of Zhiyu.

In the early years of Xiangfu, customs shifted and the population flourished. The village elders consulted one another and unanimously agreed to rebuild the temple by the banks of Qingbu’s Yansi Stream. Once, the stream’s torrents surged violently, drowning fields and crops. With divine soldiers, I dredged its source and diverted its flow, ensuring it no longer harmed the people. Thus, the place is still called "Altar of the Community," where traces of the past remain.

As mulberry fields turn to seas and things transform with time, there were always villagers who gladly offered their land.

To the east of Dragon’s Head Peak, the land lay broad and level, its mountains and waters both exquisite. I divined an auspicious site and made my dwelling there. Magpies and crows lent their numinous presence; bees and serpents gathered in throngs. Paper money marked the sacred bounds, and wondrous fragrance assailed the senses. By night, heavenly lamps appeared as omens; by day, the sounds of metal and stone rang forth. The villagers, moved by these signs, built a temple there. They summoned carpenters, stonemasons, and laborers, who gathered with tools in hand beneath the shrine. They hewed planks, shaped deafening columns, and laid foundations; they fired tiles, molded bricks, and raised walls. In the ding-you year of the Zhenghe era, the work was completed with ease. My likeness was rendered with solemn majesty, its presence awe-inspiring. To this day, the elders still recall and recount these events.

As the temple was expanded and opened, its halls grew deep and vast—this was during the Longxing era. In the autumn of the gui-wei year, when the reign title was changed, the kitchens and storehouses were enlarged. By the spring of the bing-yin year of the second Kaixi reign, the main halls and courtyards were erected, their steps and ranks in perfect order. Lofty was its height, resplendent its newness. Its scale and design surpassed all former efforts. This was in the eighth year of the Xianchun era, the ren-shen year. Even now, the names of the benefactors inscribed upon the beams have not faded. I reflect upon the ancients, and their hearts truly align with mine. Thus, I dare not rest idle in my abode, for I must safeguard their descendants and the common folk—such is my bounden duty, and therein lies its merit.

As for the temples and images in the west, south, and north, all trace their origins to this place. Though they were later divided and dispersed, the passage of time has been long. If one were to question the white-haired elders, they would attest to how deeply my numinous virtue has taken root in the people.

During the calamity of the Jingkang era’s yang-nine misfortune, I protected the realm with divine soldiers, manifesting the spirit of the mountains and grasses, displaying the fluttering banners and swaying standards. The treacherous rebels were executed, and the Central Plains restored to peace. My deeds were reported to the imperial court. Though I was graciously honored by the sagely emperor, I did not cross southward in the end.

During the Jiaxi era, the local prefect of Chaozhou, Zhou Sui, along with village elders Zhang Shaobai and others, submitted a petition to the court. A rescript in gold ink bestowed praise, conferring the honorific title *Lingji* upon me, and the temple’s sacrificial register was splendidly renewed, its rites further augmented. Both gods and men rejoiced—how grand and glorious!

Yet of late, times have changed and affairs diverged; the swans of the central marsh have but newly alighted. As for the temple’s upkeep, who has leisure for such matters? The roof’s crown is askew, the foundation’s sandals unaligned—who will set them right? The walls crumble in shadow, the murals dim and obscure—who will restore them? If I do not exalt my illustrious deeds, widely manifesting them to the four quarters and proclaiming them to all of you, it would be remiss indeed. Should even a single meal be neglected, how could this remain a place of reverence and divine tranquility?

The villagers each consulted me on their affairs, seeking my brush to validate their fortunes. I revealed to them the oracle, saying: *The sixty-four slips correspond to the sixty-four hexagrams of the* Yijing. *Nothing in heaven or earth lies beyond the* Yijing. *As a book of divination, it determines fortune and misfortune—thus it decides all.* Having shown them the slips’ use, how could I dare take up my brush to compose a text, entrusting and charging you all? Let this be my proclamation: from this day forward, each of you must attend to your duties, setting the mean within your hearts. Plan and labor to establish your merits, that the ruined may be revived, the old made new.

I dare not claim sole credit for this glory; in initiating such affairs, the people’s livelihoods must be ordered. May they prosper in longevity and wealth, flourish and thrive—my blessings upon them are not slight. On an auspicious day in the eighth month, the autumn of the gengxu year, the work was completed, and I was asked to inscribe this record.

My royal grandfather ruled Wu and was enfeoffed as Prince of Qi, posthumously honored as *Zhongwu*. He had four elder brothers: the eldest, named Zhigao, inherited his father’s title and was later re-enfeoffed in Tang. The next, Zhixun, Zhijian, and Zhixun, all held power in Wu, were enfeoffed as princes, and each received temple sacrifices in Jinling. I am Zhizheng, enfeoffed as Prince of Jiang. My second brother, Zhikua, was enfeoffed as Prince of Rao. These events are recorded in history, and my hidden merits are detailed in the *Book of [Missing Character]*, under the *Bureau of Sacrifices*. I have partaken of this land’s offerings for over four hundred years, and shall continue for a thousand thousand more—beyond reckoning.

In this sagely dynasty’s era of unity, all within the realm offer allegiance. Heaven and earth, ghosts and spirits, all partake of its virtue. Humbly awaiting the imperial grace, I look up to the throne’s decree. May this honor and favor meet expectations, and may the people never forget. Thrice I cry: *Long live the emperor! Long live the emperor! Ten thousand years, ten thousand, ten thousand years!*

**Eulogy:**
*Gentle and yielding, the Prince of Yan,*
*His filial descendants are blessed.*
*Unfailing in virtue,*
*He dwells in this land.*
*By winding Biefeng, east of Dragon’s Head,*
*The Lingji title bestowed—his merit to the people.*
*The new temple stands resplendent,*
*Its renown mighty and bright.*
*Shoulder to shoulder, the people gather,*
*Coming to offer and partake.*
*You, the people, do not doubt—*
*Abundant blessings descend.*
*I pledge this ode,*
*Carved in stone, unyielding.*

**On the Stele’s Reverse:**
Some say stele inscriptions must have a reverse side, while records need not. I know not what defines a *record* or a *stele*. Those called *records* without a reverse—such as the *Record of Music*, *Record of Learning*, *Records of History*, *Mourning Records*, *Biographies*—are not steles. Once engraved on stone, it is called a *stele*. Steles employ *ci* and *ming*, both mournful texts, while records do not. Long ago, Liu Zongyuan composed a stone inscription for his father, yet still inscribed the names of his disciples and followers on the reverse. The reverse of a *biao* began with Liu; the reverse of a *record* began with Xu, Prince of Jiang—who would dare question it?

**Stele’s Reverse:**
Heaven has not yet forsaken this culture, thus it lends me its hand to glorify virtue and illuminate goodness. It is your duty to assist me in this endeavor.

Recently, Lin Dongcun’s public assembly presented the words of Zhou Junxi: *"Shall we give birth to a Shen, or to a Yin?"* How profound! These words speak for me. Let them be carved at your side. When scholars ascend this hall and read this record, may they know what to strive for, and never bring shame upon the gods.

**Further:**
In life, one is a man; in death, a spirit. Those who serve the state and benefit the people deserve temple sacrifices. Temples with imperial decrees must have steles to record their deeds. Long ago, Liu Hou was demoted to the god of Luo Pool, and Han Wengong composed a record for him. Though I have emulated but a fraction of Liu Hou’s deeds, I lack his talent and cannot secure a Han Wengong to inscribe my story. How then shall I instruct posterity? I dare recount my own history; let those who come after Han Wengong record it.

**Record of Upholding Sincerity:**
The hall of Lingji bears the inscription *Cuncheng* ("Upholding Sincerity")—this is the god’s own brush. One day, a guest from afar arrived, seeking to borrow my numen. Clad in ceremonial robes, he entered the hall and found the god’s compositions—steles, inscriptions, records, and eulogies. Reading them carefully, he pondered deeply before withdrawing and asking: *"Why are only the two characters* Cuncheng *left uninscribed? Is it that they exist but remain uncarved?"* He sought my answer.

I, a spirit, cannot speak with my mouth, so I borrow brush and tongue to reply: *Sincerity is truth without falsehood. Human nature is inherently sincere; without sincerity, one cannot establish oneself. Between ruler and minister—this is sincerity. Between father and son—this is sincerity. Among brothers, husband and wife, friends—this is sincerity. Sincerity is one and the same; seek it within yourself, and it will endure. This is not the so-called* Way of Sincerity. *If one strives in this, though thunder roars above, one will not fear; though abysses yawn below, one will not tremble. There is no other reason—sincerity abides.*

If but a single word is untrue, a single phrase insincere, it is called delusion—it is not sincerity. How grave it is! In the darkest chamber, though one sees no one, every gesture, every glance, lays bare the heart’s depths. Those in whom sincerity abides are few indeed; those who speak falsely flow like a torrent. If a minister thus serves his lord, the lord will doubt. If a son thus speaks to his father, the father will doubt. When brothers, friends, husband and wife interact in such a manner, there is nowhere doubt does not arise. Once the mind of calculation sprouts, even gulls and crows will not alight—how much less men, how much less spirits and gods?

You, who do not deceive yourself, are true and without falsehood. Though you do not speak of sincerity, sincerity abides of itself. What need is there for ornamentation? The *Book of Changes* says: *"Guard against deceit to preserve sincerity."* The meaning of these two characters begins here. Do you understand? Remember this well.

**Record of the Hall of Familial Harmony**
The *Record of the Hall of Familial Harmony* serves to clarify human relationships. Among the great bonds of humanity, none is greater than loving one’s kin. To love one’s kin is for the father to be a father, the son to be a son, the elder brother to be an elder brother, the younger brother to be a younger brother, the husband to be a husband, the wife to be a wife. Thus, in the *Rites of Zhou*, the Grand Steward instructed the king to govern the myriad people through eight principles, the first of which was *"cherish one’s kin."*

Truly, for the father to be a father, the son a son, the elder brother an elder brother, the younger brother a younger brother, the husband a husband, the wife a wife—this is the foundation of human relationships. This is why the Grand Steward, in governing the myriad people, must first speak of cherishing one’s kin. Cherishing one’s kin is the root of teaching the people.

In the flourishing eras of the Three Dynasties, human relationships were clear among the high, and the common folk were harmonious below. Filial piety, kindness, fraternal love, and deference were practiced in harmony and humility. This was not a matter of cultivation or innate nature—it was the teaching of the sages, achieved without sternness.

From the Qin and Han onward, governance relied entirely on expediency, heedless of propriety. The ancient sages’ teachings on fostering kinship among the people were entirely abandoned. Thus, the great principles of order were not established, rites and righteousness were not illuminated, and the world daily descended into deceitful conduct, its ills beyond remedy. Fathers and sons no longer dwelt together, brothers could not tolerate one another, moral instruction was severed, and the customs of the state were corrupted, until the world no longer knew the meaning of kinship.

Thus, the people of antiquity were pure; the people of today are cunning. The people of antiquity were sincere; the people of today are shallow. Is it that the people of today are inferior to those of old? Only if a sage like Yao or Shun, like Yu or Tang, like King Wen, King Wu, the Duke of Zhou, or Confucius were to arise, upholding and preserving their virtue, so that fathers and sons, brothers, husbands and wives might understand the meaning of their roles—only then might the customs of high antiquity be glimpsed again in our time.

Now, under the unified sagely court, governance is guided by benevolence. The sagely words are proclaimed, with filial piety and obedience foremost among the virtues encouraged for the people. Villages and towns are permitted to recommend those who exemplify these virtues, that they may be known.

The spirits rejoice, leaping and exclaiming in delight. Hearing of this from all directions, the people naturally transform themselves. Some are renowned for their chastity and fidelity, others for their virtuous widowhood. Gates of righteousness and arches of filial piety stand everywhere, each with its exemplars. Yet despite these offerings, the gods remain as if unhearing—knowing the customs remain corrupt, this brings shame upon the divine. The spirits cannot remain silent at such a time. They command the villagers to repair this hall. To the east of the main hall, a chamber is built to honor the kin of the gods, cherishing those they hold dear. Thus, it is named the Hall of Kinship and Harmony. When those who come to the temple ascend this hall, gaze upon these images, the heart of kinship and harmony may naturally arise within them.

The hall is completed, and this record is made to illuminate human relationships. From this day forward, let fathers be kind and instructive, let sons be filial and admonishing; let elder brothers be friendly and respectful, let younger brothers be reverent and obedient; let husbands be harmonious and righteous, let wives be gentle and upright. Strive on, strive on—may you not be unworthy of this hall’s name.

In the third month, the first day of the gui-chou, the jia-wu year of the Zhiyuan era.

*Record of the Divine and Efficacious Perfected Lord Jingxian of Biefeng.*

---

Moreover, to the east of the main hall of the Divine Efficacy Palace stands a hall, its plaque inscribed with the words *Kinship and Harmony*. A scholar, gathering his robes and ascending the hall, inquired: "Does *kinship and harmony* not refer to what the stele inscription calls ‘harmony with the kin of father and mother’—that is, one’s relatives?"

I replied: "Indeed."

He continued: "From great-grandfather down to great-grandson—are these not the kin of the father?"

I again replied: "Indeed."

He further asked: "The mother’s brothers are called uncles, and the mother’s cousins are called maternal cousins—are these not the kin of the mother?"

I again replied: "Indeed."

The scholar then sighed and said: "Man, born between heaven and earth, is the most spiritually endowed of creatures. What distinguishes him from birds and beasts is his knowledge of the kinship of his father’s and mother’s kin. If one knows the kinship of one’s father’s and mother’s kin, then harmony follows. Yet if one must rely on these two words—*kinship and harmony*—to be known among others, what is the true intent behind this?"

I said: "Well said! Do you understand? In today’s world, customs are governed by cunning and deceit, neglecting proper order, abandoning ritual and righteousness, discarding integrity and shame. That people still know harmony with the kin of their father and mother—I cannot yet believe it. Since the decline of the southern and northern courts, the way of filial piety and brotherly love has been lost. The *Tangdi* ode is forgotten, and the bonds of brotherhood are severed. Who still chants the *Lisi* ode, praising the virtue of descendants? Who still sings the *Taoyao* poem, celebrating the bride who brings harmony to her household? When fat lambs are prepared to hasten the arrival of uncles, when plump bulls are offered to welcome maternal kin—who still recites the *Famu* ode? Mencius said: ‘One without a heart of deference and yielding is not human.’ Thus, my heart is restless with what I desire, for silence is not permissible. To the people of Qin, the plumpness or leanness of the people of Yue stirs no concern in their hearts. Therefore, today’s discourse must be inscribed in writing, proclaimed in speech, and grounded in antiquity to enlighten them. Perhaps then the heavenly principle in human hearts—though as tenuous as a thread—may yet be unbroken, continuous, and still possess a way to be preserved and restored. This is what the ancients called *transformation*.

For the present age, nothing surpasses verbal transmission; for future generations, nothing surpasses the written word. Yet if the ancients had lived in a time when their Way could be practiced, there would have been no need for writing or speech. Speech arises only from necessity."

The scholar said: "Truly, it is so."

Here the translation follows:

---

Now, what is spoken of as the "Sacred Father," the "Sacred Mother," the "Sacred Elder Brother"—these are the divine kin, and do they not embody the spirit of the *Nansha* and *Tangdi* odes? As for the "Sacred Son," the "Sacred Nephew," the "Sacred Grandson," even the "Sacred Uncle" and the "Sacred Son-in-Law"—these too are divine kin, and do they not further reflect the essence of the *Zhongsi*, *Famu*, and *Taoyao* poems?

Yet though the divine call these kin, I know well they are but the kin of parents’ clans—kin in the eyes of the gods, but what of the common world?

I said: "How rigid, how foolish! You cite the *Record of Rites* in your question, yet I take you for a scholar

Once, it bore the title "Responsive to Devotion"; now, it is called "Numinous Aid"—such is the origin of its name. Yet what I speak of is not some power to sway men with fortune or misfortune, nor can I escape the duty of giving voice to its story. Though its shrine’s construction may be grand, surpassing that of former times, it is still insufficient to stir the gaze of later generations. Thus, we undertook a thorough renovation: repainting in five colors, sculpting the divine images, adorning them with yellow curtains and vermilion tables, lending them a measure of solemn dignity. By reckoning the years, this was the fifth year of Xianchun, in the cyclical year *jisi* (1269).

Alas! Man is the most spirited of all creatures, and who does not know the love of parents, cherishing them all their lives? Yet when it comes to ancestors, that love fades and is forgotten—how much more so for those of higher generations? Those who till the land, labor as artisans or merchants, or even aspire to be scholars, ought to trace the origins of their forebears, restoring their legacy rather than dismissing it as a relic of the past, fit only for idle chatter in some book-lined study. Though all who read and grasp the principles of virtue may be called scholars, I dare not compare ordinary men to the likes of Yi Yin, who rose from the fields of Shen, or Fu Yue, who was discovered while building walls—these were sages of their age. Even the emperors of the Han, who won the realm from horseback, welcomed the counsel of peddlers and butchers, inscribing their merits on bronze tripods and recording their names in history. How could such achievements be the sole province of those who read and understand the Dao?

In my humble view, when the Qin dynasty brought ruin, men took refuge in obscurity to preserve their lives. But today, the state nurtures scholars through education, summons the worthy by edict, and sets forth clear paths for advancement. Whether one is a farmer, artisan, or merchant, all may be drawn upward to become scholars. Thus, I dare to set down this account, to encourage those who would follow that path. Some may call this hall a village study, a place for warming the *Li Ke* sacrifices—such talk is but the prattle of rustics from Qi’s eastern wilds; pay it no heed.

In the third year of the *Dade* reign (1299), on the auspicious day of the mid-autumn month, the *Shefeng* Mountain Recluse wrote this. *Composed on behalf of the Elder of Dong Village, the Record of the Numinous Aid Palace.*

In my youth, I was driven by a bold and restless spirit, chasing after fame and office. I shouldered my provisions and traveled thousands of miles, visiting every majestic and wondrous place under heaven, leaving no trace of the ancients unexamined. Just as I was lost in reflection, a letter arrived from my scholar-friend Zhang Guilong, presenting me with *The Collected Writings of Numinous Aid*. He wrote: "In the indigo hills of Min, there stands an ancient temple called Numinous Aid. Its deities bear the surname Xu—one enfeoffed as the King of Jiang, the other as the King of Rao. Since the Kaiyun era of the Later Jin, over four hundred years have passed, and their merits in aiding the state and pacifying the people, in warding off disasters and repelling calamities, are too numerous to recount. Yet of late, the temple has fallen into disrepair, unworthy of its divine radiance."

In the summer of the guisi year, Zhang Guilong, along with Shen Jianweng, Zhou Quan, Zhou Yong, Ming Linchun, Guo Yongweng, and Zheng Ruxian—seven men in all—jointly resolved to restore the temple and consulted the deities through divination. The gods manifested their presence, transforming brushstrokes into script, entrusting the task to them and renewing the edifice in radiant splendor. By the eighth month, the work was completed, and the deities again descended through the brush, recounting their deeds in sequence to compose this record. They revealed fortune and misfortune through hexagrams, exhorted loyalty, trust, ritual, and righteousness through prose and verse. Every word and phrase echoed the intent of Confucius and Mencius in their teachings for the ages. Such are the gods—wise, luminous, and upright—that even the brush of Ban Mengjian in the Western Capital, the *Three Capitals* of Zuo Taichong, the *Five Blessings* of Yang Ziyun, or the odes to sacred mushrooms and precious tripods, the hymns of yellow steeds and jade tablets, cannot compare. With gods so manifest, how could one dare to neglect them? The seven gentlemen, Zhang and his companions, reverently received the deities’ words and inscribed the record upon stone. The local scholar Lin Digong Yannian transcribed it, while I was entrusted with engraving the title in seal script.

In the twelfth month, the deities bestowed an ancient poem, saying: *"The stele now stands—today’s affair is for you to record, for such is the will of the gods."* Before I could take up my brush, they granted another poem, this one spanning ancient and modern times, sixty-five stanzas in all. Among them were the lines: *"Where the record endures, so too does the name; you may forget, but the gods do not."* I dared not refuse and thus composed this account of their words:

*Of old, there were sacrifices—now, there is transmission.*
*Of old, there were decrees—now, there is proclamation.*
*Ancient writings exhort to virtue.*
*The temple stands renewed, its affairs complete.*
*Rain and shine respond to the dual heavens.*
*The people pray with ever-deepening reverence.*
*This inscription I carve to endure ten thousand years.*
*May the world never forget, for virtue knows no change.*

**Record of the Yüqìng Hall Daoist Sanctuary**

Thus have I heard. The Buddha has a gatha:
*"All things arise from the mind—where in the mind are they sought?"*
And why? Because *"the mind itself is Buddha."*
What is Buddha? The *Shi* explains: *Fu* means "person."
If not for the person, there would be no embodied form.
Eternally real, without truth, without form,
Without patience, without hindrance, without substance,
Without coming or going, without entanglement—
Thus, thus, there is nothing to be settled.
Where is the person who does not understand?
One who exhaustively investigates the mind will find
The Buddha present at all times.
If the mind is otherwise, mere recitation of prajñā,
Empty talk, false guidance, greed for gain, and deceitful merit—
These are far inferior.
Even when the great chiliocosm crumbles,
How much less can the Buddha’s single sanctuary endure?

Since the Jingde and Xiangfu eras, the Buddha’s meritorious deeds have been renowned throughout the world. In every place, Daoist sanctuaries were established, each adorned with painted and sculpted Buddha images, revered with incense and offerings. Our village’s Yüqìng Hall is one such sanctuary. From the Kaixi bingyin year to the Yuanzhen bingshen year, reckoned by the Xia calendar, ninety-odd years have passed. Though not so distant in time, why has the sanctuary fallen into such disrepair, far inferior to its former state? Its roof leaks, its walls are breached, and damp rot lingers unaddressed.

Those who pass by—though they have eyes, none turn to look; though they have hands, none point in recognition. Truly, it is like the people of Yue gazing upon the sacrifices of distant tribes, unmoved by whether they are fat or lean.

Yet do they not reflect that the labors of the ancients—hauling water, carrying firewood—were not accomplished in a single morning or evening? Thus did the Buddha’s vow declare: "All buddhas arise in the world from mountain huts, unbothered by the nests of magpies upon their necks." With this illusory, fleeting body, I cannot idly occupy the land of kings or covet fine terrain. So I came to dwell in a stone chamber among the cliffs of Nan’an. The rise of Yüqìng Hall adds nothing to the Buddha’s realm, nor does its ruin take anything away. Yet by the Buddha’s wisdom, all are as these people. If such is the case, then their hearts are but yakṣas and rākṣasas—how can they be spoken to of the Buddha?

Master Zhigong of the Tang once met the Venerable Bodhisattva in the Yellow Cloud Mountains to expound the Dharma. A mendicant from Mount Song rose from his seat, bowed, and knelt to ask: "In the degenerate age, by what karma do sentient beings fall into *sahā*, suffering for kalpas on end? What expedient means can save them?" Zhigong replied: "In the latter days, those who do evil are many, while those who form karmic bonds are few." The Venerable Bodhisattva said: "These sentient beings bring it upon themselves." Truly, these words are true.

Now I broadly expound the Dharma for all beings, universally guiding them to awakening. If there are good men and good women who can arouse the mind of *anuttarā-samyak-saṃbodhi*, and with the seven treasures filling worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges offer them in generosity—without deluded thoughts, without attachment, without retreat—then by this cause and condition, the adornment of the Buddha-land’s merit and virtue: can it be measured? I do not speak falsely. These are true words, real words. Listen carefully now, and take the Buddha as your heart.

Then he raised the Buddha’s verse again:
*"I have a single root of goodness,*
*All born within the heart."*
Do you understand? Thus it is said: *"The mind itself is the Buddha."*

In the third year of the Qiandao era, the Buddha bestowed upon Dingguang the title *Yuanying Putong Ciji Dashi Bodhisattva*, commonly called the White-Robed Sage Duke, or the Pan’gu Dingguang Buddha. I dare to compose this record of Yüqìng Hall for the Buddha, to be inscribed before the hall and exhort the world.

---

**Record of the Laibin Pavilion**

When the request was made for Han Wengong to compose an inscription, many lamented not seeing his true portrait. I alone rejoiced. Little did they know that Wengong’s words so vividly captured the spirit and form of his subjects that, by grasping his intent, the true likeness becomes clear before one’s eyes. This is why I rejoice—understanding Wengong’s purpose in establishing the pavilion.

Moreover, all things decay, yet literature alone endures. How true this is!

The lyrics of the Perfected One of Lingji were already exquisite in their beauty and perfection. Yet there remained a gap in the wall to be mended. Measuring it with a handboard, I found it to span over seventy tablets. Several gentlemen exhausted their efforts to complete it, then asked me to provide the painting. I considered it from afar and said: "A wall standing alone in the open sky is vulnerable to the ravages of wind and rain. In time, it will surely crumble. If you wish to preserve Wengong’s record, that would suffice. Should the painting perish, at least my record may endure. For later generations who seek to recount this, let it be said: it is the record that matters, not merely the record."

Now, in this era of sagely unity, the radiance of imperial might shines forth. The southern seas and all their realms present tribute. Gods and men rejoice, and the world once more knows peace. I dare to offer this painting as celebration for our time.

At its center stands a pavilion, its plaque reading *Laibin* ("Receiving Guests"). Before and behind it, scholar trees are planted, forming a "Scholar Tree Market." To its east and west stand two arches, one named *Yuejin* ("Delighting in the Near"), the other *Huaiyuan* ("Cherishing the Distant")—such are the names given to them. The peaks rise steeply, the crags loom dark and wild. The post road stretches far, and travelers tread its path without cease, one following another.

Here the forms of the southern seas’ myriad barbarian folk are depicted: two men mounted on elephants, called "barbarian officials." Beneath each barbarian official stands one who bears the seal. Four men, able to tame the elephants with golden hooks, are called "elephant slaves," also named "elephant lords." One man holds the banner of *Bearing Treasures* and stands to the left; another holds the banner of *Presenting Gifts* and bows to the right. Two chariots are drawn, with four charioteers, two umbrella-bearers, and two interpreters. The rest of their clansmen go barefoot, their torsos bared. Among them, thirty-six men gird themselves with five-colored woven cloth. The treasures they offer are called *Primordial Tortoises*, *Elephant Tusks*, *Great Bribes*, *Southern Gold*, *Penetrating Rhinoceros Horn*, *Patterned Armor*, *Kingfisher Plumes*, *White Silver*, and fragrant woods such as *sandal*, *aloes*, and *clove*. There are also rare marvels, strange wonders, exquisite luxuries, and precious rarities prized by the world—such as the *Jade Disc of He*, the *Pearl of Marquis Sui*, and corals like the *Stone-Crushing Coral Tree*. Some fashion platters of gold, vats of silver, basins of glass, curtains of true pearls, and pagodas of the Seven Treasures. Laden on carts and palanquins, wrapped in mats and felt bundles, they cross seas and scale ridges, arriving in leisurely procession to amass before the pavilion. Gleaming and resplendent, their colors shimmer and blur, intersecting and scattering in disarray—beyond all counting.

Those who present regional tributes must be organized as the *Zhou Rituals* prescribes under the *Huai Fang* office: eight officers, four treasurers, four scribes, four attendants, and four laborers, each with their assigned duties, their roles apportioned according to their abilities. Who would claim that only two supervisors—one chief and one deputy, with two attendants—could suffice? I observe these men: unless they possess talents surpassing ordinary men, how could they manage such affairs alone?

As for the pines encircling the paper mulberry grove and the willows flanking the road, they enclose an area of several dozen paces. Viewed from afar, their forms seem to sketch and vanish in an instant—distant, fleeting, evoking clouds and mountains ten thousand *li* away. The market bridge stretches like a sash, the creek flows like a ribbon of silk. Adorned with jade balustrades and vermilion railings, their carved craftsmanship exquisite, they stand tall and orderly, perfectly aligned. At their center rises a depiction of the Five Great Marshals—Zha, Bian, Zhao, and Diao—emerging in martial array. Valiant warriors lead the vanguard, cavalry guards the rear. Their radiance blazes, their martial presence crackles with authority. This is how the civil and martial deities manifest their power to govern the spirits of the underworld, repay the grace of the state above, safeguard this land below, and bestow blessings upon the people.

Yet this painting could not have been conceived in a single day or night; its creation was surely laborious. Though I admire it, I must compose this record in the manner of Master Wen to preserve its essence. What harm then if wind and rain erode it over time? Future gentlemen who attend to this place will surely read this record. In reading it, they will preserve the painting. In preserving the painting, they will extol the great dynasty’s majestic virtue—vast as the heavens’ height, profound as the earth’s depth, radiant as the sun and moon. As for the Nine Yi, Eight Di, Ten Jie, Six Man, and the remote frontiers beyond the seas—all are encompassed within its dominion. The seasonal tributes and envoys who hasten here as guests from every quarter cannot be fully chronicled by this record or this painting alone.

In the year of *tingtan*, on an autumn night when the sky was clear and the moon shone bright as day, the Hermit of Biefeng Mountain inscribed the events of the *Pavilion of Welcoming Guests* and its mural, carving it at the Lingji Palace.

**Inscription for the Qilong Mountain Spirit Shrine**

Qilong Mountain, an offshoot of Biefeng, bears a temple called the *Hall of Rain-Praying Kings*, also known as the *Dragon Gate Society*—the local shrine of the Lingji Palace’s royal procession. In the seventh year of the Song dynasty’s *Xuanhe* reign, the year *yisi*, from summer through autumn, no rain fell. The fields withered, the people toiled, and the fine grain faced ruin. No deity was left uninvoked—yet would they not heed our pleas?

Thus did the village elders and longtime residents speak among themselves: "To the east of the lane stands a temple, home to the deity known as the *Guardian of the Realm, Responsive and Efficacious King*—a spirit who wards off calamity and repels misfortune, whose blessings upon the people are vast, and whose divine aid has never been wanting. Now that the drought has grown dire, how can we delay the rites of sacrificial beasts and jade offerings?" On the *renshen* day of the seventh month, at the first light of dawn, they purified themselves and hastened in earnest to the temple courtyard to perform the sacrifices. The deity, moved by the people’s innocence, responded as swiftly as an echo or a shadow. In an instant, clouds and mist rolled in from all directions, thunder and lightning arrived, and a torrential downpour descended. Gazing toward the southern slopes of the mountain, they suddenly beheld a shimmering divine dragon, above which a yellow banner fluttered, emblazoned with the five characters: *Guardian of the Realm, Responsive and Efficacious King*. This title was none other than the former honorific of the present *Spirit of Lingji*.

Three days later, on *jiayin*, the skies cleared. That autumn, the harvest was abundant, the rice yield doubled, and the once-parched fields teemed with life, their granaries overflowing. The farmers and elders, facing north, leapt and danced in reverence, bowing and prostrating themselves, their joy unbounded, their hearts brimming with gratitude.

In the eleventh month, on the winter solstice, the sovereign personally established an altar upon the bend of Qilong Mountain, where monthly sacrifices were offered in repayment of the deity’s virtue. After a full cycle of sixty years, a temple was erected—its halls grand and spacious, a perpetual abode for the spirit, enduring through the ages. Qilong, a mountain of renown, was thus revealed as the site where the gods first manifested beneath its banner. When the people prayed for rain, rain came; the deity never failed in wisdom or response. The community petitioned the authorities, and so the temple was named the *Hall of Rain-Praying Kings*, also called the *Dragon Gate Society*. This is the very aspiration of the village’s finest and most loyal, those who cherish merit and renown.

For mountains, forests, valleys, and hills that bring forth clouds and wind or reveal strange phenomena are all called *shen*—spirits. According to the rites of sacrifice, they are duly recorded in the canon of worship. Now that this deity has manifested through Qilong’s timely rains, transforming barrenness into sweetness and turning pestilence into harmony, the people have been revived, their lives restored. For four hundred years, the temple has received offerings, and who among the people would dare forget or grow negligent in their devotion?

Yet time passes swiftly; the shrine has fallen into disrepair, its images faded, no longer befitting the radiance of the divine. The temple keepers, Lin Chun and others, having received abundant blessings from the spirit, reflected upon the past with a sense of unfulfilled duty. They consulted the villagers and resolved to restore it. When they prayed to the deity, the spirit accepted their sincerity. A divine bird descended to inscribe characters—seal script and clerical forms, strange and wondrous—at first indecipherable. They then requested a transcription in common script. The prose, composed in parallel couplets, could now be heard and understood, as if someone had been present all along, though none had noticed.

In the first month of the new *Yuanzhen* era, on an auspicious day, the people of neighboring hamlets east and west joined forces, pooling their resources in harmony. They commissioned artisans to complete the work, renewing the old with the new. On the twenty-fourth day of the twelfth month, *guihai*—the day before the beginning of spring in the following year—on the morrow of *jiazi*, the project was finished. They entreated me to compose this record.

As for me, how could I dare to boast or flatter, seeking favor with others? Humbly, I have set down these words, that the deity may forever be venerated in this land and eternally receive its due offerings.

Long life, wealth, peace, and ease—these are the wishes of all people, and I shall surely grant them.

As for the noble lineage, titles, and origins of this deity, they are fully recorded on the stele of Lingji Palace to the east of the lane; even a hundred generations hence, they may be known. My name is carved in stone—what more need I say? I merely set this down to record the Lingji Traveling Shrine on Qilong Mountain, known as the Rain-Praying King’s Palace.

*Self-preface by Lingji Perfected Immortal of Biefeng.*

**Record of the Temple of Lingji Worthy and Sage King**

The stele of the Temple of Lingji Worthy and Sage King—this stele stands to ensure that his merit and virtue are never forgotten.

Since the *Xiangfu* era, the deity’s responses to prayers and exorcisms, along with his supplications at the eastern shrine of Longshou Temple, have led to his imperial enfeoffment as the Worthy and Sage of the Three Celestial Jade Towers. The Perfected Immortal of High Inspection, Gaoxian Weiling Guangji—later incarnation of Lingji—though venerated in the sacrificial canon, had no formal liturgical composition.

During the *Shaoxing* reign, the realm was divided into western, southern, and northern domains. In the sixth year, *jiachen*, the northern temple was grandly constructed, and the villagers, led by Lin □, recorded its history—ninety-eight years in all. Though the temple had nearly fallen into ruin, it was revived, and the names of its benefactors remain traceable. Counting the years, sixty-seven more have now passed.

In the third year of the *Dade* era, on the eighth day before the spring community sacrifice, Zeng Chen, Fu Long, and others, leading their fellow villagers, carried a single incense censer and hastened to perform rites in the eastern courtyard of the temple. They prayed to me, saying: "This temple has long been abandoned. We wish to restore it but have not yet received an auspicious divination and dare not proceed." I admonished them, saying: "It is auspicious." They then entreated me to compose an inscription for its stele, and I could not refuse.

The prayer reads:

In this era of imperial unity, the realm enjoys great peace. Daoist sanctuaries and Buddhist monasteries—all neglected things are now revived. The village establishes its altar, and the deity dwells in tranquility.

In the year of *Yuanxian*, the Dipper’s handle points to *yin*. The time for sacrifice arrives, and the rites are performed with utmost clarity. Yet the images are dim, their forms obscured. Tiles crumble, walls are defiled, and wind and rain batter them. Fearful and anxious, we seem to have failed in our duty. Thus, we consulted the people and divined by the gods. We cast yarrow and tortoiseshell, weighing the long and the short, the before and the after. Hesitant and uncertain, we placed our trust in me alone.

In obscurity, without sound or form, how dare I boast or deceive, misleading the ears of others? Yet I depend on this benevolent land—what more can I say?

The script turns from seal to clerical, from fish to bird. On the *yichou* day of the second month, an auspicious day and hour, earthworkers and carpenters set to their tasks. They trimmed flaws, filled cracks, restored the old, and enhanced the new. They adorned it with whitewash and painted it with cinnabar and azure. Assistants and attendants, above, correspond to the four stars. If the people act with virtue, they shall receive blessings and prosperity. The two steps bear the shields and plumes, guarding the village and its neighbors.

The six lords, adorned with pearls and jade, perform wondrous dances to the sound of reed pipes and songs. The *Yunmen* ninefold music resounds, and the *Fangzhang* eight delicacies are arrayed. A hundred entertainers, their forms lifelike and fierce, perform. Spectators fill the roads, their carriages lined side by side. Laughter and joy abound, their faces alight with happiness. The work is completed with ease, and I record it in writing.

My words are coarse and unrefined, like one clutching their chest in distress. Who am I? The Perfected Immortal Lingji. To the east of Longshou Temple, this record is inscribed on the stele.

Heaven deems him worthy and sage; my later incarnation bears the surname Pan. His given name is known through his blessings. A direct descendant of the *Chengyu* lineage, he first entered Min. Clad in the robes of office, he was once recommended as a filial and incorrupt candidate. The imperial commissioner, Marquis Liu, honored him with silk and a rush-wheeled carriage. Though repeatedly summoned, he declined, holding wealth and rank as floating clouds. He chose to dwell on Biefeng, carving fields from the earth. Upright and pure, he felt no shame in partaking of simple fare.

His immortal bones were extraordinary; he studied under *Wochuan*. From Dragon Peak, he received a scroll, emerging from the blue and entering the mysterious. West of the Gorge River, where flying waters seethe, he walked upon the void without wetting or drowning. The world was astonished, the age amazed—his deeds touched the unseen.

Weary of the clamor of the world, he made himself known through the Dao. "To hear the Way in the morning is to die content"—thus did he return to his true nature. One day, he transformed like a molting cicada. After the *Qiande* era, he appeared in dreams. Once his transgressions were purged, timely rains answered his prayers. The five grains flourished, bringing benefit to the people. The people, grateful and joyful, offered autumn sacrifices and winter provisions.

Before the temple’s establishment, sacrifices were held in the great courtyard. According to the old plaque, this began in the *Shaoxing* era. The temple was built with reverence, its reputation resplendent. In the first year of *Shaoding*, the hall was near collapse. Restoring its former grandeur required great effort. Only the worthy may approach sageliness, truly earning their illustrious title. The people exalt his virtue, and the authorities preserve it. If the divine is made manifest, listen with singular devotion.

From this day forth, the sage may be venerated as Heaven. As for me, Lingji, my deeds have fulfilled the three thousand. Possessing all, I match the Three Spirits. On the *wu* day, most auspicious, the offerings are pure with pepper and fragrance. "The people, too, are weary"—this truly accords with my heart.

Inscribed in stone, this record shall endure for a thousand ages. I rely on the restoration of wonders, for without people, there is no cause. I humbly hope for imperial enfeoffment, that eight characters may shine with glory. With hands clasped in reverence, I bow my head—may the Son of Heaven live ten thousand years!

**Record of the Temple of the Minister of Agriculture and the Community Corner Shrine**

Once, while attending the Heavenly Laba Festival, I lingered on my return. Suddenly, a guest appeared—girded with gold, robed in purple—standing beneath the hall. He cried out in a stern voice: "Alive, one is a man; dead, one becomes a ghost. To be a gentleman’s ghost is to never dare bring misfortune upon others. To be a petty man’s ghost is to lack efficacy."

"The western temple has its record; the southern and northern have their steles. I shall soon rot away like grass and wood." Hearing his words, I ordered my attendants to bring him forward. He was an upright deity, the god of the Community Corner Shrine.

I descended, took his hand, and led him up, saying: "Had you not spoken, I would have nearly lost you. Wearing stitched robes and a ceremonial cap, one finds it hard to journey to Yue. In this age, only the wicked and the richly powerful may establish themselves. Only those who calculate to the finest detail may secure their livelihood. Your duty is to open the land for agriculture."

"In spring one plows, in summer one weeds; in autumn one reaps, in winter one stores. Throughout the year, toil knows no rest, not a day of peace. The gates of Heaven lie ten thousand li away—who would be the one to plead for your distant cares and beg for mercy? Who is Changfang that he dare command and drive you? The magpie’s numinous power did not spare it from being painted to fill the river; the tortoise’s numinous power did not spare it from the agony of propping up a bed. Why does the cock not crow? Why does the dog not bark? Why does the ox pant? Why is the horse exhausted? How exacting is your reproach!

"Can one truly expect the private prayers of a pig’s trotter and a bowl of wine? Yet even so, the farmer does not cease to plow because of flood or drought. You oversee the duty of agriculture—how can you dwell in ease and delight in your office? Why must you angle for fame and fish for reputation, adopting the ways of the vulgar world?

"From this year onward, if the harvests are bountiful, then the sacrifices will be most luminous—how could it stop at a hundred generations? They shall endure as long as Heaven and Earth. In admiration of your ability, I inscribe the plaque: *Temple of the Controller of Agriculture and the Community Corner Shrine.*

"As for the temple’s origins, it was first moved here from Five Blessings Crag, and many years have passed since. The dates of its renovations are recorded on the beams, so I shall not belabor them here. Written in the year of *sheti ge*, on the second day after the Heavenly Laba Festival, by the Man of Turtle Peak Mountain.

*Record of the Temple of the Controller of Crops and the Dragon Mountain Elder*
The lord’s surname was Gong, his given name Chengzan, a remnant subject of Wang Rong of Futang. In the fourth year of Qianning of the Tang, he passed the *hongci* examination and rose to the highest ranks of office. During the chaos of the Five Dynasties, the realm was carved up by heroes and warlords."

Amidst national peril and political chaos, when moral winds had decayed and customs grown shallow, the lord abandoned his wife and children, left his ancestral tombs, concealed his brilliance, and refused to serve the times. He journeyed north to White Deer Mountain, traversing lofty peaks and perilous passes, until he chose to build his dwelling halfway up Turtle Peak, naming it Flag Dragon Mountain. There, the land lay level and expansive, with secluded waters and limpid streams, allowing him to fulfill his resolve.

The Min king, surnamed Wang, sent gifts of silk and treasure to summon him, yet he would not bend to serve. During the Yonglong era, when the Wang brothers turned against one another in internal strife, routed soldiers scattered like stars, fleeing eastward into the mountains and forests. Hearing of the lord’s lofty integrity, they passed by his gate without daring to enter.

In the Tianfu era of the Jin, when Tang forces marched south in punitive campaign, the elders of the village, fearing the chaos would turn to cruelty, begged the Tang army to exterminate them. The lord donned Tang robes and cap, and paid homage to the two kings—now the Divine Perfected of Numinous Aid. When the king reclaimed his throne and offered him a position in the幕府, the lord declined the salary. Instead, he carved fields from the mountainside, tilling and sowing himself. In years of abundance, when the grain was fine, he would provide for his neighbors who lacked enough in spring.

A humane man’s intent is carved in ten thousand mouths as a stele.

In the eighth year of Tianfu, one day, a shooting star suddenly fell beside his dwelling. The lord, knowing his allotted span complete, received the villagers’ joint veneration, his incense smoke ascending. He donated the fields he had tilled and sown to the communal mountain endowment, to be held in perpetuity for ancestral rites. Hoping the Divine Perfected of Numinous Aid would guard this land, he was granted a shared place at their table. Tasked with overseeing the harvest, he governed the rains and sunshine, tended the cattle and livestock. From beginning to end, he discharged his duties with diligence. As the years passed, the shrine halls fell into disrepair; the village elders pooled their strength to rebuild and renew them.

When winds and rains came in season, the five grains flourished, livestock multiplied, and the sounds of chickens and dogs filled the air—these were the blessings the elders received under his shelter, not for a single day alone. If fathers were kind and sons filial, elder brothers friendly and younger brothers respectful, husbands virtuous and wives chaste, who else could have brought about such harmony if not the lord? Was he not a disciple of Zheng Zizhen and Yan Junping?

O spirit of the lord, I have been asked to record this. I dare to set forth his life’s path, that later generations may know it.

On the *la* day of the twelfth month in the second year of Dade, recorded by the Old Man of Biefeng, Xu Zi.

**Record of the Hall of United Spring**

On the evening of the great autumnal purification rite in the year of *Yuanxiu*, a villager named So-and-so came bearing incense and prayed to me, saying: "For seventy or eighty years, my humble dwelling has been the dilapidated house of my ancestors. There was once a wooden plaque that read *Hall of Extended Blessings*, but it has long been buried in dust and neglect, its luster faded. I wished to renew it but could not. Now, admiring your resolve to honor such an ancestral intent and bring it to fruition, I have ordered my attendants to grind a thick ink of *Chuanmo* and fashion a brush from reeds. In one breath, I inscribed it—characters as large as a dou—reading *United Spring*.

What is *United Spring*? It is the spring that pervades all things between heaven and earth. The sluggish stir, the submerged rise, the hooked sprout, the armored split—all swell and fill, all grow and mature. Who brings this to pass? Do you understand the meaning of spring?"

I replied, "Yes."

Retiring to reflect in private, I found no answer. Returning with my robe gathered, I said, "Your servant is dull-witted. Pray, instruct me in these words."

"You ask what makes spring spring? There is heaven and earth. Man, born between them, is but one thing among many. If spring does not even know why it is spring, how can man comprehend it? Ah, how stubborn! Your words are confounding. Sit again, and I shall tell you.

The spring of heaven and earth *is* spring. The spring of man is this same spring. How could one seek spring outside of it? What is it, then? The spring of heaven and earth lies in its *qi*—warm and harmonious; in its nature—radiant and tender; in its *qi*—nurturing, sheltering, and protecting. This is why all things partake of spring. If the *qi* were scorching and harsh, that would be summer, not spring. If the nature were bleak and desolate, that would be autumn, not spring. If the intent were stern, urgent, and pressing, that would be winter, not spring. Without spring, what would become of things?

Man is no different. You must understand this. Father as father, son as son, husband as husband, wife as wife, elder brother as elder brother, younger brother as younger brother—these are the great human relations. They must first take root in filial piety, kindness, fraternal love, harmony, and humility. Only when the root is firm and deep will the spring wind stir and move them: their branches will grow long, their leaves flourish, their blossoms bloom, and their fruits ripen. Otherwise, though spring rains water them day after day, it will be of no avail.

Thus, the root of man must be tended with care—planted with effort, entrusted to diligent nurturing—so that one may hope for growth, like the young shoots of a sapling. Extend this understanding to unite your household as one, and this will be a spring without spring, a spring in harmony with heaven and earth. How could it not swell and fill, grow and mature?

Having received these words, I was suddenly enlightened. Clutching the text, I returned home and hastily took up a brush to write it down. I instructed my family to prepare a feast—one dish, one cup—setting the table in the *Hall of United Spring*. Young and old gathered in order, toasting and urging one another, drinking and pausing. As I hovered between drunkenness and sobriety, I called for a servant to bring water for rinsing, then read the text aloud, making a humming sound for my family to hear. Clapping my hands, I declared, 'Though not yet drunk, I am fortunate that nothing troubles me. My heart truly knows the spring of this day! From today to tomorrow, and tomorrow to the next day—no day without spring. From this year to the next, and the next to a thousand, ten thousand years—what year will lack spring? I have found this spring, and you, too, have found it. May we never shame the name of this hall.'

The feast ended, and my family clapped their hands, rising with great laughter."

*—Written by the Old Man of Biefeng.*

---

**Inscription for the Gate**

Words must be loyal and sincere,
Deeds must be earnest and respectful.
Rites must be solemn and reverent,
Demeanor must be upright and proper.
In conduct toward oneself and others,
Only harmony and prudence suffice.
Ears, eyes, heart, and thoughts—
I examine myself thrice daily.
Advancing knowledge and putting it into practice,
One may aspire to sagehood.
In affairs, emulate Yi and Zhou;
In virtue, follow Yan and Min.
Accumulate goodness to transmit to your family,
And blessings will surely overflow.
Inscribed upon this gate—
Let your descendants heed these words.

---

**Record of the Longevity and Heavenly Jade Cliff Daoist Sanctuary**

The Great Master of Universal Illumination of the Buddha’s Heart, Qingcong, styled *Stone Path*, was surnamed Bi by birth. A native of Youyang in Putian, his family had long followed the Confucian tradition. In his youth, he studied at the prefectural academy of Sanshan, where those he associated with were all eminent and outstanding scholars. When the imperial tombs were relocated and the celestial bodies dimmed, he abandoned his secular pursuits and entered the gate of emptiness.

Initially, he received instruction under Master Heng Zhenwen of Dongshan and received the transmission from Master Guangzhi of Yuzhong. In character, the Master was inwardly luminous and outwardly harmonious, requiring no cultivation to refine himself. His mouth constantly recited Buddhist sutras, and as for Confucian texts, he never set them aside for a single day. Moreover, he was well-versed in the calligraphy of Zhong [You] and Wang [Xizhi], and at that time, his peers acclaimed him as the foremost among the Southern School’s White Eyebrows—who else but him?

From Putian, the Master entered Fuzhou and first took residence at Yingtian Temple in Min, where he spent sixteen years. He often told others, “Among the eighty-four thousand dharma gates, one must first practice expedient means oneself.” He wished to establish another sanctuary but could not find a suitable place. Not far from Yingtian Temple, in the land known as *Niumingdi*, stood the former residence of Councilor Tang, which had been reduced to rubble after the ravages of war. In the *renchen* year of the Zhiyuan era (1292), the heirs of that estate offered the land for sale to the Master. Privately rejoicing, he said, “My karmic affinity with the Dao is no mere coincidence.” Thereupon, he donated his remaining robes and savings to purchase the site and began construction in earnest.

One day, accompanied by his friend, Master Chonglin Daohuan, he carried a volume of texts and surveyed the land, discerning the mountain’s dragon veins with the eye of dharma. Twin peaks rose like hair buns, vying in splendor to the north and south. North of the twin peaks, a path led eastward. The needle pointed to *qian* (northwest), then branched into two arms—one forming a stone arrangement resembling the character *pin* (品), its three faces jagged yet upright. Perhaps heaven and earth had nurtured this numinous formation, awaiting its destined purpose.

During the Yongjia era of the Jin, a recluse surnamed Yu crossed the river and built his hut here. Inspired by the *pin*-shaped stones, he styled himself *Master of the Jade Pin Mountain*, and thus the mountain came to be called *Jade Pin*. *Jade Pin Mountain* was the mountain’s general name. From its summit, a vein emerged from the *pin* formation, winding like a wasp’s waist or a horse’s jaw. Midway, it branched into three peaks, towering and steadfast, their rounded summits firmly planted—these were named *Hanqi* (Shaking Marvel). Proceeding further, the path shifted with each step, its turns resembling oar strokes, and was called *Lianhua* (Lotus Flower). At its terminus, three ridges pressed into the earth, majestic and precipitous, forming the shape of an immortal’s grand seat.

To the east lay the *Eight Turtles Hall* and *Sizhou Bridge*; to the west, the *Qilin Grotto* and *Zhenwu Crag*. Above the *Mingtang* (Bright Hall) stood mountains named *Huxie* (Gourd Arbor), *Wushi Gang* (Crow Stone Ridge), and *Ancient Pine*. Around the mountain, twin emerald streams meandered in serpentine coils, their every inch spanning a thousand *li*, stretching endlessly into the distance. An outlying peak, *Fangshan* (Square Mountain), rose externally, while internally, *Tianzhu* (Heavenly Pillar) was erected as its support. Below *Tianzhu* lay Yingtian Temple. Two peaks leapt backward, enclosing the water’s tail like a gate, resembling oxen and horses drinking from the stream—playful and full of charm.

South of the twin peaks, the path led to *Dazuo Feng* (Grand Seat Peak), *Yuyao Feng* (Jade Medicine Peak), and *Shuangtong Feng* (Twin Youths Peak). Below *Shuangtong* lay *Baotai Feng* (Treasure Terrace Peak), *Qiyun Feng* (Rising Cloud Peak), *Jinlan Feng* (Golden Rail Peak), *Sanlang Feng* (Third Son Peak), and *Chaoyang Feng* (Facing Sun Peak). These peaks stood in imposing array, their spires clashing like antlers. Beyond them, the horizon stretched unbroken in all four directions.

When Master Yun of Zhishan’s *Bieshan* (Separate Mountain) Lodge visited, he composed a *gatha* on the *Ten Marvelous Peaks* to express his sentiments. All who climbed and explored the site could not help but praise it. Having acquired the land, the Master set about construction in grand fashion, orienting the sanctuary to face southeast with its back to the northwest. Pointing to the extraordinary site, he exclaimed, “Who has led me to delight in this land and take the initiative? Is this not the karmic affinity of a sanctuary?”

The Master’s conduct and practices astonished all who heard or saw him. Though he wished to conceal himself and leave no trace, he found that even in the absence of traces, concealment was impossible. The leading figures of various schools, unwilling to let him go, invited him to Dongyue Mountain Temple and presented him with hundreds of sheets of calligraphy. Dongyue Temple lay north of Fuzhou’s administrative seat, adjacent to the clamor of markets and dust. Those of shallow learning and limited vision who dwelled there were inevitably bound by attachments. Yet the Master’s nature was unadorned and free, devoid of form, thought, knowledge, or attainment. Only the thought of establishing a sanctuary never ceased; he longed to fulfill this aspiration but could not bear the burden alone.

In the *yiwei* year (1315), he entrusted his disciples, led by Master Long, to oversee the project. Construction began on a certain day, month, and *jiazi* cycle, with rituals performed and ground broken. Walls were raised and huts built atop the site, and the sanctuary was named *Shoutian Yuyan Daochang* (Longevity and Heavenly Jade Cliff Daoist Sanctuary). *Shoutian* (Longevity Heaven) shared the same lineage as *Yingtian* (Responding Heaven), while *Yuyan* (Jade Cliff) was derived from the name *Jade Pin Mountain*.

At its center stood a hall named *Yingji* (Responding to Opportunity). Behind the hall was the *Guanyin Hall*, and behind that, two chambers were built to honor his masters—one named *Dongshan Shouyu* (Longevity Domain of Dongshan), the other *Yuzhong Guizang* (Returning to Repose of Yuzhong). The Master did not claim this sanctuary for himself but dedicated it to his two teachers, thereby demonstrating his unwavering devotion to his lineage.

Beneath the hall, earth was excavated to form a pond, broad at the edges and deep at the center. Springs were channeled to fill it, its waters neither increasing nor decreasing. Nine grades of lotus flowers bloomed there, their varieties distinct yet sharing the same fragrance. When the nine merged into one, their wonder defied description—was this not the essence of *Jade Pin*? The mountain’s veins originated from three stones above, three peaks in the middle, and three branches below, totaling nine—hence the division into nine grades, a numerological significance drawn from this.

The lotus pond derived its name from the mountain’s *Lianhua* (Lotus Flower) peak. Moreover, the Master’s lineage traced back to *Tianchi* (Heavenly Pool), and thus he named the pond after those two characters. The mountain’s orientation aligned with *qian* (northwest) and *hai* (north), corresponding to the names of heaven and earth—truly a harmonious resonance.

Beside the pond stood a pavilion, and the pavilion’s structure included a terrace. Flowers and trees were planted, their fragrance perfuming the four seasons. This was a sight to inspire admiration in all who visited—how could one not delight in such a place?
