As for the three hundred *mu* of land set aside for vegetarian offerings, they provided resources for incense and lamps, fulfilling the Golden Immortal’s teaching of the *unfailing mind*.

Expanding upon this was Yu Zhong of Dongshan. Furthermore, at the foot of Chaoyang Mountain, he established a burial mound as a cavern for dwelling in truth—was this not also in accord with the Golden Immortal’s teaching of the Dharma of quiescent extinction? Was the Master not a disciple of the Golden Immortal?

In the third year of the Dade era, in the ji-hai year, six days before the Mid-Autumn Festival, the Master sent Dao Huan to present me with a map of the land and request a record, saying: “The construction of the hermitage is nearly complete. I have also purchased the neighboring land, acquiring the foundations of former worthies. Though it is now an overgrown wasteland, this too is the legacy of Tang Chengyi. I have already begun clearing and cultivating it, estimating labor and materials to build a great Buddha hall, modeled after the finest monastic halls, to pray for the eternal peace of our present emperor. Thus may the faithful of the countryside find a place of refuge. Beyond this, there will be three gates, two cloisters, a Dharma hall, living quarters, infirmaries, granaries, and storehouses—each with space reserved for future expansion. Plans are made to proceed step by step, though I know not when the work will be fully accomplished.”

Alas! The Master’s words seem fitting indeed. With such vision, such aspirations, and such capacity, how could his endeavor not succeed? The only fear is that the Master might not act. I, Hong Nian, unworthy and unknown, dare to bow in respect to the Master and raise the verse of the Venerable Master Zhao Xian:

*At the hundred-foot pole’s tip, one must take another step—*
*The ten directions’ world is the whole of one’s body.*

Hearing this verse, the Master could not help but laugh aloud and say: “So it is, so it is! I shall accomplish it.” When it is accomplished, might you then write a continuation of this record for me? I replied: “I dare not refuse.”

I, who am I? A son of the Xu family of Guangling, my name and style lost to time, my origins recorded in the *Genealogies of the Five Dynasties* under the Qi lineage. I call myself the Hermit of Biefeng Mountain. Here I gather these matters and set them down in record. The Master had the words carved upon stone, leaving them to show to later generations.

*Continuation of the Inscription for the Shoutian Yuyan Daoist Sanctuary*

The meaning of the character "Buddha" is explained as "not-human." Being "not-human," it has no roof above, no shelter below, no walls in the ten directions, no gates on four sides. Taking the three-thousandfold great-thousandfold world as its vastness—who knows what transient combination this is?

The *Diamond Sutra* says: "It is not adornment; this is called adornment." This is why it is called Buddha, for the Buddha-mind shines universally.

The transmission of the Sixth Patriarch’s robe and bowl followed in due order. First, he received the teaching from Zhen of Dongshan, residing at Yingtian Temple. Then, the seal was passed to Zhigong of Yuzhong, who dwelled at Yueshan Temple. The morning bell and evening drum were enough to bring ease and contentment.

Yet on Yupin Mountain, he built a hermitage called the Shoutian Yuyan Daoist Sanctuary. Was this merely for hauling firewood and carrying water, or to display supernatural powers? Every beam and tile, every timber and stone, were all personally handled by the Master. I say his effort was both laborious and clumsy.

When the hermitage was completed, he asked me to record its history. To record is one thing; to add words of praise is another. Yet I could not escape the worldly habit. The Master’s mind, like Han’s horses or Xue’s cranes, was already complete in itself.

The structures included: one grand hall as the largest; two halls for the Dharma and for repose; three for the spirit assembly. There were gates and corridors, long verandas and side halls. There were granaries, storehouses, kitchens, and monastic cells. The abbot’s quarters stood square and solid, resplendent in their completeness—nine out of ten things were in place.

The earthworkers, carpenters, and stonemasons were nearly finished—seven or eight out of ten tasks done. Counting backward, it had taken four, five, or six years to complete. One can imagine the grandeur of its construction.

I say the Master’s effort was still laborious and clumsy. When he commanded me to continue the record, I feared I might fail to capture the true intent behind it. Have you not seen Master Cen? He said, "This is a man of the present age—he must hide himself away." Can you fathom this?

The Master sat in silent meditation. In an instant, he raised a staff and shouted: "Though the hermitage is small, it contains the Dharma-realm. Open it wide—nothing is beyond its vastness. Draw it in—nothing is within its smallness."

Such words are no Dharma-case of Bodhidharma—do you grasp them?

In my eyes and ears, the sound alone pierced through. Thus I knew that the Master’s construction of this Dao-chamber was no different in scale from the Jetavana Monastery built by the Buddha. Was the Master’s purpose in erecting this Dao-chamber truly here? If not here, then it resided solely within the square inch of the heart.

Its framework defies measurement by foot or yard. Its orientation eludes the compass’s needle. Its realm transcends the wild vastness of unhewn stone. Its artistry surpasses the towering steepness of the Ten Peaks, its depth beyond the abyss of the Twin Streams—no source can be fathomed. How fine, how excellent—all virtues □□.

If one can comprehend this, one sees that the entire earth is a monastic ground. One eye opened wide, the other roams freely. Eastward, no bounds; westward, no limits; upward, it props up the heavens; downward, it props up the earth—unobstructed in all four directions, eight reaches. Its radiance shines brilliantly, illuminating the ten directions, like a thousand suns casting boundless light. Otherwise, how could it be called *The Master of Universal Illumination of the Buddha-Mind*?

The Dao-chamber is built—how could it be easy? To record this is to inscribe its intent. I dare not overstep the Master’s gate. Borrowing Mount Sumeru as a peak, the ocean’s waters as ink, and a great slab of stone from Lion Rock, I inscribe and erect this stele, so that for a hundred million kalpas to come, unbroken and indestructible, it may proclaim the purpose behind *The Master of Universal Illumination of the Buddha-Mind* and the *Record of the Jade Crag Dao-Chamber of Longevity and Heaven*.

The hermitage’s history has been recorded before; thus, I compose this eulogy:

Master Cong, styled Stone Path,
Hailing from Putian, bearing his surname to the end.
A scholar by family tradition, clad in noble robes—
Yet hills and valleys shift, literary stars fade.
The world’s tides surge, but he did not contend.
Unadorned and pure, such was his true nature.
His studies refined, he turned to the Western Sage.
In a single moment of insight, not through mirrors or towers,
He traced the source’s flow, a lineage of endurance.
The Eastern Mountain’s revival—he received the touch of ordination.
From fool to sage, he transmitted the true seal.
First serving in the capital, praised for his talent,
Now in Yue’s mountains, leading the multitudes.
He founded the Dao-chamber, divining Jade Crag.
Dragons received the Dharma, the lineage unified.
The dense forest gleams, the canon corrected.
Qi congeals, stone □□.
Twin streams flow, ten peaks arrayed.
Facing southeast, peerless in splendor.
Excavating earth, clearing obstructions,
Molding bricks, hewing beams steady.
Raising walls, planting screens,
The hermitage complete, Chan calm and still.
Its name fitting its purpose, inscribed upon the hall.
Exotic lotuses bloom in the pond’s long furrows.
Pavilions stand like tripods among flowers and trees.
Two masters’ shrines, revered with devotion.
The living thrive, the departed rejoice.
Year after year, offerings made—this record was my request.
Neighbors settled, labor and land combined.
Earth, wood, stone—craftsmanship ever vigorous.
One hall, open and expansive.
Three chambers, where Dharma-clouds repose.
Corridors stretch, long and straight.
Joints precise, doors aligned.
Granaries filled, kitchens pristine.
Quarters and rooms, all in perfect order.
Fields of two or three *qing*,
One stupa mound—all affairs settled.
Disciples Long and others, following the Master’s command,
Five or six years—work at last complete.
Open one eye, observe closely:
The Master’s dwelling—realm or no-realm?
Within the square inch of the heart, traces of labor vanish.
A single point of light, ever bright,
Permeating the Dharma-realm, towering ten thousand *ren*.
The Buddha came from the West—what cultivation, what realization?
Water in the vase, stars on the scale.
Silent, then speaking—suddenly, self-awareness.
What is the meaning? Pure faith arises.
Robes may spread wide, draped over Mount Dayu’s ridge.
Continuing the lamp’s lineage, the flame never extinguished.
Mind is Buddha—no comparison suffices.
Called *Universal Illumination*, the two resonate.
The Master’s subtlety—let all listen closely.
This record’s intent—my dullness persists.
The eulogy lacks polish, shameful in its crudeness.
This stele carved, its years endure.

---

*Record of the Western Grove Monastery*

In the old customs of Fuzhou’s city in Min, local records speak of the "Three Mountains." According to Guo Pu’s *Relocation of the State’s Land Records*: "Before it lie the twin brows of a youth, adorned with powder and rouge." This refers to Wushi Mountain, Jiuxian Mountain, and Fang Mountain to the north. There are also Gaogai Mountain and Lianhua Mountain, corresponding to Fang Mountain. The name "Three Mountains" originates here.

Among the Three Mountains, Fang Mountain stands most prominently. South of the prefectural seat, it appears like a table—heaven’s craftsmanship, earth’s creation. How could it be merely for the prefecture’s sake?

I have often said that among mountains of numinous wonder, there must emerge people of extraordinary talent, virtue, loyalty, and integrity to match them—yet I had never witnessed it. One day, wandering at the foot of Fang Mountain, after a *li*’s journey, I came upon a Buddhist monastery, coiled and towering, its plaque reading *Western Grove Chan Monastery*. The Dharma-gate vast, halls deep and grand, corridors stretching like wings, pavilions soaring like channels.

To the east, the *Heavenly Meal Fragrant Accumulation Kitchen*; to the west, the *Cloud Assembly Chan Banquet Hall*. As for the *Arhat Shrine*, *Jeweled Flower Hall*, and *Abbot’s Quarters*—none lacked splendor. Granaries and storied chambers left no space unfilled. Timber, stone, rafters, tiles—whitewashed walls all renewed. Bells, fish-shaped wooden drums—every implement in place.

As for the pond’s excavation, water channeled to cultivate pure minds—unstained, unsoiled, this is called adornment. The azure lotus, the golden Buddha—rays of white light illuminate the ten directions, pervading the three thousand great thousand worlds.

I burned incense, pressed palms together—like ascending to heaven. The four great elements, the five aggregates—truly, nothing exists. I sighed: this is how those who transcend the world achieve merit and renown.

After a moment, the abbot emerged, draped in his robe, sleeves holding his sitting mat, and greeted me.

I beheld the man—his bearing open and forthright, free of self-and-other distinctions. Bowing, I asked: “Master, were you the founder of this mountain temple?” He invited me to sit, brewed tea in welcome, and spoke unhurriedly: “The temple’s founding master was the patriarch of the Tang’s Guangming era. A century later, Chan Master Jingde of the Song expanded it into a proper sanctuary. Counting the years to now, more than three hundred springs and autumns have passed. In the Xianchun year of *wuchen*, this old monk came from Longjiang and dwelt on the mountain for a full decade. Just then, war broke out, reducing all to ruins and overgrown wilderness—passersby could only sigh. Without consulting others or seeking aid, I emptied my own robes and alms-bowl, single-handedly shouldering the task. Hoarding what little surplus I had, treating it as personal wealth, I hired laborers and artisans upon the old site. Through years of toil and service, I at last achieved this restoration. I added fields to the holdings, bringing greater benefit than before, merely to sustain the morning incense and evening lamps for the assembly. Above, I pray for the sovereign’s longevity; below, I offer the villagers a refuge within the Buddha’s Dharma. Thus may I face without shame the two founding masters, Guangming and Jingde.

Within the abbot’s quarters, I built a hall and erected a pagoda, arraying the ocean-wide assembly of past generations to left and right. Above, I sculpted the Three Buddhas; below, I designated a single niche as the *spirit chamber*, where the true returns to its source—this fulfills this old monk’s vow. Now I wish to quarry mountain stone and inscribe the years, that future eyes may know the origins of this place. Having long heard of your true goodness, I wished to learn of the causes and conditions of the *Empty School’s* teachings—how fitting! With Mount Sumeru as my brush and the sea as ink, I could compose but a few lines, no different from speech. As you offer skillful means, I dare not refuse.”

Thus I replied: “Excellent! Excellent! The master’s name is Zheng Rong. His ancestors hailed from the old county of Changle, bearing the surname Xie. Though his family had long been scholars, he was one of those rare, loyal, and virtuous men who could forsake the world and yet attain merit and renown.”

Having recorded this, I let out a long whistle and stepped outside—no one knew where I had gone. The master said: “How spirit-like!”

On the day preceding the Chrysanthemum Festival of the first year of the Yuanzhen era, this was recorded by Xu Ruzi, former Prefect of Guangling, of Donghai.

*End of Volume Two of Xu’s Immortal Brush*
*Volume Three of Xu’s Immortal Brush begins*

**Ode to Turtle Peak**

How towering! How lofty!—the soaring majesty of Turtle Peak!
According to the *Diagrams of Symbols* in the *Huainanzi*, we know its ancestral roots lie in Kunlun;
Examining the *Green Satchel Classic* of Master Red Pine, we know its branches spread from Mount Yuling.

From the south it comes, as if the myriad foreign lands had converged, turning northward to pay homage.
It emulates the reverence of Mount Song in offering blessings to the sacred—its bearing refined, its form upright.
Like an overturned golden bowl, like a vast cauldron upturned.
Like an immortal donning ceremonial robes, like a jade maiden unveiling her mirror.
Like a warrior’s helmeted reflection, like a nobleman’s scepter held in hand.

Before it, Square Mountain bows as its footrest;
Behind it, Mount Pu stands as its screen.
Deer Mountain flanks it to the left, while Zhihe Mountain supports it to the right.
All stand in perfect order—tall, erect, and steadfast.
Even the Nine Flowers of Chi or the Yellow Mountains of She could not compare to this peak’s grandeur;
The peaks of Chengdu or the cliffs of Yandang could not rival its fame.
The mountains of Kuaiji in Wu, the marshes of Yunmeng in Chu, Luofu in Hui, or Wuling in Lang—none could capture its form, nor dare describe it.

I measure it with my tablet held horizontally—its expanse spans no less than two or three hundred *li*.
I gauge its height with a marker—it rises no less than four or five thousand *zhang*.
A mere hand’s breadth from heaven, it looms before the gazer’s eyes.
Climbing with a flask at one’s waist and a rope in hand, one ascends directly.
Each step brings one closer to the Purple Tenuity’s court; with a raised hand, one might scoop the waves of the Silver River.
How small are men and things! The vast earth lies before my eyes, as if displayed on my palm.

Had General Meng lived here, he would surely have feared his cap falling;
Had Governor Xie known of it, he would have longed to lace his sandals.
Yet those men are gone—who now appreciates this sight?

As for its undulating forms—high and low, convex and concave, uneven yet ordered—layer upon layer, like fields and ridges.
At dawn, the mist recedes, and the sun’s crow rises in the east;
At dusk, the rosy clouds scatter, and the white hare’s moon appears in the west.
It draws in the pure radiance—how bright!
It gathers the lush shadows—how verdant!

When spring winds stir, flowers and herbs bloom in fragrance;
When summer clouds gather, the forests cast deep shade.
When autumn dew dries, the mountain mists fade;
When winter frost falls, the grasses and trees wither.
Such are the rare moments of beauty, the scenes poets and scholars gaze upon in admiration.

Here, tigers and wolves, foxes and hares dwell in dens and burrows;
Birds and pheasants nest in roosts and bowers.
Some cry, some clamor, some howl, some roar.
The hunter casts his sky-spanning net, laying traps across the wilds.
With a summer bow in his right hand and a black-elk arrow in his left,
He captures or releases, steams or roasts—offering for my rites, filling my kitchen.

Its trees include pine, cypress, camphor, ju, chinaberry, paulownia, nanmu, *ning*, maple, fir, *luo*, beech, sandalwood, locust, catalpa, *ke*, *cha*, banyan, *□*, *zheng*, *cong*, *zhen*, wolfberry, *guang*, *□*, *si*, *□*—
The small may be used for shop signs, wedges, door pivots, or gates;
The large may serve as beams, rafters, ridgepoles, or eaves—so vast their uses, one may choose as needed.

There are also red plantains, white ramie, fine kudzu, green hemp, wild mulberry, wild silkworm oak, pagoda tree blossoms, indigo, *di* seeds, *nou* bark, true red, purple *□*—all to adorn the body.
Lychee, longan, *lü* oranges, pomelos, crabapples, yellow bullets, red cherries, white crabapples, *jia* loquats, *lu* cherries, bayberries, and black *bei*—all to delight the palate.

As for its herbs: elsholtzia, achyranthes, Solomon’s seal, yams, *jiaoteng*, *mawu*, rehmannia, *guzi*, mint, wormwood, perilla, nutgrass, evodia, chrysanthemum, hyacinth beans, vetiver, bitter *lou*, fennel, mugwort, *sui* *□*, phellodendron, *mubie*, poppy, goat’s milk—
Some grow as purple bamboo shoots of Guzhu, others as Job’s tears of Jiaozhou—nothing is lacking, though I cannot record them all.

How different from the past! I sigh that the months and years have fled.
Thus, with a single note of the flute, I join the cowherds in returning home.

Amidst the decayed chessboard of the *Burnt Axe*, one may learn from the woodcutter to steal a moment’s leisure. Where rocks stand jagged, they form the chambers of Daoist adepts; where grasses grow thick and wild, they become the hermit’s thatched hut. Here is a seat for meditation, there a terrace for ascending transcendence. Once the auspicious glow of heavenly lanterns appeared; once the alchemical cinnabar of the immortal’s crucible lingered. The echoes of stone drums still resound, the scars of sword-stones remain etched. Narrow and perilous paths are called the *Gates of Drawn Battle*; broad and level expanses serve as *Bays for Resting Horses*. These are not empty words—how could I speak falsely of ancient traces? Yet why this sigh of emotion? Surely they are still there to behold.

Lofty, lofty—cliffs too sheer to scale. Deep, deep—pools without bottom. Each stands in its depth or shallowness, dividing into two or three. Orchids and angelicas share fragrance, rich and dense; wisteria and creepers intertwine in emerald tangle. Springs gush forth in ceaseless flow, their murmuring currents soft and clear. A single waterfall hangs from cliffs ten thousand feet high—white as silk, blue as indigo. When it scatters, its droplets are round as pearls; when it surges, its depths are vast as a basin. No golden blade could so easily cut it, no jade ruler could truly measure it.

At times, a divine dragon transforms—now dim, now bright. It soars, it dives; it emerges, it vanishes. Now it lashes forth as thunder and lightning, now it rears as *□* and *□*. Now rain pours down in torrents, now clouds rise in gentle swells. Its shrine is grandly enfeoffed, and the people never grow weary of reverence.

When chaos first parted and the earliest ages began, who shaped this place? Adorning the earth with beauty, it lay beyond the Qin capital, outside the Han maps. Ruling over Nanyue, only King Wuzhu resisted the topknots and barbaric customs. Guarding this corner of the world, he concealed his light and hid his tracks—who dared praise him, who dared blame?

From the *Zhenguan* era of Tang, it was named an *Upper Capital*. Its reputation was grand, its bearing majestic. Like a slope rising abruptly amid miles of plain, it gathers verdant hues and spreads like a market, embracing the tidal roar like a basin. Its mound is called *Passing the Rammed Earth*; its ridge is named *Incense Burner*. Behold the whiteness of its waters, the blackness of its stones. The front ocean serves as its elbow and armpit; the rich mountains as its toes and instep. Here, upon this land, these people till their fields and build their homes.

Yet how the hills have shifted and valleys changed, how times alter and affairs diverge! In the Five Dynasties’ chaos, ten clans drove forward together. Who could have foreseen the Wang family, calling themselves the humble and foolish? Within, they drowned in excess; without, they imposed heavy levies. Palaces knew no bounds; hunting and pleasure were their sole delight. No fixed seasons, no rest from toil. Birds took flight in alarm, beasts fled in terror—life nearly ceased. Mountains were left barren, forests stripped—all turned to desolate mounds.

Was it not the Su clan’s repeated rise that withered the Eyebrow Mountain? As the saying goes, the matter is no lie. From *Jianlong* to *Xiangfu*, the court’s civil rule flourished; village schools and classical learning thrived. In time, great scholars and renowned officials emerged, aiding and promoting one another without hesitation. This place led all prefectures, breaking through the path to office. Crimson and purple adorned their backs; carriages and horses filled their gates.

Once, this peak’s numen shone through the excellence of things. Now, this peak’s essence resides in the store of men. Alas! North and south, east and west—time flows like the shuttle, yet this peak does not age. Entrusted with divinity, it was granted noble rank. Its honors increased, its wisdom never faltered, guiding people toward goodness. Though the literary spirit has not perished, the Dao hides in secrecy, subtle and obscure. I know my account may seem strange, fearing it borders on the dubious. I feel great shame before Cao Zhi’s grand principles, deep regret before Zuo Si’s decade of toil. Let this be my *Capture of the Unicorn* brush—lest it invite the scorn of being fit only to cover a wine jar.

*Ode to the Lingji Palace*

Dragons carve tortoises, turtles peddle books—how strange and uncanny! In the *Taiyuan* era of the *Jiao* dynasty, silence was not the way; I sought answers in the earliest images. Fu Xi and Nü Wa questioned the cause, yet remained dim and unenlightened. Only when the dark crow’s revelation came did they suddenly create and return to it. In profound obscurity, the craft of Chui aligned the compass—patterns emerged, intent was set. This was no mere omen from banquet mats or wine vessels; that numinous breath remained unknowable.

I observe the reclusive one, faint and indistinct, guarding the profound mystery in silence. Lightning flashes and vanishes in an instant—how fleeting! To fathom the universe is to reach its limits—who can foresee its end? Like the earth itself, it stretches without bound. Thus the Five Emperors stand tall, the Three Sovereigns unfold in succession. Divine parks have their regulations, sacred halls their standards. Treading the present with measured steps, who now strays or falters?

The nine heavens stretch vast and distant—where to place, where to attach? Why descend so sharply to dwell upon Bie Peak? Ascending winding paths to the splendid altar, I arrive at dawn’s first light. Heaven gazes down from azure heights; earth is piled into hills and mountains. Clouds twist and waves writhe—its foundations coil and twist, beginning in Dongping. Tracing back to the Tang’s sudden *□*, its roots split the tripod to form peaks. Towering, like a docile camel’s team; lofty, like a crimson steed and azure dragon.

Beholding the winding streams of lotus blossoms, I draw in pure numen and feel my heart refreshed. The sacred mountain’s *□* stands majestic, draped in lush, verdant qi. The nine wards and nine paths spread in disarray, like wells and houses linked in rows. Morning tides, evening ebbs—Dao flows vast and surging. Truly, mountains and rivers, earth spirits and ghosts, the Queen Mother nurtures the numinous.

In the *Zhenghe* era, officials were consulted to build it. Its solemn uprightness was grand; earth, wood, and stonework stood firm. Yet the columns of whetstone gleam, the carved jade pillars rise sheer. Flying eaves soar, tiered roofs ascend. Layered beams and interlocking brackets spread like wings, rich and intricate. Double rafters and stacked arches rise ever higher, tier upon tier.

The vermilion gates of layered pavilions unfold grandly, their grandeur rivaling the stone drums of Qiyang. The low walls and embankments stand in splendid ruggedness, resplendent in red and blue. Secret chambers and secluded halls loom jagged and deep, stretching into the boundless haze. The eastern corridors and western wings rise in towering succession, embracing the land’s contours in mutual embrace.

Here too are screens of kingfisher feathers and pearls, silken nets of peacock plumes in ordered ranks, and golden geese unfurled. Embroidered hems are stitched, woven mats spread beneath. They receive the sacred talismans of Mount Gou, moved by the golden vines of Manlu. Yet these are not enough to evoke antiquity—interspersed with glistening glass, they weave a tapestry of splendor.

Vermilion lingzhi clouds surge in swirling mists; auspicious incense from Boshan censers billows thick and fragrant. The wind sings through jade-railed cedar and pine; the moon hangs upon the patterned windows of locust and cassia. Some enclose earth to pool springs, their murmuring waters alive with rippling currents. Carp and mandarin fish dart through clear eddies, their tails flashing. The passage of accumulated blessings opens vast, gathering the great thousand worlds into a single grain.

The Dragon’s Head Peak soars to towering heights, its lofty summits circling the numinous pagoda. Outer structures link in endless succession, front and rear merging seamlessly. Forms vary, sights diverge—none can be fully described. Even the steps ascend in graded ranks; the guards’ formations follow strict commands. To the left, voices hush; to the right, silence reigns. The demons and specters stand firm, their shoulders laden with shields and spears. Harshly they bar the way, permitting no breath of disobedience. The reckless halt, hands bound, awaiting orders.

On the eve of rituals, vigil is kept with utmost care. Heads lift and bow in measured rhythm, advancing and retreating in turn. Faces are composed, expressions solemn. Eyes hold sincerity; reverence is etched in every gesture. The painted figures bristle with ferocity, their fiery auras blazing fiercely—these are the stalwart warriors. Vigilant and imposing, they stand like jade-clad soldiers. Tiger-headed, swallow-chinned, their strategies drawn from dragon’s arts and leopard’s cunning. Bows and arrows arrayed, banners and pennants raised, they wield halberds and brandish axes. Their movements leave no trace, their schemes hidden in the unfathomable. Like the ramparts of a willow camp, they display the order of a well-guarded encampment.

Thus they sever ties with factions, distance themselves from distant lands. From remote peaks and across the seas, tribute flows in ceaseless streams. Behold this hall—here guests and envoys find respite. Let it be recorded as a royal assembly, celebrating the Great Peace of this day.

With reverence, we extol the Emperor: may his reign span ten thousand years, a father and mother to all. Under heaven, all is the sovereign’s domain. He refines culture and stills warfare. Loyalty is his armor, trust his helmet; ritual his shield, righteousness his bulwark. He gathers poets in literary societies, hunts for talent in the forests of words. He roams the ink fields, dispatches envoys to the realm of brush and silk, drawing the multitudes to lofty brilliance. He summons auspicious clouds, □ the white elders. The Eastern Wall shines radiant, a dazzling spectacle to behold.

How grand! The Lingyan Pavilion, where the finest figures gather, all girded in gold and robed in purple. Like the Western Liang’s lion-dancing Hu, or the acrobats of Xunzhuang, they perform with dazzling agility. Veils swirl, movements blur—mastering the arcane arts of Zanggan, the melodies of the Pear Garden rise in resonant song. Here are the beauties of Chu, the charms of Wu, the maidens of Qin, the sisters of Wei. Powdered faces, dark brows, adorned in silks and brocades, adorned with gold and pearls. They feast on fine delicacies, sated with meat from the imperial cauldrons. Eight treasures flow in abundance, apportioned from the imperial kitchens. The assembled concubines vie in splendor—what path of indulgence is beyond reach? Yet the Emperor’s heart remains discerning, allowing no slack in skill, no laxity in pleasure for the eyes and ears.

The gates of heaven stand wide, unbarred, with no steps to ascend. The glory of his name and virtue rivals the sun and moon. Even a weight of ten thousand jun would not suffice to measure his worth. Yet how does he govern with effortless ease, ever vigilant as if in fear? He cherishes kin and fosters harmony, reciting the verses of *Famu*. The Lord of Lu feasts in joy, offering the hymns of the *Biyong*. The Hall of Names bears woven characters as its rule. Subtly it conveys its meaning, perhaps nearly enough to warn the multitude of cares.

The *Yu* dynasty’s omens of loss and gain were once divined—let them serve as a lesson. The literary legacy remains unlost, like Yan and Kong of old. The calligraphy of Huiping and seal scripts of Zhuanxu may be inscribed on the halls of the Xuanyuan. Breathing like bears, still as turtles—observing their techniques, even the later masters Huang, Ren, Xu, and Dong pale in comparison. Who among men could rival this? Only he may be proclaimed and enfeoffed, receiving heaven’s favor.

Again, the thunder gods are summoned to clear the path with rumbling cries; the rain masters are commanded to wash away the dust. Feilian drives the winds with fierce shouts. Their voices ring out, the Divine Mother hurls her might. The great procession begins, chariots drawn by dragons. Cloud banners flutter, eight simurghs and five phoenixes swirl in abundance. They converge in welcome, ascending on pure qi. The journey presses on, passing through the gates of heaven to reach the Purple Court. There is no pause in coming or going—below, inverted reflections; above, radiant light. A hundred flavors, fine wines offered with fragrant orchids. Though the halls of Du Heng stand serene, how can peace be assured forever? May your sacrifices endure for ten thousand ages.

*Wenxing Hermitage Rhapsody*
One day, a gentleman of refined tastes approached me, incense in sleeve, and said: "In our homeland, palaces and temples each have their steles, and the Hall of Accumulated Blessings has its record. The pagoda of Wenxing Hermitage, as a Daoist sanctuary, cannot be dismissed as insignificant—why not compose a few words for it?"

I do not dare to lag behind, but as the sanctuary nears ruin, no one has yet emerged to restore it to its former state. Thus, I feel shame. Now, prompted by your inquiry, I gather these details to encourage those with affinity.

Thus, I compose this rhapsody:

Turtle Peak rises ten thousand fathoms high, a mere grasp from heaven. Winding its way here, who knows how many rises and falls it has known? Suddenly, it unfurls into three verdant peaks—here stand your hut, your home. Bowing to the encircling ridges in homage, it gazes west and lingers in hesitation.

No donkey’s hooves could tread this ground—only the dragon and elephant make their dens here. Enshrined within is the Tathāgata’s true relic, each granule gleaming like golden peaches inlaid with jade. Wrapped in felt, bundled in mats, borne by camel from the Western Regions, it was set as the pagoda’s heart. Hewn from the mountain’s stone with water-polished edges, its seven-tiered bones ascend in towering splendor.

A thousand Buddhas manifest in its form, carved with wind-driven chisels and divine precision. A sacred flame burns cold and blue, illuminating the Dipper and Ox stars above. Beheld by humans, devas, ghosts, and spirits, all rejoice in my resolve—though some may fear its fulfillment. Morning and evening, devotees bow in reverence, their devotion deepening at the dark of the moon, its waxing, its waning, and its return.

This was the former abbot’s hermitage, where the monk Xiū composed his gāthās. A descendant of Bi Gan named Huaixian, he was the village’s pillar of generosity. With the wooden fish’s startling knock, his voice echoed through the night like a mountain crane’s call. His merit perfected in the Hall of Many Fragrances—one bowl, one spoon.

The stele inscription from the Yuánfú era still marks the years, though words cannot fully capture its tale. Here, I merely sketch its broad strokes. The name *Pure Wisdom* once resounded in antiquity, speaking of the Western Paradise’s bliss. *Wenxing* shines in the present, bearing the intent of the scholar-gentleman.

Some dedicate halls to Confucius, aspiring to learning; others worship the Primal Sovereign, seeking official rank. The Hall of Vast Kindness stands here, the Pavilion of Pure Integrity beside it. To the right, they pay homage to the Dark Heaven; to the left, they gaze upon the Eastern Peak. They invite the guiding masters of Sukhāvatī, seated upon blue lotuses in radiant splendor.

A thousand wheels of wisdom’s power manifest in their forms, unadorned by leaden cosmetics yet draped in jeweled garlands. Like Yuanying’s dazzling white robes, some display their simplicity; like Mingjue’s golden railing’s luster, others proclaim their elegance. Through medicine, they heal and revive the people, needing no ginseng or cassia. Through filial piety, they elevate themselves, transforming the world’s shallow wickedness.

The hall is named *Assembly of Attainment*, its numinous presence profound. A thousand gates spread like five phoenixes in flight, eaves carved high and intricate. Chambers and storerooms stand in order, kitchens and baths rebuilt anew. Zēng Yóu’s brushwork adorns the halls with murals, though some are marred by time’s soot and whitewash.

With all vessels prepared, none remain steadfast to exert their strength. Yet each endeavor reaches completion—not through a single morning or evening’s work. Restored in the Xuānhé era by Zhang, Wu, and Guo, it was later expanded by Xu, Zhou, and others. From Yuánfú to Jǐngyán, renovations and renewals have numbered many.

Praise their loyalty and diligence, which shall plant roots for eons as countless as river sands. How dare I abandon my heart and let it fall to ruin, bowing and rising without shame? Yet the Torch Dragon’s light does not endure forever; the incense’s fragrance fades into obscurity. The musk’s embers have long since died—who can fan them back to life? The great edifice teeters on collapse—who can prop it with a single timber?

Mourning the ancients who vanished like the wind, with whom can I consult? Those yet to come, I shall not hear. Alone, I sigh in sorrow, my cap askew, muttering: *What is this?*

Mountains and valleys shift and intertwine—what era’s path is this? Why scurry like ants on a snail’s horn? Why abandon the root to chase the branch? Why lament aging too soon, shrinking in shame? Why fret in ignorance? Why, like a stolen carriage, meet no Bole to recognize its worth? Why, though skilled in painting, fail to reach the frontier’s craftsmen and remain a mere ink-stained scholar? Why do the ancients leave traces, yet this path is blocked? Why speak of gains without substance? When will the neglected repairs be addressed?

From *Self-Defense of My Writing*
What I have composed, others deem impractical and absurd.

Today’s absurdities may become tomorrow’s enfeoffments and inherited titles. To regard these as proofs of merit and steps to advancement—such is the ignorance of men like you. In former times, works like the *Soushen Ji*, *Shenyi Zhi*, *Youguai Ji*, *Shanhaijing*, and *Youming Lu* were all dismissed as absurd. Yet today, who reads them without a sigh of admiration? Who would say otherwise?

I have read the words of the Sage: *"The Master did not speak of prodigies, force, disorder, or spirits."* Yet in the *Capture of the Lin* and the *Oaths of Alliance*, omens and portents appear repeatedly, recorded without omission. Some criticize these passages, attributing them to the mouths of Gongyang, Zuoshi, and Guliang. But the one who first shaped such accounts cannot evade responsibility—hence the saying: *"Those who understand me do so through the* Spring and Autumn Annals; *those who condemn me do so through the* Spring and Autumn Annals."* How profound is the difficulty of discerning true talent!

Literature is the public instrument of the world; if it is not in error, what need is there for debate? My own works—I know not whether they are right or wrong. I ask only that they be examined. When Duan Wenchang composed the *Stele of Pingcai*, Prefect Chen Xiang removed it, valuing only the prose of Han Changli. Though Changli is revered by later generations, how could he not know what Su the Academician meant when he said: *"I do not know if the world contains verses like Duan Wenchang’s—how does he compare to Duan Wenchang?"* My own works, though lacking Duan Wenchang’s talent, may be preserved; they must not be discarded. If they are cast aside, even if this record remains, who will speak for it?

I do not seek to preserve these writings to boast before the present age, dazzle future generations, or deceive the ears of the multitude. Rather, when later men attend to the temple rites, reading my record and witnessing my conduct, they will not deceive themselves in the darkness but feel reverence for the spirits arise naturally within them. Since the record is already written, it must be engraved and transcribed—only the right person can be entrusted with this task. Thus, I dare to appeal to your judgment, certain that you will have your reasons.

Dongpo once said: *"In my life, I have never composed eulogies, epitaphs, or tomb steles for others."* Yet when he wrote the biographies of Minister Sima Wen and Duke Fu Han, he declared: *"I dared not firmly refuse, though it was not my true inclination."* If you undertake this stele, is it not to assume the role Dongpo claimed for himself?

Nevertheless, Cai Yong said to Lu Zhi: *"I have written many steles and records, all with some regret—only the* Stele for Guo Youdao *leaves me without shame."* I dare make this plea with the same conviction.

*A Treatise to Silence Slander*

Since assuming my sacred office in the Dao, after universally delivering all beings, I have never once engaged in worldly intercourse. Even when I have uttered a few words or penned a single character, they have been nothing but inducements toward goodness. Yet if the recipient’s essence is not pure enough to resonate, no sign or omen will respond. As for floods, droughts, epidemics, or unforeseen calamities—these are all due to my own lack of virtue. I can only tremble in fear, reflect deeply, and exhaust every means of averting and dispelling them.

Impartial and selfless, I answer doubts as swiftly as an echo. Yet today’s scribes and chroniclers cannot be trusted, for among them lurk those who fabricate illusions—crafty deceivers who borrow the guises of fox spirits and rat demons. They peer through doors, lurk in empty chambers, and embellish their tales, casting lines to hook the gullible and boast of their exploits. How can they claim that men may be deceived, yet the gods deceived as well? As the saying goes: *"One may be fooled, but not utterly deluded."* Fish eyes and true pearls—genuine and counterfeit are plain to see. Let the discerning not be led astray, lest they bring shame upon the divine.

Thus, I set down these words to silence slander:

*The gods inquire in mystery, dwelling in the unseen.*
*How then do brushstrokes transform, their numen sharp and bright?*
*Strung like pearls, inlaid with jade, they gleam like sun and stars.*
*Ban and Zhang’s steeds were unmatched, their worth beyond compare.*
*The life-saving scriptures of Han Kang endure like flowing streams.*
*Guo Jingchun’s geomancy charts the hidden paths of yin and yang.*
*Followers throng with provisions, their carriages blocking the gates.*
*Virtue engraved in human hearts shall last ten thousand years.*

Yet now it is otherwise: the divine conceals itself in heaven.
Appearing without, vanishing within—such is called immortality.
Vague and indistinct, silent and wordless.
Who could have foreseen that night-rats would borrow the fox’s cry,
Twisting truth into confusion, stirring men’s indignation?
Seeking the gods’ favor, they prepare offerings, both male and female.
Whose fault is this? The wretched knave, severed and cast aside—
*"One may be deceived, but not utterly deluded."*
Dare they mock me? Let their sins accumulate until their bodies perish,
Punished by men and cursed by ghosts.

You scholars in your caps, shaped like men yet not men—
Do you think the gods, ever-changing, are deaf to your schemes?
Once revered, now you scramble for profit.
Once obscure, now your temple rites are complete.
Self-promoting, self-hawking—only the gods are shamed.
I lay down my brush and sing, a song long familiar to men’s ears:

*I sing to warn you: do not follow deceit.*
*This text is written to silence slander.*

*Not a divine decree, but a verse like Nüwa’s severing the turtle’s legs—*
*When primordial chaos first parted from Mount Song and Tai.*
*The towering peaks of Heng and Hua—*
*Their gods are called Di and Zhi.*
*Shun piled wood for sacrifice, Yu made his inspections;*
*Qin performed the feng rites, Han offered their prayers.*
*Yet the transmission of the rightful succession remains unproven—*
*Who claims it was a water dragon or a wooden kui?*

*My origins in this world began in Xiaopi.*
*My spirit conceived at Caishi, nurtured by the marvels of Biefeng.*
*Alas, in the fleeting moment of soul’s destruction and spirit’s dissolution,*
*I was summoned from the dark void to gallop through the unseen.*
*Mourning the desolation of the sacred temple,*
*I gaze at the abandoned halls, their grandeur faded.*
*I long to cast off my cap and tear my robes,*
*To see the treacherous paths of the world.*
*The great edifice is collapsing, yet none tend to it—*
*I fear a single timber cannot prop it up.*
*In the year of* zhaoyang *and* dahuangluo*,*
*I sharpen my wits by Luochi.*
*Wielding the writing brush as my voice,*
*I invert truth to expose beauty and ugliness.*
*Some slander with cunning, clamoring loudly,*
*Claiming I deceive with flattery.*
*Flourishing their tongues like petals and leaves,*
*They prattle on like Qin’s courtiers.*
*You, silent and composed, why not stand firm in integrity?*
*Small as a gourd ladle or a midge,*
*How can you fathom the depths with a well’s narrow gaze?*
*Your bellies swollen with parasites,*
*You are no match for the wine-skin’s capacity.*
*The sprouts of* nie *valleys perish by dawn—*
*What use are the short-lived fox’s shadow-watching schemes?*
*Heaven and earth stretch boundless, without end;*
*Life and death surge vast—who can comprehend them?*
*Ruan Zhan’s debates on right and wrong—*
*His bones now crumble to dust.*
*If you say my words lack proof,*
*Then take the sorcerer’s new wheat as your omen.*
*It is said: Heaven’s Way blesses the good and punishes the wicked,*
*As surely as a shadow follows its form.*
*Those who scheme and feign madness,*
*Waver like foxes, full of doubt.*
*Revere the gods as if they stand beside you—*
*I remain silent. What more can I say?*

*Do Not Destroy the Stele*
To destroy the stele is to say it must not be destroyed.
The *ci* are the words that voice its transgressions.
These words say:

*How extreme the deceit of the age has become—*
*Cunning as rats, clever as macaques.*
*With gardenia faces and waxen tongues,*
*You mimic the whispers of your ilk.*
*Your clever paintings strive to surpass dogs and horses,*
*Yet only ghosts and demons occupy your designs.*
*You avoid the great paths to tread the fish and waterfowl’s way,*
*Yet know well the dangers of the road.*
*The ranks of the dark and numinous are dense—*
*They are not like trees, devoid of spirit.*
*Pity the firefly chasing the dark,*
*Calling the long night endless.*
*You beast-faced, speaking like men—*
*Are you not mere apes?*
*Can pythons truly swallow deer?*
*They only twist and shed their scales.*
*You cultivate bravery by shooting at ramie,*
*Yet Yi was ultimately trapped in his own body.*
*Defiling wealth, you suffer calamity—*
*Chong’s greed surpassed even his love for wife and child.*
*When evil accumulates, it harms the full,*
*Yet you cover it with praise and resent the record.*
*You carve your clan’s precious stone,*
*Listening to the wails of your ancestors.*
*The sun sets in a haze of yellow,*
*Suddenly scorching the parched east.*
*Behold the net of heaven, its cords spread wide—*
*Who can escape its three-sided snare?*
*Blowing on cold ashes to make soup,*
*You cling to the heat, refusing to wake.*
*No model to follow, you trace the steps of Han’s fools.*
*Stalking the tripod’s power in secret,*
*Why prattle on with your mouths?*
*Before the drum is buried, its sound is scorched by the sun.*
*Flames blaze—why heed women’s slander?*
*The loyal hearts of great generals burn bright—*
*Why repent of others’ schemes?*
*The public’s judgment has its merits and flaws—*
*It is not bias that favors Han.*
*Moved by the wheel’s hub, I reflect—*
*Knowing the Creator’s preferences.*

*(End of translation)*

The canonical punishments remain complete—
etched for ten thousand generations, never to be erased.
Yet the benighted, knowing nothing,
indulge in wanton malice and stubborn folly.
They burn the transmitted classics to drown the scriptures—
of Shao and Lu, which shall endure?
The white-stone ox cannot bear its trembling slaughter—
ah, what grievous wrong!
Some are swayed by fortune’s shifts,
dying at the gate in vain.
Some tread divergent paths in darkness,
yet face reckoning in warmth.
Is this age so unlike the past?
Deeds bear no karmic fruit.
Or do water and fire rail falsely?
I dare not whisper even to the cautious tortoise.
The black snail is no stranger to the distant Yao—
virtue flows unseen between them.
Thus Heaven blesses the good and curses the licentious,
enforcing the Dao’s decree.
Even when hurling a rat, one fears breaking the vessel—
why do they fail to ponder this?
To cover all eyes with a single finger—
who could thus deceive the heavens?
Heaven’s rafters are vast—
upon whom shall blame fall?
Do not deface the stele—
what excuse could I offer?

Alas! Such are these matters.

*End of Scroll Three of Xu Xian’s Literary Flourishes*
*Scroll Four of Xu Xian’s Literary Flourishes*
*Ode to the Joyful Rain*

At the Sacred Rain-Praying Ritual of the Venerable Lingji Temple,
we reverently invoked the Enlightened Arhat Bodhisattva,
Founder of Deer Park,
who struck his staff upon the holy waters,
offering devotion—
and swiftly received divine response.
In solemn record of this event,
I compose this *Ode to the Joyful Rain*,
inscribing it upon the Spring Pavilion,
to be passed down for generations,
awakening the resolve of those who seek refuge.

Thus have I heard.
The Master spoke in verse:
While all strain their eyes toward clouds and rainbows,
I, at this very hour, bring down the blessings of rain.
My vow extends boundlessly—
such is the heart of compassion, benefiting all beings.
Why point to wide-open eyes,
only to clutch at the Buddha’s feet in desperate times?
How could one fail to recall the fields of Mulberry Forest,
where prayers were offered to mountains and rivers?
Do mountains and rivers possess spirits?
When drought grows dire,
every stream and peak is cleansed,
no deity left uninvoked—
yet some still ask, *"What use is worshipping spirits?"*
Spirits are not spirits in themselves;
rather, the human heart, moved by sincerity, summons them.
The Master’s numinous power
derives from the Tathāgata’s divine might.
His precepts, virtue, and merit
are likewise beyond comprehension.
The villagers’ faith in him
has stood unshaken for ages.
Over five hundred years,
how many have been sheltered by his grace?

Since last autumn through winter, and now into the third month of spring, not a single drop of rain has fallen, and no seeds have been sown in the earth. Truly, this is a time of dire peril and urgent crisis—who would dare to slaughter geese and soften their blood in sacrifice? To place trust in snake-oil healers would only invite ridicule from those of discernment. Yet it was said: we had heard that within the Chan Master’s sleeve, indolent dragons could be summoned, and with a whip’s command, made to obey. Thus, we came to pay homage and beseech our Master. Following his chariot, rain fell on the day after the *renyin* day, a gentle drizzle spreading in all directions. By the *bingwu* day, the rains had come in full. Young and old alike leapt for joy, their delight mingling in celebration. They declared: *"The Perfected One has opened the mountain rains!"* And so, they offered incense and chanted praises.

**Record of the Eastern Well’s Restoration**
The Eastern Well’s origins are recorded in stone inscriptions, which state: *"In the eighth year of the Xining reign, the year *yimao*, on the thirteenth day of the intercalary eighth month, *yisi*,"*—two hundred and twenty-four years have passed since then. Before the Xining era, when the well’s bricks were first laid, its masonry and railings still stood, bearing the inscription: *"Zhang Jue donates the Eastern Well’s railing"* in six characters. Jue was a man of virtuous renown during the Five Dynasties period. By reckoning, this was the eighth year of the Tianfu reign of the Later Jin, the first year of Tiande in Min—spanning, in total, three hundred and sixty-seven years. For all things, long duration leads to decay: such is the nature of fate. Yet decay may be repaired: this, too, is fate’s design.

The well had silted and collapsed for over forty years, and the simple folk of the village, though not negligent, knew not how to address it. Below, the pit yawned like a bottomless abyss; above, the crumbling edge hung like a sheer cliff, on the verge of breaking yet still clinging—so treacherous that no hand could find purchase. Then, on the second day before the Dragon Boat Festival in the first year of the Dade reign, *wushen*, as the sun neared its setting, a sudden wind and rain arose, and in an instant, the site was reduced to a wasteland of mud and shattered stone. Heaven seemed to lend its hand, compelling men to restore it—truly, this was fate’s decree.

Where there is land, there are people. And the people belong to Heaven. Blessed by Heaven’s fortune, in harmony with Heaven’s timing, and aligned with Heaven’s will, they consulted the omens and found them auspicious. Four days later, on the *yichou* day, laborers were gathered. With no other means, they hauled stones and cleared mud. To the north, on the sunny slope of Bie Peak, grew a vine as thick as seven or eight inches in diameter, its length straight and uncoiled, stretching over seven or eight *zhang*. They used it as a rope—what marvelous craftsmanship of creation! Could this not be Heaven’s aid to its people?

By the seventh day of the month, *jiawu*, stonemasons and earthworkers had assembled, and by *yisi*, the work was complete. Does this not mirror the *yisi* day of the well’s original founding? Or was it Heaven’s doing? It was Heaven’s.

Here follows the translation:

---

Dare I record these events in writing, to show the people and invoke the spirits? The words read:

Heaven aids its people,
Wind and rain respond in kind.
Prophecies align with spirits,
And thus the work began.
Once dredged, it draws from the depths,
Its hidden flow reaching the Milky Way.
Nurturing people and aiding all things,
Its merit and use are vast.
The eight spirits of the eight directions,
The royal kin and the mighty sword.
With martial clamor and military might,
They ward off misfortune and banish ill omens.
People receive its blessings,
Years yield abundant grain.
Drinking from the spring, they know its pulse,
Enveloped in Heaven’s grace.
Restoring the old and renewing the well,
The task is thus accomplished.
May peace and tranquility endure forever—
*Qian*: Origin, prosperity, harmony, and constancy.

**Sealing the Well: An Inscription**
The meaning of the well is profound indeed.
In the heavens, it belongs to the twenty-eight constellations;
In the Five Phases, it is the essence of water;
For people, it serves the needs of drink and sustenance, indispensable even for a single day.
The well cannot be abandoned because its waters nurture people and aid all things.
Yet if it becomes silted and unclean, old and murky, it becomes a forsaken well.
Thus, the sages, in composing the hexagrams, declared:
*A well silted is undrinkable; an old well attracts no birds.*
This is precisely because an old, forsaken well’s mud is unfit to drink.
As for birds and beasts not coming—it cannot nurture people or aid all things, but instead harms them.

To the east of the lane lies the temple grounds, where the mountain rises from Bie Peak, winding and undulating, its rises and falls uncounted, until it settles here. Long ago, someone quarried the mountain to dig a well, excavating until the spring was reached, nearly nine *ren* deep. Yet they feared abandoning the work, for their heart was set on nurturing people and aiding all things—even if it meant wounding the mountain, no harm was done.

But now, one enters the well yet cannot draw water. The well’s purpose is lost.
The thirsty cannot drink,
The soiled cannot cleanse.
The frogs in its depths fall silent,
The fish in its dried ruts nearly perish.
Left unused, it has lain thus for years.
The diviners call it the *Black Warrior pierced by an arrow*,
The physicians, a chronic ailment of the vital organs.
The clamor of opinions is great—this mountain has suffered no small wound.

Since then, customs have changed, and the village has withered.
Men of great talent, now impoverished gentlemen, bear no radiant glory.
The withering, the decay, the drowning in this—what could be worse?
Whom to blame? The fault lies with the well.

In the ninth month of the *gui-si* year of the Zhiyuan era, on the auspicious *bing-zi* day, the villagers proposed sealing the well. They prayed to me, and I answered: "Sealing it is auspicious." Thus, I compose this text to inform the well’s spirit:

Long ago, Geng Gong dug a well fifteen *zhang* deep. He straightened his robes, bowed, and the well gushed forth with water. Yet when Prefectural Clerk Zhu Bo took up residence in an office with over a hundred chambers, all the wells ran dry. Both were wells—why did one benefit Geng Gong but not Zhu Bo? Was this partiality?

May the well’s spirit lower its head and heed this command, replying: "It is not partiality, but fate.
That there was water was fate;
That there was none was also fate.
To open it when there was water, to seal it when there was none—this is proper.
What concern is it to the spirits?"

Yet where fate lies, human effort is tied.
Thus, the proposal to seal it arose.
Seal it, I say:
On this day of sealing, spirits will guard it,
Warding off misfortune and banishing ill omens.
After sealing, may households prosper and suffice,
May people live long and in health.

**On the Difficulty of Temple Restoration**
The responsibility of restoring the temple is arduous indeed—I have heard as much.
Year-round labor, endless expenses—
From dawn to dusk, no respite,
Is it not difficult?
Exhausting ears and eyes, straining strength and thought—
Is it not hard?

The rotted and blackened beams and buckets have now been repaired.
The broken and missing roof tiles have been restored.
The faded red and white hues have been renewed.
Whose work is this? To call it solely one’s own would be excessive;
Yet one might shirk responsibility and evade the difficulty.

Nevertheless, the eastern pond’s inscription has dried up,
The western steps’ stele remains unerected,
The furnishings of the inner and outer halls are unworthy—
What is to be done?
It is like setting out on a thousand-*li* journey, only to turn back at the gate—
This is not the conduct of a true man.
A true man acts with righteousness as his weight,
To see righteousness and not act is cowardice.

Of old, the two sons of the Lord of Gudu stood alone in their conduct, heedless of praise or blame—
Were they acting for gain?
My sense of righteousness arises from innate nature,
As distant from those who seek fame and profit as heaven is from earth.
People far and wide have heard and know this—
Even without consulting spirits, there is no doubt,
So why must I speak so earnestly?

The *Book of Songs* says:
*Though alone in your chamber, be not ashamed before the rafters.*
That which moves the spirits must be fitting.
When the halls are restored, the walls erected, and all things prepared,
Then offer sacrifices and present them,
And reflect upon them—
Reverence will arise naturally.
Otherwise, if one’s demeanor is even momentarily irreverent,
A careless heart will deceive the spirits.
Not only the spirits, but people too.
Not only deceiving others, but deceiving oneself—
What grandeur or solemnity can there be?

Those who later tend to the temple,
Walking these halls and reading this record,
Will surely ask:
Who were the eight who restored the temple?
Who first proposed it?
Who joined in the planning?
At first so diligent, in the end so negligent—
They too are people; what was their intent?

All things under heaven begin well, but few endure to the end.
To excel in both beginning and end—
This is the fulfillment of the human way.

Difficult indeed is this—it is why I hasten so earnestly, seeking to ensure a strong beginning and a successful end.

To your question, I say: today’s scholars cannot proceed without wealth. Heaven does not send rain, spirits do not deliver offerings, the practice of alms-seeking yields nothing, and those willing to give generously are rarely seen. What plan, then, can be devised?

I counsel you thus: when the multitude acts in accord, it must always treat righteousness as its profit. If profit is not taken as righteousness, no one will truly value righteousness, and the endeavor will not endure. Remember well this word: *righteousness*.

I imagine those who joined this work rising abruptly, leaning against the wall, and declaring: “How dare we not accept this?”

**Record of Completion**

Affairs under heaven are not difficult at the outset—only in their conclusion. As the *Book of Songs* says: “All things have a beginning, but few endure to the end.” The *Book of Changes* states: “The fox crossing the river wets its tail”—speaking of how easy the start is, and how hard the finish.

Thus the noble person reveres the beginning and is cautious of the end, maintaining consistency from start to finish. This is called great good fortune. But if one is diligent at first and negligent at last, willingly yielding to the pressure of petty men—such are the schemes of those who achieve nothing. What use are they?

Take, for example, the construction of this temple. At the beginning, there were eight of us. By the end, only seven remained. Of these, only three devoted themselves wholeheartedly. From this, we see clearly: affairs under heaven are not difficult at the start—only in their completion. This is indeed true.

Yet though the difficulty of completion may neither add to nor diminish my own standing, discerning observers will perceive in others the lack of resolve to see things through. How, then, can one hope to cultivate this as a field of blessing and seek its benefits?

Now, six or seven of us have gathered in the courtyard to perform the rites of completion. Our merit lies in the altars of the state. We carve our inscription into metal and stone, that for a thousand generations to come, none may dare to neglect or forget.

As for the offerings—fine livestock, fragrant wine—our rites exceed what is proper, verging on excess. This is truly a shame for me. As the *Rites* in the *Tan Gong* says: “In sacrifice, if reverence is lacking but ritual is abundant, it is better to have ritual lacking and reverence abundant.” Here, both ritual and reverence are fully present. Why, then, does this record of completion seem to reproach me for insufficiency?

The numinous power of the spirits is indeed potent—why, then, do they not bestow a little more of it upon people? Such words are narrow indeed.

Wealth and honor are what all men desire; when desire is unfulfilled, there must be a seeking after it. Now you six or seven men, joining your strength for the royal house, have shown yourselves able to begin well and end well. I know full well that you harbor ambitions of great achievement. Heaven’s Dao and the numinous spirits bless the good. The mountains echo and the valleys respond, answering every sound—why, then, do brush and tongue labor so earnestly to speak for me?

Have you not seen the portraits of meritorious ministers in the Lingyan Pavilion? Twenty-two men in all, yet those whose loyalty endured from beginning to end were but seven: Wei Zheng, Xiao Yu, Duan Zhixuan, Liu Honggong, Yao Silian, Yu Shinan, and Qin Shubao. Now you seven, if you can hold fast to this resolve to the end, may one day rise to the halls of state and take the affairs of the nation as your own responsibility. Why fear you will not match the deeds of those seven in the Lingyan Pavilion? How could your merit be confined to the mere repair of a temple?

I dare offer this as encouragement and append it to the record of completion.

---

**Exorcism of the Pestilence Ghost**

The *Annals* say: The Marquis of Jin fell ill and dreamed of a bear, which he took to be a pestilence ghost. Later, Han Tuizhi, Liu Yuxi, and others—men renowned for their wisdom and insight, universally admired—also spoke of "malaria ghosts." Some composed texts to drive them away, others wrote poems to dispel them. Only then did people begin to believe that autumn fevers and winter plagues were truly called "ghosts."

Yet ghosts are formless and soundless—how could they sicken men? Moreover, man is the spirit of the myriad things. Heaven covers him, earth bears him, sun and moon illuminate him, wind and rain, frost and dew nurture him through the four seasons. Grain, millet, mulberry, and hemp clothe and feed him. I see how deeply the workings of creation attend to man. If the human Dao is not established, then what we call heaven and earth, sun and moon, wind and rain, frost and dew—I know not what they truly are, save that we name them so. Alas, they would be but lifeless clods!

Since creation has brought forth man in this world, how could it permit such ghosts to afflict the people? It is not the ghost—nor is the ghost the sickness. Rather, it is intemperance in food and drink, indulgence in sensual pleasures, the scorching of the bowels by sweet, sour, bitter, and pungent flavors, the rotting of the stomach. Joy, anger, sorrow, and delight disorder the nature and dissolve the spirit. The body is not wood or stone; assailed by so much, how could it not fall ill?

Yet in ancient times, when men fell ill, they were treated with decoctions and acupuncture, and none failed to recover and live. Now, when men fall ill, neither decoctions nor acupuncture can reach them. Alas, truly are these ghosts obstinate and unyielding to transformation!

I, by the mandate of the Supreme Emperor, guard this land and govern its people. If any bring poison and harm to my people, I shall consult the *Laws of the Mysterious Code of the Female Celestial* and decree that they be beheaded a thousand thousand times, their forms severed ten thousand ten thousand times, until all are reduced to hares scurrying into their burrows, with none left as fish slipping through the net. Only then may my people’s desires be fulfilled.

Now, as the Lower Prime announces a general amnesty, I shall recite a verse by Master Du and proclaim it thus:

*You, with skulls blood-smeared and grim,*
*I seize and hurl back to Magistrate Cui!*
*Ghost, if you have any power, flee—flee—flee!*
*Linger but a moment, and the Heavenly Axe falls!*

Written on the *jiazi* day of the second lunar month in spring, the eighth year of the *Kaibao* reign of the Song (Year of *Yihai*), by the Old Man of Biefeng.

---

**Exorcism of the Drought Demon**

You, whose name is drought, are called *Chi*. I know your ancestor was the Flame Clan, who ruled the world by the virtue of fire and rendered great service to the people, earning the revered title of Flame Emperor. Your lineage was once illustrious, yet how degenerate you have become, so base and unworthy! Your form is hideous, your nature perverse. Even the poison of *Bifang* and the calamity of *Huilu* are not as severe as you.

When the Xia sovereigns cast images upon their tripods, you were so terrified that you led your kind to flee to the lands of the tattooed and black-toothed peoples, where you have dwelled for many years. Once, you vied for dominance with the *Kuafu* clan, reversing the sun’s shadow to chase them between the valleys of *Cigu*, but parched and weary, you sought to drink the waters of *Mingbo* and the great marshes, only to fail. Thus, you stole life by surreptitiously drinking from the rivers of the human world, nearly draining the Luo and the Yellow in a single draught. This led to the *Song of Mulberry Grove* reaching the heavens, whereupon your crime was judged, and you were nearly exterminated.

King Xuan of Zhou and Duke Jing of Qi treated you with benevolence, sparing your punishment. Since then, your remnant seed has spread and multiplied to this day.

I, by the mandate of the Supreme Emperor, guard this land and govern its people. The people toil year-round, for food is their heaven. The heart of Heaven cherishes my people—how could it not transform, how could it not send rain? Who is to blame if not you? The grain is about to ear, the millet to ripen—do you not consider this, yet bring such ruin upon them?

Though you bear the form of man and the nature of things, examine your heart—can you truly bear this? The straw chariot is prepared, the provisions are ready. I pour you a cup of clear water and solemnly declare: The lands of the tattooed and black-toothed peoples, your ancient homeland, lie south of the sea. You may reach them by evening if you depart at dawn.

Now I make this pact with you: By the end of this day, gather your companions, take your kin, ride the dust and spread the wind, and set forth at once. If you do not heed these words, proving yourself obstinate and insensate, I shall invoke the *Laws of the Mysterious Code of the Female Celestial*. Any who harm the people and creatures of the land—all shall say they deserve death, and they shall be killed. Heaven has its records and its net; punishment will reach you, and there shall be no regret.

Thus, this proclamation.

**Exorcism Text to Drive Away Toad Miasma**

Locusts shall not cross our borders; tigers shall flee with their cubs in tow—this is the sacred duty of those who guard the land, the righteous act of ridding the people of harm. Now, this miasma is called the "Toad." Here, I compose this text to banish it.

I once read in the *History of the Southern Dynasties* of a man named Qiu Jie, who fell ill after eating raw vegetables. Years later, he dreamed his mother told him: "This is the poison of the toad. I leave you three pills—take them." He did, and voided several *dou* of toad spawn. Thus, the harm wrought by toads is indeed true.

Of the creatures born of water, there are three hundred and sixty kinds. Among them, the toad is the most despised in Heaven and Earth. Its form is grotesque, its limbs scrabbling in the sand. Its belly swells, its throat constricts. Its color blends with mud, its diet is filth and dung. It leaps upon well-rails, rests in crumbling cliffs. Its dwelling is foul, its appearance vile. Day and night, it croaks, enslaved to demons.

"Heaven has bestowed virtue upon me—what place has the toad?" Yet some say: The toad’s essence is a demonic thing. When the moon is full, it puffs its gut, bares its gaping maw, and raves in triumph. In the green-black void, it capers, devouring the essence of the Great Yin, disrupting Heaven’s order, ravaging Heaven’s creations. Such a witless beast—if even Heaven cannot endure it, how much less can man?

Now the people—what crime have they committed to suffer such afflictions? The malady begins at the tip of a hair, festers in the joints and bones. At first, it brings stupor and blockage, dizziness and confusion. As it advances, the body twists, the sinews weaken, the waist grows heavy, the head spins. Nasal mucus flows like rain, the throat swells with phlegm, spittle and vomit choke the chest. Within, parched; without, desiccated—thirst without thirst—until searing heat erupts. Folk remedies and coal fumes, the fires of disaster, leave scorched heads and blistered brows, as though seated in a pit of flames. Even were one to ride the giant spirit, seize the Dipper’s ladle, and scoop the waters of the Bohai Sea to drench the flames, it would not suffice for relief. Some, born under the signs of *chen* and *xu*, sweat until the fever breaks. Others, once the heat subsides, are left with a dry cough that lingers for days, worsening without respite.

I trace the source—whose fault is this? Is it not the autumn acting as summer, the imbalance of yin and yang? Or the five flavors indulged without restraint, the excesses of food and drink? Or the eyes fixed on dark and bright, the ears enslaved to lewd songs, the unchecked pursuit of sound and sensuality? What physician cannot cure it with medicine? What moxibustionist cannot apply the mugwort stove? The curses of the exorcist crackle like thunder, the talismans of the talisman-master slash like blades—yet the heart labors in vain, the eyes grow weary, and all efforts yield no result, their effects delayed. The people’s grievances converge upon one culprit: the toad.

I, entrusted by the Supreme Emperor to govern this land and care for its people, find the toad still defiantly alive. It fills ditches as ghosts, becomes a demon-chief, a *chimei*, a *wangliang*, lurking in the dark, crouching in shadows. Treacherous and perverse, it stirs cruelty and oppression, devoid of mercy. The shadow-shooting of the short fox, the venom of the grass-biting centipede—none compare to the harm you wreak. Your crimes tower to the heavens; the people all cry for your death.

Now I make this pact with the toads: Gather your kind within a single day and relocate together to the east of Lake Wanshan, where you may eke out a living, a borrowed existence—so long as you no longer harm my people. If not—if you remain stubborn and senseless, heedless of this warning—I shall commission the *Guo* clan to burn your dens and drown you in ash-laced wine. Then I will sever your heads, split your bellies, mince your flesh, and share it with the people to feast upon. Your kind must be eradicated to satisfy the people’s desires.

Hearing these words, the toads trembled in fear. Donning green robes, they knelt in the courtyard, begging for their lives: "The willow-wheeled carts are ready, the red grass packed. We willingly submit and depart today. Spare us, O King, and pardon our crimes."

Thereupon, I composed this decree to hasten their departure:

*"Clear and upright, I embody the divine.
By Heaven’s mandate, I cherish the people below.
O wretched toads, creatures of malevolence,
Rejoicing in calamity, why so cruel?
Defying Heaven’s decree, you disrupt the seasons.
The people’s qi stagnates, their paths grow treacherous.
Even Lu and Bian despair, their skills in vain.
The shamans chant and chew, their arts shrouded in darkness.
What crime have the people committed to endure such suffering?
O wretched toads, your sins overflow.
The people’s wrath gathers—your name is cursed.
They long to gnaw your bones, to mince your flesh.
My fury blazes like thunder and lightning.
I entrust the Guo clan to execute justice.
Kneeling in the courtyard, you plead for mercy—
A single cry for life, a reprieve from ten thousand deaths.
O wretched toads, if you truly possess spirit,
Hear my words and reform.
The towering peaks of Wanshan, the limpid waters of the lake—
There lies your refuge, your haven of peace.
Go deep, hide far, vanish from sight.
Depart swiftly, swiftly—hasten as the law commands!"*

Composed in the third month of the *dingwei* day, spring of the eighth year of *Kaibao* in the Song dynasty (975).

---

**Ode on the Exorcism of Toads**

The people of Yue revere omens and believe in slaughter—an ancient custom, unshaken even now. When illness strikes, they grieve and fret. Yet the shamans and mediums warn: "Without *ginseng* and *poria*, the cure demands blood." Some slander and deceive, currying favor with the crowd. Thus, I compose this *Ode on the Exorcism of Toads* to justify my actions. Its words read:

*"Alas, the world turned upside down—
The square wears the cap of the round.
Yin congeals into sores, the springs surge and flow.
Nightly, the blood-dripping specter roams the perilous forests.
The archer-demon lurks, listening for shadows to strike.
How can the pebble of Yan be mistaken for pearls?
The quack’s concoctions muddle yellow millet—who can tell true from false?
They accept tiles as gold, their thunderous clamor drowning the music of the *Xianchi* Pool.
They praise the beauty of the *Mo* mother, while the idle *Zou* son grows profligate and shameless.
A wild bird enters the house—what omen does it bring?
The people lament and mourn.
The world craves the dregs of wine and pickled fare,
Yet I would feast on chrysanthemums and weave orchids.
The customs of the age oppress—
Moths drawn to the flame, ants to the lamp.
The six excesses arise from imbalance—
Why blame frost and dew?
The cases of Shen and Tai Luo prove it—
Are these not strange and unnatural?
They brandish spears and arrows like divining stalks,
Their words filthy, their claims absurd.
The dragon-horse reveals its form—
Later, Fu Xi deciphered its celestial patterns.
To kill in pursuit of life—
Even the southern wind cannot reverse it.
The bandits rage, drunk with power—
They deem all wood sacred.
The *Kui* burns for its recklessness—
The land suffers ever-greater drought.
Better to swallow charcoal and wail,
Or face the wall and meditate.
What crime have the fish and *zong* committed that the skies darken,
Their qi tangled in resentment?
They borrow the *Huiping* from the postmaster,
Bewildered by the snake in the cup.
They usurp creation’s harmony, tuning the pipes,
Hoping to thaw the ice and revive spring.
The mother clings to her child as the greatest concern,
Yet they cling to darkness, lost in ignorance."*

To call *changyang* useless for prolonging life—
Then why seek *fuling*?
The net’s mesh tightens, the body ensnared—
Alas, how can the valley’s whale endure?
Thus one drifts through the world’s currents,
Yet remains deluded by omens, never awakening.
Moving the firewood earns no virtue—
The guest’s brow is scorched, his face seared.
Who is to blame?
The base man’s gluttony, his schemes spun in cunning words.
The ringleaders of sin—
Men indict them, ghosts demand their due.
Borrowing metal and iron to slaughter,
They cast down order and plunge into chaos.
Calling wrong right, right wrong—
Do they not invert all things?
Man’s triumph over heaven’s decree cannot deceive—
Light endures, darkness fades.
Vying in their mad rush,
The tumors burst in ruin.
Truly lamentable—
Who would don a cap and shake their robes now?
Beat the drum to rouse their stupor, illuminate their blindness—
Yet gloom lingers, unyielding.
Better to refuse all words—
Without me, all would perish.
Hair unbound, stammering—

*Xu Xian’s Literary Flourishes, Scroll Four* ends
*Xu Xian’s Literary Flourishes, Scroll Five* begins

**Eulogy of the Perfected One Ascending to Court**

Ascending to the imperial throne, advancing through the celestial steps,
Radiance clings to sun and moon, office holds the Three Terraces.
Constellations array in guard, the Silver River shines in revolution.
The eastern dragon’s horns rise—alone he claims the Dipper’s helm.

**Eulogy of the Perfected One Returning to His Palace**

Canopy of stars, steeds of the celestial stable—
Left and right, the Feathered Forest generals escort with solemn clarity.
Memorials flow in succession, pitch and tone harmonize in voice.
Returning in glory to the heavenly court, a journey of ten thousand leagues through clouds.

**Eulogy of the Office of Blessing Bestowal**

The house that accumulates goodness knows peace and well-being.
Behold, O Star of Fortune—vast and boundless in your exorcism.
Grant long life and wealth, grant flourishing and prosperity.
Descendants by the thousands and tens of thousands—endless your grace upon us.

**Eulogy of the Office of Registration**

Turtle Peak’s noble sparseness, Dragon Head’s lofty bearing—
Behold, O Star of Prosperity—brilliant and radiant your light.
Gazing upon your many scholars, resplendent their compositions.
Shoulder to shoulder, step by step—they enter golden gates and jade halls.

**Eulogy of Marshal Zha, Eternal Guardian of Yong’an Prefecture**

Behold, Marshal Zha—his qi firm and upright.
He mends armor and drills troops, levies taxes to ensure ample provisions.
Swift as a spirit in strategy, in every battle he prevails.
Pacifying ten thousand leagues, his duty lies in aiding the state.

**Eulogy of Marshal Bian, Eternal Guardian of Wu’an Prefecture**

Behold, Marshal Bian—the reincarnation of a divine destiny.
From youth, keen and refined, his nature born of heaven’s truth.
He calculates victory before battle, his campaigns always just.
With campaigns but no combat, his duty lies in pacifying the people.

**Eulogy of Marshal Wei, Left Guardian of the Gate and Military Commissioner of Weibo**

Behold, Marshal Wei—burdened with the frontier’s weight.
Loyalty not in force, benevolence not in might.
Eight battles, eight victories; seven captures, seven releases.
When employed, none can oppose—his host need not be vast.

**Eulogy of Marshal Zhao, Right Guardian of the Gate and Commander of the Allied Forces**

Behold, Marshal Zhao—his commands uphold discipline.
Rewards and punishments certain, outward refinement and inner order.
Within, he guards the Tiger Pass; without, he wields the Leopard Tail.
