Plotting within the tent, he decides victory a thousand leagues away.

**Eulogy of Marshal Diao, Vanguard Commander of the Allied Forces**

Behold, Marshal Diao—his campaigns always just.
A million fierce warriors, encamped beyond the Min.
Striking where least expected, silent as the unseen.
Terrible in wrath—his host is called the Divine Army.

**Eulogy of the Thunder Division**

Lightning flashes, thunder roars.
Shocking might across ten thousand leagues, the Dragon Head’s towering might.
Called to bring timely rain, to comfort our mortal realm.
Wind and clouds in auspicious union—enduring peace for a thousand years.

Lightning darts, thunder surges, clouds soar, winds rise.
Sweet rains fall in season—the farmer’s joy.
Fields are tilled, furrows sown—millet and sorghum grow lush.
Granaries filled to bursting—countless measures of grain.

**Eulogy of the Assembly of Worthies**

Flying blades and leaping thrusts, crossbows drawn and bows bent.
Flutes and zithers, ivory clappers, hawks in harness, orioles in cages.
Trays borne on immortal palms, blossoms plucked by heaven’s craft.
Golden Ball’s noble sons, drunk in the spring breeze.

**Eulogy of the Palace Maidens Offering Longevity**

Six palaces’ splendor, the moon’s Lady Chang’e.
Golden hairpins and pearled slippers, rouge and emerald maidens.
Gentle their swaying waists, soft their singing voices.
Jade cups offer longevity, Queen Mother’s immortal peaches.

**Banquet of Auspicious Longevity: Record of the Carnivorous Kitchen**

First course, first cup: phoenix breast and dragon embryo, great silver-threaded vermicelli soup.
Second course: Queen Mother offers longevity, immortal melodies and ritual music—fitting only for the imperial retinue.

**Banquet of Immortal Assembly: Record of the Vegetarian Kitchen**

First course, first cup: celestial incense and seven treasures rare, jade-strip vermicelli soup.
Second course: the host of immortals offers longevity, cloud-borne music—fitting only for Cao Wenji.

Eight precious delicacies, five ritual vessels, six vegetarian jade dishes.

**Eulogy of the Colored-Play Children**

In times of peace, ten rains and five winds.
Farmers rejoice, celebrating the year’s abundance.
Pipes trill, drums thunder.
Hands dance, feet stamp—the joy of children.

**Eulogy of the Music Bureau of the Board of Rites**

The royal chef oversees the feast, employing the eight rarities.
The grand master oversees music, pitch and tone in harmony.
Music accompanies the meal, the meal discerns the flavors.
Drunk and sated, they sing of great peace.

**Eulogy of Immortal Music**

Jade and chalcedony palaces, musk and eaglewood fragrance drifts.
Lady Yang performs the melody, Lady Dong plays the flute.
Little Man’s waist dances, Yue’s maidens sing.
Golden cups offer longevity—a full ensemble of immortal music.

**Eulogy of the Immortal Kitchen**

Eaglewood and sparrow’s tongue—fragrant and pure their savor.
Fortune fruits and immortal peaches—arrayed on golden platters.
If offering sweet dew, you provide jade dust.
If brewing sweet wine, you are the sacred grain.

**Eulogy for Those Seeking Longevity at the Sacrificial Offering**

**Eulogy of You Song, Courtesy Name Shenlifu**

Timber for towering halls, bearing a soaring mien.
Virtuous the gentleman—how he resembles these!

*Again:*
Frosted boughs and snow-laden branches, naturally embracing.
Painted in red and green—people say it is I.

**Eulogy of You Mei, Courtesy Name Muyuan**

Bones refined and strange, spirit rich and radiant.
Born of creation’s work—what need for brushstrokes?

**Eulogy of You Zhu, Courtesy Name Muren**

Pure in heart, upright in body.
Upright and pure—the gentleman is he.

*Again:*
Towering and grand, how strange your form!
Yet holding fast to integrity—no painting could capture it.

**Eulogy of You Ju, Courtesy Name Huabo**

Fragrant his virtue, elegant his talent.
The gentleman alone possesses what he possesses.

*Again:*
Unbending and noble, his manner distinct from his form.
Dew glistens, frost descends—yet his resolve remains unchanged.

**Eulogy of You Lan, Courtesy Name Senglv**

Upon the thousand-fathom cliff, once he displayed his power.
Now entering the chamber—all four elements are void.

**Record of the East Side of the Hall of Swallowing Joy, Office of Registration**

By gracious decree, the descendants of dukes and marquises shall restore their ancestors’ glory.
Upon verification, this person is indeed of an illustrious lineage and noble virtue.
Thus, his descendants shall be granted abundance, each attaining rank and eminence, to bequeath to posterity.

**Record of the West Side of the Hall of Swallowing Joy, Office of Registration**

By gracious decree, the descendants of families that accumulate goodness shall surely prosper.
Upon verification, this person is indeed temperate, reverent, yielding, and sincere.
Thus, he shall be granted descendants by the thousands and tens of thousands, his lineage enduring for a hundred generations, to manifest his goodness and make known his hidden virtue.

**Granting of Descendants in Perpetuity, to Bestow Boundless Blessings**
*From the* Han History.

**Office of Blessing Registration, Temple of Numinous Aid**

By reverent decree of the Three Heavens’ Gate, it is commanded: To all the common people, We bestow blessings upon you.
Those who receive this blessing receive the blessing of Heaven.
Heaven, in its hidden efficacy, watches over the people, first harmonizing their dwellings—how dare I overstep its will?
Proclaim to the lords of our allied states, the governors, the many officers, and the stewards: Hearken to Our words.
What the people desire, let it be your concern—make them flourish and prosper, prosper and grow great, daily richer, daily longer-lived, daily in peace and well-being.
Let them have enough to honor their fathers and mothers above, and to provide for their wives and children below.
May a hundred blessings come to them in full, that the people may find them fitting.
You shall thus fulfill your duties. Let this office, in accordance with the decree, respectfully heed and faithfully implement it.

This office, reverently receiving the imperial edict, hereby promulgates the following conditions for registering blessings upon families that accumulate goodness:

1. Bestowed with longevity.
2. Govern the people with nobility.
3. Clothed and fed in abundance.
4. Household affairs peaceful and prosperous.
5. Both parents blessed with joy.
6. Husband and wife in harmonious respect.
7. Descendants numerous.
8. Farming and sericulture amply stored.
9. Fruits and vegetables flourishing.
10. Herds of sheep thriving.
11. All craftsmen skilled and adept.
12. Merchants and travelers secure in their trade.

**Office of Emolument Registration, Temple of Numinous Aid**

By reverent decree of the Three Heavens’ Gate, it is commanded: Virtue is honored with rank, merit rewarded with emolument.
Those who receive this rank and emolument receive what Heaven possesses.
Now that Heaven thus ordains, how dare the people not follow?
Among the people, the outstanding are called *shi* (scholars).
Among scholars, the virtuous are called *junzi* (noble persons).
Thus Mencius said: Cultivate your Heaven-bestowed nobility, and human rank will follow.
Confucius said: Speak with few faults, act with few regrets, and emolument lies therein.
This is what is meant by the noble person’s service—acting in accordance with rightness.
1. Rank to govern their nobility.
2. Emolument to govern their wealth.
Though this be the prerogative of the Grand Steward, it is your duty, O sovereigns.

Proclaim to the lords of our allied states, the governors, the many officers, and the stewards: Hearken to Our ancient charge.
We have heard that when good persons do good, their descendants will surely have those who rise to continue it.
From this day forward, for families that accumulate goodness, you shall register their rank and emolument.

Let this office, in accordance with the decree, respectfully heed and faithfully implement it.

This office, reverently receiving the imperial edict, hereby promulgates the following conditions for registering emolument upon families that accumulate goodness:

1. Descendants wise and bright.
2. Persons of talent, outstanding and majestic.
3. Emolument received from Heaven.
4. Preserved blessings and extended years.
5. Household way flourishing and harmonious.
6. Good spouses gentle and mild.
7. Generations with many sons.
8. Official careers smooth and successful.
9. Carriages, horses, and retinues.
10. Caps and crowns resplendent.
11. Emolument exalted and rank illustrious.
12. Perpetual inheritance of prosperity and flourishing.

*End of Scroll Five of Xu Xian’s Literary Flourishes*
*Scroll Six of Xu Xian’s Literary Flourishes*

**Preface on Friendship**

Those who share the same path are called *peng* (companions); those who share the same aspirations are called *you* (friends). The meaning of friendship lies in neither flattery nor deceit, neither extravagance nor indulgence. It must first be realized in the heart before it can be called true kinship. If companionship exists without sincerity, it is mere superficial association. If friendship exists without sincerity, it is but a face-value friendship. The noble person does not partake in such.

Now, you have sought from me inscriptions for paintings, and I have named them after the *Five Friends*: Friend Pine, Friend Bamboo, Friend Plum—these are the *Three Friends of Winter*. Friend Orchid—this is what Confucius meant when he said, "Dwelling with a good person is like entering a room of orchids and iris; over time, one no longer detects the fragrance, for one has become one with it." Friend Chrysanthemum—truly, does not the chrysanthemum embody the Dao of the noble person with its noble fragrance? The meaning of these *Five Friends*—can it not be trusted?

In painting, nothing is more difficult than capturing the true likeness. Yet capturing the likeness is not the greatest challenge—capturing the form is harder still. But even capturing the form is not the hardest—capturing the heart is. Human hearts differ as widely as their faces. The noble and the petty may appear similar in countenance, but their hearts are not the same. If one abandons the heart and seeks only the outward form, one reduces the noble to the petty. Though this painting exists, who does not possess such a painting? Now that you have named these *Five Friends*, you must take the heart as your enduring companion. Thus it is said: *To judge by form is not as good as to judge by heart.*

---

**A Record of the Painter**

I once read Liu Zihou’s *Biography of the Carpenter* and Han Tuizhi’s *Biography of the Decayed Wang Chengfu*, and was deeply moved. The purpose of a biography is to transmit a name. Reflecting on those times, carpenters and decayed men were as common as flowing water—why were only Yang Qian and Wang Chengfu remembered? As the Grand Historian once told Ren An: "In ancient times, those who were wealthy and noble yet whose names were erased are beyond counting." These two men gained their fame through the biographies written by those two masters—how fortunate they were!

Now, there is a man who makes himself known through painting. I fear his name may not be transmitted to posterity, so I dare to follow the example of the ancients and compose this record for him:

In Jishan Village of Min, there has stood the Temple of the Divine Aid King for many years. Its painted decorations had long faded into obscurity, its walls crumbling, nearly becoming a den for spring worms and autumn snakes. In the *guisi* year of the Zhiyuan era, the villagers restored it, wishing to repaint and renew it. Soon, those who licked brushes and ground ink arrived in droves, but no decision was made on whom to employ. They consulted me, but I refused their request. The villagers were astonished, finding it strange—was there some master who had stripped off his robes and sat cross-legged, yet had not yet arrived?

One day, a painter suddenly appeared, moved by a dream. Only then did they believe my words were true. Observing the man, he was steadfast and upright, refined and untrammeled by俗, dressed in the robes of a scholar. He did not speak his name, nor did anyone know his origins. That day, when gentlemen gathered to discuss integrity, he sighed and said: "The greedy follow profit, the heroic follow fame. I have come for fame, not for profit. My name has long been known in other lands, but this region has not yet heard of it. Let me try—if I succeed, so be it." The temple’s protective walls bore old, incomplete paintings, so he was ordered to complete them with his brush. He rose eagerly, though a trace of reluctance remained—his skill had yet to be proven. The onlookers clapped their hands and laughed, saying: "He has a hollow reputation! This is nothing but copying gourds or tracing horses from diagrams—what is there to praise?"

I then assigned him to paint the eastern and western galleries and admonished him: "Painting has six principles: first, *qi* and movement that bring life; second, bone and strength in brushwork; third, responding to things and capturing their forms; fourth, applying colors according to their kind; fifth, planning and positioning; sixth, transmission and copying. In your painting, you must consider these six principles and seek beyond mere form. Perhaps then you will succeed."

The painter replied: "Understood."

Thereupon, he ground cinnabar, sipped white pigment, diluted ink, and thickened smoke. Grasping his brush, he studied intently, and after several days, completed the work. He painted armored warriors, one hundred and twenty-eight in total: two commanding generals, two seal-bearers, two standard-holders, four axe-wielders, two charioteers, two umbrella-bearers, two hat-attendants, two executioners, four messengers and order-givers, two interpreters, four gong-and-drum strikers, two cymbal players, two advancing with Han Xin sabers, eight standing at attention with slashing sabers, twenty-six with spears at hand and swords at waist, four vanguard formation-arrangers, two name-flag bearers, four signal-flag bearers, two merit-flag bearers, sixteen bearing the Eight Trigrams and Eight Arrays flags, two falconers, eight tribute-bearers from the southern lands, and the rest wielding bows, arrows, halberds, spears, and polearms, all in service. Their appearances were imposing, their ranks disciplined, their momentum continuous, their movements coordinated from head to tail. Orderly and systematic, precise and reasoned—truly, they embodied the strategies of the *Six Secret Teachings* of the Grand Duke and the military methods of Sun Wu.

Additionally, there were two retinues of attendants, forty-eight in all, their waists adorned with gold and purple, dutifully carrying out their tasks. They led and followed, flanked left and right, their artistry so exquisite it seemed as though real people were present. All who saw it marveled, saying: "Your painting is indeed masterful! Why was it so crude before, yet so refined now? Why was it so inferior before, yet so excellent now?" As the ancients said: *"Great skill appears clumsy; great wisdom appears foolish."*

That, indeed, is what it means.

The painter Ni Shou slowly replied: "What your servants painted before was merely capturing appearances to complete forms, fulfilling their structures to serve their functions. Others may have intentions, but divining them is no easy task—heart and tongue contend. What is called 'clumsy' and 'foolish' is truly so.

What your servants paint today arises from the heart, envisioned in the mind’s eye, spiritually grasped and intuitively understood, flowing with the moment as the brush is commanded, in harmony with nature. We do not know why it becomes refined or crude, nor why it becomes excellent or inferior. We simply exhaust our artistry in the doing, not employing any cunning or clever technique.

Then, can the fame of your servant’s painting truly be discussed?"

When the painting was finished, he inscribed it on the wall: *"Painted by so-and-so on such-and-such a day and month."* The inscription merely records the name.

Alas! If he were like that, then he too would seek fame through painting in the world and for posterity.

Ah! When a man is born with a seven-foot frame, he is the most numinous being between heaven and earth. With mulberry bow and reed arrows, his aspirations extend to the four directions. He must establish himself, practice the Dao, and make his name known to later generations to honor his parents—only then is a man’s duty complete. Otherwise, he is as if gagged and silenced, stifled and confused, content to be a jar or urn, like a brewing insect, growing old and dying to rot with the grasses and trees. Compared to the painter, would he not feel shame?

Who is this painter? Cai Mengchen of Yurong.

Who wrote the biography of Cai Mengchen? The Xuanfeng Lingji King.

---

**Postscript to Zhou Zhuangweng’s Stele Inscription**

What I have written before and after is merely for my own amusement—truly, like a wild dog eating celery, unaware of the sting in its mouth or the pain in its belly, how could it know the taste?

Yet I am unworthily honored by your lofty friendship and pure prose, flowing like the mighty Yellow River. Your praise of my virtue shines resplendently.

As for the numinosity of mountains and rivers and the flourishing of people, there must surely be those of extraordinary talent, Daoist arts, loyalty, and integrity born among them. Like you, who harbor ability and hold potential, long suppressed—if you could rouse yourself to stand out and respond to your circumstances, even slightly, it would be my deepest hope.

What I call the *spiritual* is virtue rooted in intelligence and uprightness, aligned with the seasons in its timing and applied to the myriad things with appropriateness. It is not the kind of spirit that flatters or curries favor to bring fortune.

Of the hundred things seen morning and night, no one pays them heed. But when something extraordinary is heard of, all gaze upon it and speak of it. What you have asked me to postscript is also in hope of such extraordinary work.

*"The song ends, no one is seen— / On the river, several green peaks remain."* Is this not a poem of the spiritual? I dare use it to express my gratitude.

---

**Postscript to Zhou Zhuangweng’s *Explanation of the Yi Diagrams***

"Lingji" is merely a temple name—why is it called a *king*? Those who hear of it cannot avoid offending the brush of the *Spring and Autumn Annals*.

Though I dare not refuse the honor of writing a postscript to your *Explanation of the Diagrams*, the profound meanings of the *Yi* are most abstruse—how can they be fathomed with a bamboo tube or a gourd dipper?

I wished to take up my brush but feared being laughed at by those who carry firewood. Thus, I have gathered the discussions of earlier scholars and, with effort, written this postscript. If I have not fully grasped your intent in the middle, I have trimmed it away—may you find it fortunate.

The study of the classics has long been unclear. Our dynasty venerates schools and cultivates talent, placing the illumination of the classics as the foremost duty. The authorities promote education and governance, and for each classic, an instructor is appointed to guide its study—such is the earnest and diligent intent of their instruction.

Of all the classics, the *Yi* is the most profound and recondite, not easily understood in its entirety. Those who study Wang Bi’s *Yi* engage in lofty discourse on principle, but their flaw is reckless extravagance. Those who study Jing Fang’s *Yi* rigidly adhere to images and numbers, but their flaw is inflexibility. Gaining in one aspect means losing in another; in the end, both are lost.

Master Jianxin Zhou of Sanshan, known as Gongbi in his official travels, possesses the *Yi Shaozheng Transmission* handed down in his family. It harmonizes both principle and images, and its words are purely and correctly derived.

The *Yi* has sixty-four hexagrams, and each hexagram discusses its primary line. Each line has its image, for which a diagram is made. Each diagram has its principle, for which an explanation is given. The explanations grasp principle, the diagrams grasp images and numbers—unified in one thread, they proceed without contradiction.

I say that Master Zhou’s study of the *Yi* is refined—how could this be achieved in a single morning or evening?

The son of a skilled archer learns to make baskets; the son of a skilled furrier learns to make fur garments. If the transmission is not correct, how could one create diagrams and explanations that fully grasp the essence of the *Yi*?

One day, a fellow student of Master Zhou—unknown to me—tucked the diagrams and explanations into his sleeve and sought my opinion. I am ignorant and unlearned; glancing at them, though not easily understood, I grasped a thing or two. It is like food: when it comes to rare delicacies from distant lands, who would not crave them?

I wished to write a postscript for carving and printing, to spread its transmission and not betray Master Zhou’s learning. Yet I fear my name is obscure and unworthy of the task.

Master Zhou’s given name is Zhuangweng, his courtesy name Zhibo, and Jianxin is his sobriquet. Those with whom he associates are all distinguished individuals—they will surely write a postscript for him.

Should I let these diagrams and explanations be used to cover jars? How fitting indeed.

In the third year of the *Dade* era, on the fourth day after the Double Ninth Festival of the *Daxian Yuan*, inscribed by the Man of Biefeng Mountain.

Emulating Jingchun’s *Qianzhou Record* to mark the title:

High ridges and deep valleys—who presides over them?
Why the mounds and trees, if not for the sages Fu Xi and the Yellow Emperor?
By middle antiquity, the northern lands flourished.
When Master Red Pine ascended as an immortal, where was the *Green Satchel*?
The study of post-heavenly arts—Guo, Li, Huang, and Yang—
A raging torrent daily overturned, grains scattered like Sun Bin’s ruse.
Tigers and dogs confused, pointing at the faintest glimmer.
The eyes of the vulgar miss what lies beside them.

The great transformations of Heaven and Earth—these are the records, these are the guiding principles.
Who revealed their subtlety? The clouds and mountains, vast and indistinct.
To exalt earth and honor the self, branches and leaves spread their fragrance.
The chief of the celestial stars governs the great constancies.
In pairs, deer and horses; in abundance, clouds and sunlight.
The sun rises from the blue sea, illuminating the four directions.
The moon nears the imperial throne, its radiance gracing the western palace’s splendor.

Heaven engenders water—its source deep, its flow long.
Emerging from the void of heaven, entering the Great One.
The *Gouchen* strides forth, the *Tengshe* conceals its treachery.
From the eastern ancestor to the western, forming abysses and marshes.
Girded with gold and jade, noble attendants stand by.
Who says the vine bears no flowers or fruit?
Consult the illustrated classics—gourds fit the pattern.
Observe the ancestral dragon: its momentum arrives, its form rests.
If it coils with affection, its "ears" must be ample.

On an auspicious day, at a propitious hour, the west responds with omens.
Not for oneself, but for one’s descendants.
How could the dragon’s path be clear if its head and tail are severed?
What delights is the celestial emolument, stretching a thousand *li*.
Blowing and plucking to avert peril, welcoming spirits and expelling ghosts.
When mountains halt, they become feet—beware the rut.
Mistaking the ox’s slumber, one still laments the hare’s flaw.
Yin flourishes, Yang wanes—each holds to its measure.
This is nature’s way, not the work of human hands.

With a keen mind and focused gaze, what need for needles or stones?
The ancients said: "The fortunate are blessed in abundance."
Kong’s tomb endures to this day, neither brambled nor thorned.
After divination and burial, its yin influence deepens further.
For a hundred generations, it shall prosper, like vines spreading melons.

I am no Qing Wu, yet I divine Zhou’s auspiciousness.
I record and leave this, inscribing it upon the stele.

**Inscription:**
*(The inscription would follow here, untranslated as it was not provided in the original text.)*

An old master holds a spade in hand,
seated upon a wicker chair, driving away the green serpent.
The serpent transforms into a dragon, encircling my home.
Who says the melon vine bears only flowers at its stem?
The pine roots twist and coil, its branches lush and full.

No need to stretch the feet—ox horns rise in jagged peaks.
No need to turn the mirror—no crooked topknots appear.
Facing the desk, brows aligned, skillfully adorning the six *ga*.
Girded with gold, bound with jade, fame gathers in the capital.
Radiance clings to sun and moon, brush and hemp wielded in the imperial academy.

Encountering a scholar on the path, his hidden merit startles—how admirable!
Is this carving truth or falsehood?
Thus inscribed upon stone, for the beholder’s pride.

The spirit of ancestral virtue—no human effort can surpass it.
From generation to generation, like roots intertwined, like reeds uprooted together.

**On Reading**
To read is to teach the people the Way of kinship and harmony.
At eight, one enters the lesser school;
at fifteen, the greater.
What is learning? It is learning from books.
Books—books! Their purpose: to record and compose, to craft words for fame and fortune alone?
Yet the essence lies in the Way of kinship and harmony, the greatest of human relations.
Observe how the sages taught the people to cherish kinship and harmony—it is recorded in books.
Those of noble intent must study deeply, ponder, and inquire.
If one knows the Way’s natural course, first practice it in oneself and in the home,
then let it shape the village, and from one village, reach the world.
To transform the people and refine customs—what is this if not the Way of kinship and harmony?
Thus the governance of all under heaven begins with loving one’s kin.
To read is to teach the people the Way of kinship and harmony.

**On Weiqi**
*Preface:*
One day, a guest in tattered robes and hempen sandals, appearing unkempt, raised his voice and challenged the painter:
"Kinship Hall—this is the spirit’s intent in teaching the people.
Yet in your painting, books have sincerity, the zither has harmony, and paintings have praise—all seem slightly inadequate to the spirit of kinship and harmony.
The art of weiqi arose in the Warring States, teaching the people the ways of war.
But to harbor deceit and cunning, to vie in falsehood—this is not the path of the wise.
I fear it does not embody the spirit’s intent for teaching kinship and harmony."
The painter had no reply and turned to me. I offered this explanation:
The board is square, reflecting the self.
The path is straight, without bias.
Black and white stones mark distinction.
Their paired arrangement sets order.
Adap

This volume lacks two texts at its end, as noted in the catalog.

**Xu Xian’s Hanzao, Volume Seven: Poetry**

*Ancient and Modern Poems from the Upper Forest Prefecture Judgeship*

Secluded, I withdraw from worldly affairs,
Silent, I watch the rise and fall of dynasties.
Once I knew it as Han,
Now I reckon it as Tang.
Deep mounds turn to valleys,
The blue sea becomes mulberry fields time and again.
Kuafu races a thousand li,
While slanderers measure but a foot in height.
Who is foolish, who wise?
What is long life, what is untimely death?
Ants cling to their cauldrons, mindless,
Moths flutter toward the flame, aimless.
Joy drains the heart first,
While sorrow drives locusts from the fields.
The hawk dies for lack of sight,
The fish perishes for greed of bait.
Human nature is as fickle as a palm,
Customs shift with the thaw and frost.
The treacherous waves of the Three Gorges,
The rugged paths of Mount Taihang.
Ill-starred, the Han ox and Dipper clash,
Fated apart, Shen and Shang never meet.
A flick of the finger—regret spans millennia,
A turn of the head—a dream dissolves.

How rich were Jin and Chu in their boasts,
How cunning Su and Zhang in their schemes.
The silken-armed snatch their brother’s food,
The upright testify against their father’s sheep.
The gibbon chatters endlessly,
Tigers tear each other in their ferocity.
Rats stand on ceremony, bowing,
Oxen stumble, wild and reckless.
The white eagle seizes the cunning hare,
The yellow sparrow snaps up the mantis.
The whole world wages war like beasts,
Only the phoenix sings of wounded times.
Poetry and books lie high on dusty shelves,
The path of benevolence and righteousness grows ever more desolate.
Rouge and powder intoxicate the markets,
Red gates resound with pipes and flutes.
Who knows the bamboo slips, inked in haste,
Lie buried in coffins of yellow cedar?
The *Hai* character misread as "pig,"
The *Zhang* jade inscribed as "hawk."
Colors blur black and white,
Techniques favor the dark and yellow.
Grain buys a post as Commandant,
Wealth secures a rank as Cavalier Attendant.
Talented men stagnate by the Liao River,
Heroes hide in Mangyang.
Desperate, they scramble for fame’s tablets,
Relentless, they tangle in profit’s reins.
Lofty caps mock the rustic ways of Lu,
Left lapels imitate the barbarian Du.
Every tongue speaks the language of Yue,
Every body bears tattoos in the light.
Vile words constantly assail the ears,
Carving flesh cannot heal the wound.
Carving wood—what cruel law!
Planting melons—what flawed governance!
Heaven’s course often loses measure,
Yin qi rises to invade yang.
Sun and moon spin like a millstone,
Heaven and earth drift like a boat unmoored.
Floating clouds stretch endlessly,
The earth below vast and boundless.
The four seas choke with dust and mist,
The Central Plains seethe with war.
Warlords vie for hegemony,
The Five Dynasties lose the royal net.

In Fujian, I serve as a minor official,
In Jiankang, I guard the fief as a vassal.
My fame resounds east of the sea,
My virtue blankets the southern lands.
Blood sacrifices endure for a thousand sacrifices,
My spirit dwells in this place.
The old shrine nears ruin,
The ancient temple fears collapse.
The flood of grace now gathers,
Yet the swallow’s nest remains dim.
No harm in divining with chicken bones,
To restore the hall of the sidelong glance.

The paintings rival Changkang’s walls,
The clay revives the walls of Master Kong.
Gongshu follows the compass and square,
The Minister of Rites adorns the robes.
The work is done with ease,
The stele’s completion is my earnest hope.
From Zhongshan, I pluck the finest brush,
From Zhenze, I fell the darkest wood.
Fish-skin sifted clean of sand,
Phoenix and tortoise take wing together.
Empty words are mere recklessness,
Yet actions are frantic and perverse.
Dark learning knows fortune and misfortune,
Golden rules reveal hidden treasures.
Wen Qin and Han Yu,
Sentences admired by Duan Wenchang.
What merit lies in trampling grass?
What strength in butting against Zhuangzi?
Tomb talks transmit the teachings of Lu,
Pavilion records dream of the Prince of Teng.
Poems praise the spirit of Mount Tai,
Prose is not the ghost wailing in the beams.
Ancient texts confirm these words,
Sacrificial rites demand solemnity.
To succeed the sages is to follow Mengzi alone,
To know Heaven is to fear no correction.
If this culture is not yet lost,
How can our Dao fail to shine?

I admire the Master’s conduct,
Vying to transmit the fragrance of the noble man.
Born like a striped leopard in the mist,
Tempered like dragon’s brew in old vats.
Jade and coral trees,
Gold and sable tails.
Calligraphy refined as Wang Xizhi’s,
Script bold as Zhong You’s.
Pouring water reveals its brilliance,
Stroking the void stirs its fervor.
The capture of the qilin nearly ended his brush,
The presentation of white silk awaits his memorial.
My meager virtue pales in shame,
His lofty talent deserves exaltation.
As you are praised for your fine reputation,
Accumulated goodness brings abundant blessings.
The name endures as long as the record,
Forget the man, but not the spirit.
Ashamed I lack silk to offer in condolence,
I pray for blessings to ward off misfortune.

Ascending and descending, the immortals scatter as guests of the Three Isles,
Ethereal and elusive, beyond all measure.
Your soul stands resolute as a ghostly hero,
For you alone are wise and upright.
The people of Jing revere ghosts,
The people of Yue honor cunning—
An old custom since the Spring and Autumn period.
How restless I am in this world,
Driving alone on a desolate road, moved to tears.
I have no belligerent heart to toy with alchemy,
Who would willingly seal themselves in silence?
I honor Confucius and Mengzi, advance Han and Liu,
Reject Buddhism and Daoism, condemn Yang and Mo.
I seek to exalt filial piety and brotherly love, discard the strange and extravagant,
To return to frugality and value sincerity.
Pointing at the air, writing in astonishment—truly strange!
Surely the people of Luoyang do not recognize this.
Alas! The Shu roads are steep and perilous,
Tigers and leopards vie to devour.
No news from the trees or the waters,
Only the village hypocrite—thief of virtue.
Who knows that in the dark room, spirits watch?
Willingly becoming ghosts and demons with petty men.
*Bok bok*, *zhuo zhuo*—the sound at the door,
The lonely owl’s cry, the screeching of birds, all clamoring.
Profit and wealth stride across the altar,
Eerie words shock the crowd with their deceit.
Who is not a Bian He or a Zhen,
Holding their hearts, unaware of others’ scorn?
The old man of Liqiu, drunk and raving,
How can one not clap in approval of the extraordinary?
Disloyal in planning, untrustworthy in friendship,
Fleeing to the clouds, their traces erased.
Jade cannot be burned on Kunlun’s peaks,
Orchids and thoroughwort must not mix with thorns.
Good rewarded, evil punished—like the ruins of Guo,
Coming events are as dark as lacquer.
Heaven bestows bright wings not by chance,
I only fear that literature will be severed and lost.
Who is the Master of the Eastern Village?
The elders’ model, esteemed above all.
The purple aura of Fengcheng shoots toward the Dipper and Ox,
In youth, his fame and brilliance blazed.
The river and mountains share the moonlight for a thousand li,
The autumn light perfectly embraces the emerald waters.
Worldly affairs change in an instant, bitterly inconstant,
Midstream, the swimmer struggles against the surging waves.
Pure and secluded, I leave the dust behind,
Eating from an ox-cart, singing of white stones.
Tall hat and long sash, crane-feather robe,
I bow deeply to the lords, following Du and Bai.

The swift brilliance of your writing startles me,
Like unhewn jade split from the earth—no trace of axe or chisel.
Dragons sport in the sea’s depths, flashing fang and horn,
Red banners shimmer in hues of crimson and green.
The stele of Hongdu now stands complete,
A thousand chariots crowd the streets and lanes.
Zither and lute find kindred spirits,
Two masters of the art vie in carving and polishing.
I, shamed by fine greens, writhe in stomach-churning envy,
Dare I boast of meat before the butcher’s gate?
Yet Shangqiu’s uncarved jade must not be cast aside—
With adze and knife, let the guiding thread be drawn.
They say a single word is worth a thousand gold,
Yet I lack the brush of Wang Xizhi to lock or seal.
The Old Man of Stone Weir—what greed for treasures!
He demands a hundred thousand coins of yin.
The Master of Guanzhong lives no more,
No midnight gift can repay Wang Mi.
The miser’s chatter grates on all who hear,
Bowing low, sweating, trembling in vain.
The gates of nobles are deep as the sea—no word escapes,
Is it that they deliberately set such harsh limits?
How dare I send a guest-minister as envoy,
To open the Grand Granary and dispense a single grain?
If the shrine should rank first under heaven,
All mouths would speak with one voice of your merit.
Jade and pearls cannot repay you,
I can only wish you a lifespan like the Southern Mountains.
May your descendants restore their beginnings,
And may this deed be woven into the unseen web of fate.

---

**Song of the Captured Qilin**

They sing of the captured qilin,
Sing of the captured qilin—
The qilin’s name is paired with benevolence.
Its tail like an ox’s, hooves like a horse’s,
Its body sleek, yet one horn stands clear, never harming a soul.
Its voice, now clear, now deep, harmonizes with the pitch-pipes,
Its steps, whether turning or straight, follow the compass and square.
It treads not on living insects, nor travels in herds,
Nor tramples green grass, choosing its dwelling with care.
O benevolent qilin! O wondrous beast!
When this creature appears, the age is at peace.
We thought peace would come in our time,
Yet who could have foreseen that today would fall short of the past?
Falling short of the past—
Strange creatures of bizarre forms now emerge without end.
On Zhangyi’s mountain, crows like cranes,
Called the *Bifang*, born with but a single leg.
Where it appears, fire ravages homes,
So by day they dare not light lamps, by night no candles burn.
Then there is the *Guiche*, the Night-Traveling Ghost!
Ten heads, ten mouths, sharing a single throat.
Its cry like a cartwheel’s creak, its voice hoarse and rasping,
Dripping blood on households, bringing misfortune and woe.
Heaven and Earth endow creatures with their natures—
Why are some born ill-omened, not auspicious?
The world’s fickle passions love and hate,
Hearing good, they rejoice; hearing ill, they rage.
I hear that evil men bring calamity,
None more so than the *Guiche* and *Bifang*.
O benevolent qilin! You are seen no more—
Where are the good men who daily do good?
Good men reap fortune, evil men reap disaster,
For disaster follows excess, fortune follows virtue—this is Heaven’s Way.
King Wu’s grandson met a great merchant,
And when a qilin was captured in the wild, it was deemed ill-fated.
He gave it to the gamekeeper, who asked Confucius,
And Confucius, moved by the qilin, composed the *Spring and Autumn Annals*.
The *Annals*’ teachings strike the mean,
Yet why did he lay down his brush to end it?
O benevolent qilin! Your time was unkind,
The laying down of the brush—my Way is exhausted.
Confucius has been dead for thousands of years,
The ways of the world are no longer ancient—who laments this?
Good men, evil men—I know not which,
But praise and blame remain in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*.
This culture is not yet lost—we seek it in the gods,
Entrusting words to posterity, still admonishing.
Good men do good, and good is encouraged,
Evil men do evil, and evil is punished.
Confucius, the sage—none can compare,
So I lay down my brush and sing of the captured qilin.

---

**Laying Down the Brush: A Song**

Life flits by like a dream in Huaxu,
Restless comings and goings, chasing profit.
Confucius and Mencius are dead, never to rise again—
What has become of the Way of benevolence and righteousness?
The Son of Heaven broke the snake and grasped the Mandate,
Su Qin and Li Yiji mocked the pedantic scholars.
They never read half a line of books,
Yet rode in vermilion wheels and grand carriages, their gates wide.
They boast of their wealth in gold and pearls,
Tossing a million in a single throw, shouting down the gaming tables.
The moon’s spine is gnawed—alas, who sleeps?
Translators babble in foreign tongues, their words garbled.
They pour out wine but do not drink—how foolish their schemes!
Goblins blame their shadows—how stupid they are!
They compose rhapsodies to rival *Zixu*,
Wielding ladles and scrubbing pots, playing the host.
Yang Dafu nearly died after being thrown into prison,
His wit and humor fallen, his integrity lost.
O literature! O literature! How utterly ruined!
Even the starving ghosts clap their hands and jeer.
Thus I know that Gun, drowned in the Feathered Waters,
Was transformed into a dark fish.
Shu’s tiger eyes and pig’s snout—
He died for his greed and corruption.
Yang Shiwo brought disaster to the Yangshe clan,
His mother deaf to the cries of his orphan.
O spirits! O spirits! You who probe the world’s sorrows!
Why cast hexagrams to divine fortune?
Why count bamboo slips at the courtyard’s edge?
Uprightness is virtue—
Why burn pepper and cinnamon in the crucible?
Literature is speech—
Why wield the three-inch tongues of Zhang and Su?
Alas! To know the Way of transformation—
Is to know the workings of the divine.
Have you not seen
Wen Yougong repairing the Jiangzhi Temple,
Dreaming of an omen in Chengdu?
Or Zhong Shiheng bringing the Guangliang to Mianchi,
Using the clapping-hand man as his vanguard?
Have you not seen
Yu Bo compiling the *Soushen Ji*,
Called the Dong Hu of his age?
Who are these men?
Who are these men, who dare dismiss ghosts as nothingness?
The Prefect of Yuzhang, discoursing on the *Spring and Autumn*,
Lost his life.
At Niushijiao, burning rhinoceros horn,
The paths of the seen and unseen diverged.
Enough! Enough!
The brush that captured the qilin is laid down,
I shall speak no more of Yao and Shun.

---

**Ballad of Liangfu**

Black cloth transformed into silk,
An ancient prophecy, all men say.
The stars coil around the Dipper and Ox,
The astrological division belongs to Min-Yue.
To the left, he bows to the Giant Turtle Spirit,
Its peaks and ridges rising like sulfur.
To the right, he bows to Fangshan’s crags,
Its stone walls towering, jagged and sheer.
The tidal waters flow from the Milky Way,
Waxing and waning with the pine and moon.
Once a day it comes to court,
Winding through Jiang and Ke in its circuit.
Huang’s *Sifting Sand Classic*
Reveals sand shaped like bream and mandarin fish.
Then there is Li Boshao,
Whose *Illustrated Classic* depicts rafts of *ning*.
Three peaks stand like a tripod,
Rising abruptly from the flat earth.
Guo’s *Green Satchel Book*
Calls this the cave of the phoenix’s idle perch.
Truly, the art of fengshui is wondrous,
Its spirit soaring, its vitality unbounded.

Men are all Yao and Shun’s folk,
In ancient simplicity, they revered the Buddha’s sigh.
Scholars rose by poetry and books,
Their carriages rolling past scholar trees.
The common folk found peace in farming,
Fields yielding grain like furrows of hares.
Those summoned to the Golden Horse Gate,
Mastered a hundred arts with precision.
At midday markets, merchants thrived,
The Treasury’s great ledgers displayed.
In years of peace, joy abounded,
Spring winds brought boundless delight.

Of late, the present falls short of the past,
Old days now drift like hazy dreams.
The Maker’s craft is truly cunning,
Sun and moon grow ever darker.
No more is heard the *Song of Striking the Earth*,
Only hearts knotted with gloom.
No more the aid of village friends!
Serpents and insects devour each other’s years.
No more loyalty and trust,
Only lawsuits and mutual accusations.
No more benevolence and righteousness,
Only deceit and force in arrogant strife.
No more the whir of looms,
Year’s end brings no cloth or coarse garb.
Bo Yi and Shu Qi died on Shouyang,
No longer gathering bracken and fern.
The House of Fang and Du’s renown has faded,
No more boasting of illustrious lineage.
Confucius and Mencius, old scholars, seem foolish,
Their pure discourse now leans toward aged Buddhas.
Like Han and Chu swayed by slander,
They wither, sighing for Jia and Qu.
Ask why this has come to pass—
The *qi* of fengshui has already waned.
Once mountains stood in embrace,
Now the wind’s path is broken.
Once tidal waters flowed smooth,
Now the tides run dry.
Once pines lined the roads,
Now they are felled and gone.
Once wells gushed with springs,
Now the wells are parched.

Do not say this is fate’s doing—
Yin and yang must not be neglected.
Fengshui is not man’s work,
Save for the wells he digs himself.
How foolish are your words,
While mine brook no hesitation.
Where there is decay, there is renewal,
Where there is ruin, there is flourishing.
Mountains and waters remain mountains and waters,
Men remain men.
Is literature not precious?
The nobleman’s virtue will yet shine like the leopard’s spots.
Farming must seize the season,
Rain and seedlings burst with life.
Tao Zhu’s wealth spanned the realm,
His diligence reaped even the finest profit.
A humble craft surpasses fertile fields,
Year-round toil must stand firm.
I urge you: shun idleness,
Lest like a horse you lose the bit’s restraint.
I urge you: do no wrong,
For the law’s blade cuts deep.
I urge you: crave not excess,
Lest reckless acts mar your face and eyes.
I urge you: drink no wine,
Lest drunkenness lead you to ruin.
I urge you: deceive not your heart,
For heaven punishes deceit.
I urge you: do good deeds,
Good deeds nurture orchids and irises.
Behold Lingji Temple,
Where benefactors’ names are carved in sandalwood.
A village hails its virtuous men,
Their fame enduring through ten thousand ages.
My words are bitter as medicine,
Engrave them in your heart like a stele.

**Rejoicing in Rain**
Nine wheels blaze like scorching fire,
Mountains and rivers washed clean, seedlings withered.
The farmer’s back drips sweat in bitter toil,
Gazing endlessly for clouds, his eyes parched.
No more tales of cutting melons or severing hair,
Who now would pray to the mulberry groves for the people?

Blazing crowns, scorching arms—offerings of heart’s incense,
Yet still we hope heaven’s heart may relent from calamity.
The seals on rivers and lakes are too stern,
The Six Dings dare not unlock the golden locks.
The stele of the Sacred Dragon’s traces still stands,
Yet idle talk claims Old Dragon of Biefeng is senile.

Alas, how the customs of the age twist and turn,
Outward guise shifts in an instant, the heart already follows.
Wu Ridge exhorts the phoenix to soar to Tian Mountain,
Its cliff paths treacherous and slick—few tread there.
This rain at this hour is but chance,
Yet how could neighbors not have sought it sooner?

Addition and subtraction follow fate, fate follows heaven,
The formless image soars high and boundless.
The Master of the Eastern Sea blazes with divine power,
Having revealed heaven’s secrets to make them plain to you.
The sun sets on Western Mountain, idle words arise,
The prophecy of *xu* and *wu* is profoundly obscure.

These words are a contract that does not deceive me,
Gazing up to the radiant spirit as if it stood at my left.
If indeed the earth gods bring forth abundance,
A single drop from heaven’s gourd will overturn.
Then fields will be rich and drenched,
White-haired elders and yellow-haired children dance as one.
Loins ache from reaping the ripe harvest, joyous in the year’s plenty,
Slaughtering chickens to prepare for tasting the new rice.

The people never forget to offer thanks,
Presenting stream-grown ferns and pondweeds in tribute.
How dare the gods claim credit for themselves?
They but repay heaven’s grace with rain and celebration.

*Xielin’s* judgment on the incense missive:
Tales of ghosts and spirits—what are they but emptiness?
Soundless, formless, fundamentally void.
Idols of clay and wood are no true sages—
Ask them the dipper’s handle, and they know not east from west.

Outside the gate, the sound of carving and chiseling—
Who would know a guest has come?
Kong Rong sat and gazed upon the divine bones of purple jade,
His breast untrammled, his qi swallowing the rainbow.
Passed down to the master, the pearls strung with art,
Their pure light piercing the slumber of the black dragon.
Obtaining such marvels, astonishment and joy,
Nodding in silent dread, lips pressed in anxious muttering.
Ziqi is dead and will not live again—
Who now can distinguish *shang* from *gong*?
Forced to stitch and patch, losing the path’s tread,
Better to embrace silence, equal to the blind and deaf.

I know this visit bears some intent—
These few pages are no mere answer to a goose cage.
Face flushed, back damp with sweat, how dare I leave?
With eyes of broad vision, I contain seas and rivers.
The master strides forth, lifting the veil for me,
Well-versed in the Five Classics and the masters’ histories.

I recall my youth in Chang’an,
Golden seals and starry sashes gleaming at my waist.
Looking back, worldly affairs are but fleeting echoes,
Leaving me alone to drive joy to its end.
The scholar’s cap and robe ill suit Yue’s ways—
Now nine in ten chase the southern wind.
How many rich and mighty have fallen in reversal?
The customs of Wuling’s earth differ from old.
Xi and E race on, never pausing—
Suddenly, beard and temples are snow and tumbleweed.

How to seek joy in mountains and waters?
From my sleeve, I draw the green satchel to visit Master Zheng.
Recognize this inch of ground for what it will be,
Entrust it to sons and grandsons to learn the old farmer’s ways.

*Inscription for Secretary Lin’s Stele*
Ugly mouths lost their governance,
The Five Dynasties contended in might.
The four seas seethed like a boiling cauldron,
The Central Plains grew wild with mugwort and wormwood.
At fifteen, I studied books and histories;
At twenty, I took up shield and spear.
Leading troops by the hundreds of thousands,
I spurred my horse across the Huai River.
The imperial archives honored my commission,
Leaving me to guard the banner’s sway.
The people of Huai welcomed me with laughter,
Men plowing, women weaving in song.

In the *Kaiyun* era, I entered southern Min,
Daring not to violate even a hair.
South of the gorges, the road was lined with green cloth,
Towering peaks like giant turtles.
The temple’s image stood beside the true,
Lofty, rooted in the mountain’s curve.
The dragon’s head received its due worship,
That year named *Zhenghe*.
The people diligently offered thanks,
Presenting stream and marsh’s bounty.

Four hundred years have passed,
Yet the path remains free of wind and wave.
Resplendent, the spirit’s power shines forth—
How numerous the events recorded!
But tongues grow stiff with age,
Each word awry and errant.
Drawing a tiger, they make a dog—
The clamor of many mouths is but noise.
Poetry and books lie bound on high shelves,
Later scholars lose the way of Qiu and Ke.

I hear of your righteous talent,
These pages exchanged for a white goose.
Your writing crowns the age,
Words flowing like a hanging river.
Your brush’s array sweeps a thousand armies,
Seal script and clerical forms sharp as awls.
Silver hooks like phoenix wings in flight,
Iron strokes like dragons coiling and gliding.
Carving stone to make a stele,
At your touch, it becomes a lesson struck.
My deeds and your name,
Will endure unblemished through the ages.

*To the Recluse of South Village*
The Recluse of South Village,
Renowned for poetry by rivers and seas for years now,
Suddenly arose with verses of return—
His mother dead, unburied, he could not bear to leave Lu in sorrow,
So took refuge on Deer Mountain,
Humbly arranging her funeral.

One day, he came to me with incense in his sleeve,
And I borrowed an ancient line to compose a quatrain to encourage him.
He further honored me with *Fox-Fur and Jade-Fish*, his *Yu Yan Collection*,
Its pages ablaze before my eyes.
With respect, I followed the rhymes within,
Matching them as best I could.
This rough offering is but a moment’s gift—
How could it deserve to be cast in metal?

Lest one mistake purple for vermilion—how dare I presume kinship with the ranks of extraordinary men and lofty scholars? Yet I have been honored with prefaces and postscripts, their praise so excessive it verges on mimicry. Thus, I append twenty-two verses in the ancient and modern styles of Master Thatched-Hut, humbly attaching them after the preface to *Fox-Fur*, a small token of gratitude. Heh heh.

The daylily withered long ago,
The Meng-mother’s neighbor lies in weeds.
Alas, in the void of the grave’s silence,
Loneliness soaks my robes and kerchief.
A thousand gold gifts for the traveler’s road,
Yet the demands of foreign customs leave one poor.
Now my temples are streaked with gray,
The candle dims, and my painted brows fade.
I sketch the lands I’ve traversed on the ground—
Homeless, I must bear this aging frame.
The high heavens’ intent is mournful,
For you, my nose stings with sorrow.

By nature I follow Kāśyapa,
Singing as I walk—not truly a hermit.
I call to the mountains, but no deer stays,
Leaning on my staff, I follow others.
The clay scholar transmits divine words,
Yet my gate and courtyard fear frequent guests.
This floating world has its fixed fate—
How much more so for me, lost in barbarian dust?

Hoisting sail beneath the Southern Dipper,
I gaze at the North Star from a perilous tower.
The balmy wind moves in accord with the seasons,
The vast sea stretches without a ford.
Like a bear in fellowship, I suffer disdain,
Yet my empty heart endures bending and stretching.
I urge you: cease your sighs of regret,
For meeting me fulfills a long-held kinship.

My writings wear thin with revision,
Yet I cherish the honor of your fine mats.
I gladly mingle with the misty haze,
Envious of the painted qilin’s pair.
Wang Di’s legacy should be true,
Chu Suiliang’s calligraphy is peerless.
Gao and Cen walk at a measured pace,
Geng and Jia are also revered ministers.
The dense bamboo laughs in winter,
A faint fragrance borrows from the islet’s duckweed.
In pure discourse, one tastes its savor,
Yet sidelong glances pain the spirit.

A clear mirror hung brings weariness,
Thoughts stir like fish in a dried-up pool.
Late, I ponder the author’s intent—
Again, he takes things to be spring.
The morning’s breath admires those with purpose,
Knowing my laziness is genuine.
Like Xu Ruzi, I am honored,
Our meetings ever renewed.

*Imitating Zhou’s "Lotus City"*
Where do Xi and E race in their chariots?
They say the crimson elder knows no age.
You, sir, grew weary of the world and ascended as an immortal—
Who will pity my misfortune, left behind?
I raise my face to heaven and wail, tears streaming,
Body stiff, spirit lost, as if near death.
When the coffin lid is sealed, weariness craves rest,
Yet lotus after lotus is no dream—my eyes see clearly.

Your countenance smiles as if alive,
The moon’s beauty shines down, illuminating the rafters.
Crane-capped and crane-cloaked, you ride a dragon’s plow,
Ahead, a cicada-chariot; behind, a team of four.
Left banners, right standards, dancing pennants,
Empty songs and grand music fill the air.
Pine and willow line the lane, yet it sits in a bustling market,
Winding paths diverge north and south.
Who carved the stone wall’s inscriptions?
Oyster-shell phoenixes leap and flutter in hooked strokes.
To the west, the Hall of Western Restraint stands on one side,
To the east, the Hall of True Unity belongs to the Great Mystery.
On the hall’s stars hang lanterns like ten thousand *hu* of lotuses,
Exotic fragrances overpower dragon’s spittle.
Asking those nearby, the matter is clear—
They point: this place is called the Heavenly Hook.

Once, its master was but Yu Quan,
Now the age must find a successor—not just any man.
You, sir, were called a worthy of the world,
Meeting across a thousand kalpas, good causes intertwined.
From Yan’s lineage, you received jade texts,
Seeking the hidden, you grasped the true essence.
Rain and dew moisten even the Nine Springs,
Your fasts and rituals shine with clarity.
When the tally of merit and deeds reached three thousand,
Your name was inscribed on jade tablets by the flaming pearl’s shore.
Unwilling to take your place, you delayed,
The Emperor said: such matters have their constants and flexibilities.

The joys of heaven’s music are also fitting—
Why look back and hesitate further?
Each person wears pearl-studded slippers and sable caps,
Their hairpins and sashes, their immortal robes, more radiant still.
In the eastern and western wings, ranks align,
Coming and going like drifting wind and mist.
Bowing and receiving with reverent ceremony,
Their fame, culture, and splendor fill the four seats.

In life, you did not vie with things for beauty,
Yet before death, you ascended in an instant.
Your son, clad in [missing character], will hold the whip,
The heavenly steps rise sheer to their towering peak.
Sixty warriors clad in armor,
Their might and fury wild and boundless.
One sees only their fur bristling, shoulders trembling,
Souls and dreams flying away and returning.
The tale of the plantain deer is indeed true—
The gulf between the hidden and the manifest is as vast as heaven and abyss.

What has passed flows like a river,
Faint as a battle’s flank, yet my worries burn.
Unable to fulfill filial duty to the end,
I must cast aside this body in grief.
The tale of Lotus City is told in the world—
I wish to follow Su Shi and continue its legacy.
Yet the affairs of mourning and life’s entanglements press,
My laughter fades, my voice grows hoarse—I cannot match its elegance.

I do not delight in jest or mockery,
But wish to show later generations what is so.
Your plot of land is level and smooth,
Planted with melons and willows, may they flourish.
I firmly believe the Way of Heaven is straight as a bowstring,
Yet you received the Emperor’s favor, singular and devoted.
Desires approach—you heard them first,
To prosper your descendants and glorify your ancestors.
Your family’s caps and sashes fill the gates,
Soon, the old possessions will return to the green felt.
Admiring your virtue, I emulate Shen and Qian,
Do not betray your mother’s steadfast will—do not err.
Dedicate yourself to books and history, strive with diligence,
Avoid other pursuits, do not favor pipes and strings.

Your grand deeds are a true field of blessing,
Your family’s legacy flows like an unceasing stream.
A great mansion’s support is not just eight hundred bushels,
Under Xi’s shelter, joy and safety abound.
Always remember to offer sacrifices, advance the rituals,
To perpetuate the family’s affairs with diligence.
Your dream’s emotions are entwined,
What you saw was not inverted or confused.
It aligns like lotus roots and stems,
Every sign and omen confirms it.

The Creator speaks not, yet subtly turns the wheel,
Revealing your virtue for all to see.
I record these events, inscribing them on bamboo,
Though onlookers shrink back, fearing involvement.

*For Lord Lin of East Village*
King Zhou’s cruelty married the woman of Su,
The man from the Eastern Sea fished at Ox Mountain.
The First Emperor’s wrath burned his books,
The tragedy of Xinggu was a crime deserved.
Zhengze wandered far from women,
Wishing to join Peng Xian in daily joy.
Ziyipi of Zhu was called Tao Zhu,
Why did he take a small boat and float on the Five Lakes?

Essence and qi drowned, marrow dried,
Blind, deaf, mute—content in self-delusion.
The traces of steering are ever halting,
The sounds of crooning—what do they sing?
The Nine Wrappings no longer rest on tall catalpas,
Crying at night, hiding by day—tamed like a fox.

Alas, the customs of the age weave autumn’s thoroughwort,
Beneath the grass lie river fences and coarse-woven carts.
The Heavenly Ladder and Stone Ramparts are called perilous paths—
Yet compared to the world’s roads, they are but rugged trails.
The Bohai’s great abyss is named the Returning Void—
Yet beside the River of Desire, it is but filth and greed.
Once, scholars rich in learning burrowed like thieves,
Zealously chasing profit, guarding their hoards.
Smoothly they feign deceit, panting with flattery,
Their cunning and baseness ensnaring even the wise.
Emperors, kings, hegemons—each age differs,
No leisure for bows and courtesies like Yao and Shun.
Above, the universe; below, the cosmos—a single tendril,
From ancient past to present, but a fleeting moment.
Ten thousand fireflies scatter at dusk into the great void,
Dawn’s tangled threads race past like a colt through a crack.
Who fathoms the vast spirit’s primordial beginning?
The land of Wang Mang guards its sealed borders.
The Yellow Dragon bears forth the Five-Colored Chart,
The divine tortoise’s eight trigrams reveal heaven’s pivot.
Ancient texts and relics are no falsehood—
Cease prattling of Zhuangzi and Laozi as mere emptiness.
For over four hundred years, offerings have filled the temple,
Once bearing the imperial staff to govern Jiangdu.
The auspicious decree of Jiaxi’s reign brought blessed rain,
The sacred honor of Lingji’s enfeoffment shone in the village.
Of late, the shrine lay desolate and overgrown,
Now rebuilt on a grand scale.
I compose this stele text, casting a pearl into darkness—
Who will bear its weight and lend it praise?
Like you, a master strategist hailed as a great scholar,
Your integrity and resolve illuminate the nine regions.
Your words and brush lead the vanguard,
Stripping the Three Armies of their courage, routing Xihao.
The jade script gleams resplendent, unfurling its brilliance—
Yet who hesitates to beg for inscriptions and records?
Two letters sent, no reply—what then?
Even speech falters, lips stammering.
Only your sacred virtue matches the earth’s vastness,
I, Xu, recount these deeds in words.

**Answering Questions on Tomb Construction**
The old graves of Beimang stretch endlessly,
City and capital’s wealth promised for a thousand years.
Tombstones carve names as large as bushels—
Ask their descendants: who remains?
Auspicious and ominous signs were divined in those years,
Yet fate’s reversals—what was their purpose?
Fortune and misfortune have no gate; man summons them himself,
The heart’s core is the foundation of blessing.
Alas, the world’s people are foolish and deluded,
Why must great families erect fences between them?
Do they not recall the ghosts of shared ancestry?
How much more so for those bound as branches intertwined.
Where now is the rise and fall of royal mausoleums?
To bury a mother and be called a filial son—how could one bear it?
Have you not seen:
The imperial qi of Jinling once flourished,
Yet the Qin sought to suppress it.
The palace bore Wu Zetian,
And Gaozong was not unaware.
If even the state fares thus,
Why doubt the ways of men?
To dig graves and burn corpses is no noble virtue—
What you would not desire, do not impose on others.
Both families harbor hidden deeds,
But above, the gods cannot be deceived.

**Answering Zhou Shiyu’s Questions on Affairs**
South to Yue, north to the Hu,
West into Qin, east into Wu—
All are heroes, true men.
Setting forth on winding roads,
Bow of fleabane in hand, mulberry arrows at the waist.
The flowers of Heyang bloom in splendor,
Let me ride my donkey as I please.
In life, friendships know no age,
Bound by affection as thick as creeping grass.
Once there were Chen and Lei,
Now there are Huang and Su.
Your heart and mine align—
Why consult the shamans of Yue?

Now the path is smooth and wide,
A single meal of fine vegetables brings contentment.
The two words "peace and safety" gladden a family letter.

**Answering Master Lü’s Questions on Horses and Rice**
The old man of Gechuan, Wang Fangping,
Passed through Dongwu to visit Cai Jing.
The people saw no immortal’s form,
Only heard the sound of carriages and horses in the void.
Golden platters and jade cups arrayed eight delicacies,
As the wine waned, cloud-borne sheng music played.
In an instant, riding the wind with jade pendants raised,
Tiger-drawn and dragon-borne chariots sped to the purple heavens.
He left me a scroll called *The Yellow Court*,
Cinnabar that was once rice transformed.
Five colors dazzle the eye, radiant and bright,
Yet in the realm of immortals, sun and moon never tarry.
The sea’s waters shallowed, stirring dust,
While the men of Baxian barked at the orange-dwellers.
Why do worldly folk vie in rivalry?
Shorn hair and yellowed foreheads—what are they for?
Hurriedly chasing wins and losses in reckless gambling.
Have you not seen:
In the dark sea, dates grow large as melons,
On Ying Mountain, plums swell like jars.
How does the joy of the orange’s midst compare?
Feast on dates and plums—
They can preserve long life.

**Encouraging the Painter Cai**
They say the Cai family excels in painting,
A tiger-headed general reborn.
Fine or coarse, good or ill—all have their standards,
Capturing spirit and qi with truth.
Last year on the Double Ninth, you came to visit,
Painting elite soldiers and spirits in their forms.
Mighty warriors with great feathered arrows at their waists,
Their bristling hair and whiskers striking awe.
To east and west, the stars of fortune and eminence,
Each stroke and dot brimming with vitality.
The court teems with scholars in crimson and purple—
Now I believe the scholar’s cap does not deceive.
Above, you paint perfected beings attending the imperial palace,
Their light clinging to sun, moon, and stars.
Below, you spread rain and dispel thunder,
Personally bearing the emperor’s decree to bless the living.
Returning to your grotto, you play immortal music,
Sleeves scented with the faint fragrance of the imperial censer.
On either side, jade balustrades emerge,
Silver hooks hang high, crimson curtains drawn.
The *Liupo* melody dances with slender waists,
A hundred amusements jest with wit and charm.
Only the bedchamber’s painting remains unfinished—
I know your skilled hand values brush and silk.
You cannot leave, you cannot leave,
Painters like you are rare indeed.
Year after year, year after year,
All say a steadfast heart can pierce stone.
Wind and moon cost no coin,
Every sip and bite is predestined.
Yet they say only the Xu family’s son,
Unaware that peach and cassia are immortals.
Last year’s song left meaning unexpressed,
Now I compose this ancient poem to urge the traveler.

**Answering a Student’s Questions on Eye Ailments**
They say the five aggregates are all thieves—
Who would claim Heaven is cautious with the whites of eyes?
Better to gouge and hang them at my gate,
Than mimic the frown of one who boasts of purple stone.
Fu Sheng’s debt of books remains unpaid,
Yet even Zhu Xin and King Liu left Kong’s estate.
Rooted in sorrow for the Dao, not for poverty—
Why then do pearl-like tears fall from my lashes?
A boat’s itch cannot be scratched,
Of late, red and festering, dim and blurred.
I shame myself for wit not what it was,
My spirit withered, sinking into delusion.
Before the lamp, flames flicker, halos bloom,
Hair sparse, yellow and green—where is the jade-like blue?

Where do the returning geese point their homeward flight?
Fortunate to know east from west, south from north.
Bright moon, brain-bladder—blame the toad’s webbed sin,
Yet who dusts the terrace mirror’s grime?

Before and after me, how many Hua Tuos?
Gold needles, medicinal dots, wheat-grain acupuncture.
Is this not a deep-seated malady sinking into the vital organs?
Why then does the skin remain stubbornly impervious?

"Subtract two from seven"—what foul talk!
Laughable, these talisman-scribes wielding brush and blade.
Wu Cheng is dead and will not revive—
Fortune and misfortune, blessings and calamities, who can fathom their schemes?

I reckon your heart ever open and unburdened,
Never say this old man’s fate is thin.
Trace the cause—who bears the fault?
I call myself a book-madman, a transmission-obsessive.

Why not suspend your topknot and pierce your flesh more sharply?
Year after year, nine nines exhaust my thoughts and strength.
The blue-robed servant neglects his duty—
One night, the west wind stirs the pebbles.

Leaves wither, their cure harsher than winter’s bite,
The mountain-forest resolve now broken in dreams.
Ask Shu He the reason—
Beneath my fingers, the *ri guan* pulse.

Faint, weak, floating, scattered—how can one know?
The six excesses unregulated, qi clashing in rebellion.
I am no companion of Yu Ling’s peers,
How would I recognize Li’s three palatable bites?

I hear the divine fire turns the great potter’s wheel,
Song Yangli’s son holds the wondrous formula.
Vast and obscure, fundamentally wordless—
Yet let us try to gather words and lift them up.

Bear-gall ointment for blindness, from the *Zhou Hou*,
I entrust it to you, sir, to assuage my duty.
Your reputation resounds across the four seas—
I wish to seek a single word as my mouth’s sustenance.

**Inscription Requested by Dong Village Elder**
The spirit has a record—since receiving the seal and cover,
I am asked for an inscription to extol its virtues and fulfill its aspirations.
It must match Sun Chuo’s prose,
Then be carved in stone, preserving the ancient style of thirty-eight lines,
From one to ten, then ten back to one.
Stele, record.
The spirit is rather extraordinary.
Not for fame, merely to narrate the events.
Though pleased to appraise, I am especially grateful not to be spurned.

It receives blood offerings in this region,
Its name heard across the Five Dynasties.
Beginning in the Kaiyun Jiachen year,
Now the Zhiyuan Gui Si year.
Eight thousand valiant riders comfort the people of Min,
Over four hundred years, the temple stands on this ground.
Wisdom, brightness, uprightness—only virtue is fragrant,
Fortune and misfortune, blessings and calamities, are brought about by human deeds.

Alas! The world’s path is harder than Shu’s roads to heaven,
Why trouble with books when the Han king vies in cunning?
Silk-traders, dog-butchers, the clamorous Fan and Cao, Guan and Ying,
Presenting memorials and phoenix odes, the mediocre Dong Zhongshu and Jia Yi.

Ancestral deceit prevails, neighbors like carved jade bound together,
Wolfish greed and cruel excess—above and below, all vie for profit.
The world has no constant worthies, a single classic outweighs gold,
Who discerns the eight characters on the reverse of the Cao E stele?

You, sir, inherit a transmitted learning,
Like the reverent attendant minister, your twin in virtue.
Having received your iron strokes and silver hooks,
I must now craft ornate prose and brocade thoughts.

The shrine’s composition, Jiang Du’s writings,
The temple’s *Luochi Record*.
Truly peerless in talent,
The seal script surpasses all four styles.

Borrowing another’s voice to express my own intent,
There is a request—to seek shelter, sincerity, shame.
**Seeing You Off**
Wrong, wrong, wrong—why sing of the petty?
Wrong, wrong—yet not truly wrong,
Alas, the world’s ways are so thin and cruel.

Do not say the lychee’s branches glow red,
Or that the golden crow pecks the dawn’s spittle.
A thousand-household lord may well boast,
Dancing in a vat, laughing in self-delight.

The Feng Cheng sword, the Kunshan jade—
Such words today are mere empty gestures.
Is it that Zhan Yin’s divination fails me,
Or that Hu Gong is foolish, and I with rotten rope?

Wrong, wrong, wrong—why sing of the petty?
Wrong, wrong—yet not truly wrong.
This year at Nian Shoal,
Next year, the name becomes *E* (calamity).

Thunder rumbles, the sky’s drum resounds,
The outer dragon stirs, sprouting horns.
The four seas’ teeming masses await the life-giving rain,
Leap straight into the heavenly pond!

Do not say the years have been bitterly hard,
Birds startled in crooked trees, fish harmed by bait,
Like the cold tortoise withdrawing its head into its shell.
Have you not seen the diligent farmer,
Toiling through spring plowing and summer sowing, hoping for autumn’s harvest?

Worldly affairs are all thus—
With a heart as firm as stone, even it can be chiseled.
Wrong, wrong, wrong—why sing of the petty?
Wrong, wrong—yet not truly wrong.

I send this word to the hermit of Shu Villa:
Linzi is not the deer beneath the plantain.
Huan Tan vehemently argued against prognostication’s unreliability—
Who knew one arrow would truly strike the autumn parrot?

What day is today?
I instruct you to pass the seventh month’s first day.
What day is the next?
The second day of the tenth month, as divined, is auspicious.

The wind howls,
The horse hesitates.
The road is long and pressing,
The carriage’s wheels gleam.

Shouldering your load, stepping high, you ask again—
How many more miles to Qingyuan?
The customs differ, the land vast, the sea boundless,
Here, peaks and ridges stand tall.

The people are sparse, the smoke faint,
In the quiet lies the ancient double-walled city.
The county magistrate’s crossbowmen lead the way,
Foreign merchants and Hu traders follow in succession.

Moonlit pearl waters, granary jade brimming red,
Precious treasures, gold-threaded nets.
Coral and green trees, two feet tall,
Plus eight hundred *hu* of black pepper.

The customs checkpoint has its regulations,
Public levies and private monopolies.
What is gained is so abundant—
Felt bundles, woven mats, loaded on donkeys and camels.

I pray you, do not forget the old neighbor,
Return and share with me a single ladle.
Wrong, wrong, wrong—why sing of the petty?
Now, more than ever, hold fast.

The evening game board’s situation is most perilous,
This move is decisive—one step determines all.
Do not squander your journey,
This piece is played for you.

The forerunner awakens the latecomers,
You few, Chen’s strategy.
The bow and arrow aim in all four directions—
Who would fill the ditches?

Wealth and honor do not lie in heaven,
Self-reliance stands on its own.
Exhort one another earnestly—
Wrong is not truly wrong.

**A Jest, Laughable**
Laughable, laughable,
Do not overlook the subtle.
Investigating things to extend knowledge—
The *Great Learning*’s essence.

Naturally, so naturally,
The wondrous workings of spirit and brightness.
Unknowing, unperceiving,
Vast and obscure.

Words do not attest to me,
Deeds may be mocked.
Fortune and misfortune have no gate,
Only humans summon them.

With your talents, sir,
Your fame shines resplendent.

As with your aspirations—mountain forests, the taste of whistling winds,
Inscribing pillars like Sima Xiangru, restoring temples in Chengdu.
Who is that man? His ambitions are no small matter.
To emulate Yan Hui is still to be Yan—who would call you unworthy?
Yet your schemes today—how narrow your gaze.
If you do not act now, days and months will spin ever farther.
Old age approaches—do not say you are still young.
To abandon yourself, to cast yourself away,
Is to willingly become beast of burden.

My words flow like a spring—your nature already understands.
I gather these rustic sayings—truly, they are laughable.

**Answering Questions**

Gates upon gates,
Pine-locked, sealed tight.
I ask the master—
Is he in the west, not bound to the east?
Stay awhile, sir,
The message lingers in the passage.
Tomorrow, magpies’ cries will brim with joy,
And this day, we meet again.

My temples are not yet wild with age,
"Peace and safety"—these two words
Are my family’s creed.
How can I bear this hazy moonlight,
Seeing Chang’e call her maiden?
Double-Bloom, surnamed Dong,
Queen Mother soaring in the void.
Now, good fortune arrives—
Brows unfurrow, a touch of red.

**Answering Questions on Livelihood**

To seek wealth from poverty—
I ask: by what means?
The elder Lü sold grease, Zhang peddled wine,
The Na and Zhuo clans, Qi, Dun, Zhou, and Yang.
Such are these ranks—
Each takes up their strength.
Bustling, teeming,
The marketplace of profit.
As the ancients said:
Craftsmen cannot compare to merchants.

**Answering Chen Nou’s Inquiry on Official Service**

Fish transform into dragons—
The news from Yu’s Gate draws near.

Thunder roars, shaking a hundred leagues,
Now rain is ample, farmers rise to toil.
I ask the village ahead, wrapped in snow’s embrace—
I hear the cuckoo’s urgent, ceaseless call.

Seize the hour, and the Dragon will rejoice,
The Dragon will rejoice—once more, peach and plum bloom anew.
Clouds part, rain clears,
Swallows carry mud in their beaks.
Speaking of wealth and nobility, the scions of kings and lords,
They return from spring’s revels.
Vying in finery, silks and satins, pure and soft,
Not to mention mounds of yellow gold.
Gold fills the hollow—
Fan Li of Tao was once Zhu of Yi, who changed his name and returned to his homeland.
At Qi’s gate, he played the zither, unrecognized in his time,
Looking back, the minister’s seal was but a worn-out tray.
A worn-out tray—
Human life is but a butterfly’s dream, fluttering and fleeting,
I urge you: time is just this brief.
But preserve this square inch of ground,
And entrust it to your descendants as their foundation.
Five, six, seven—these numbers
Mark the finest prospects ahead.

**To the One Commissioning an Inscription**

Though the spirit is upright and not demonic,
In dark, desolate chambers, I sit ashamed of undeserved favor.
Though true talent exists for great use,
I, a rotten tree, am unworthy of carving.
Yet with bundles of silk and scallions, you show respect,
And with a generous verse, you vie for the prize.
A hundred generations will flourish—all thanks to ancestral virtue,
In time, the phoenix will surely sing in the court.

**Thanking Magistrate Lin for Inscribing the Stele**

On the ancient plain, a ruined temple sighs over its mounds,
Its lineage traced back to the Xu clan.
Heroes grow old, as worthless as dust,
Who now understands the classics and their profound meaning?
Though I cannot match Han Yu’s records,
I am glad to write like Liu Zongyuan.
Ashamed I lack five hundred taels of gold for the brush’s reward,
Yet the spirit, in hidden favor, knows me well.

**Farewell to Painter Cai**

A master’s brush brings forth art from nature,
Gu Kaizhi and Yan Liben inherited its transmission.
Though the craftsman’s skill is prized, the brush may seem clumsy,
The pigments deep and rich, the colors all the more vivid.
On the wall, his name endures for ten thousand ages,
Among men, his fame spreads to three thousand guests.
Now, as we part, I am filled with sorrow—
A humble cup of wine, a poem to send him off.

**Four Daoist Masters Manifest at the Altar’s Construction**

Four immortals descend to the luminous altar,
Pity those who see with different eyes.
Clad in gourds, they laugh with open mouths,
Holding bats, they step through wide-open gates.
Their cloud-like hearts lightly come and go,
A thousand-year iron pillar stands, ancient and mottled.
If you ask where I dwell,
I am beyond Penglai’s outer mountains.

**To the Old Almsgatherer of the Heavenly King, Whose Request Went Unfulfilled**

Such contempt is but the work of power’s sway,
Though the spirit descended, it curled up in reluctance.
I know well the barriers between Qin and Yue,
How can there be the steadfast bond of Chen and Lei?
Empty formalities in four-six prose, sealing jars of sauce,
Repeated auspicious divinations waste feasts and efforts.
Cease your weary thoughts of longing for me,
Go instead to the mountains and drink from the stone spring.

**Three Answers on Affairs**

Those who beg for minor blessings exist,
Yet those who exert effort and offer wealth find no cause.
Emperor Wu mocked those who gave themselves to the Buddha,
Deceitful hearts still practice the ways of the Man and Min.
Do not say planting blessings bears no seed—
I fear this fleeting body will turn to dust.
Swift as a galloping steed, human life passes in an instant,
How pitiable—Zhao Meng, ever earnest in his advice.

In stillness, I laugh at the world’s people,
Who know not the causes of past and future lives.
All are but karmic shrines from the Jin of Shi,
Who knew they once belonged to the kingdom of Min?
Wealth and nobility, ten thousand kinds, vanish like dreams,
How many heroes have already turned to dust?
Diligently practice deeds of kindness,
No need for more earnest words.

The spirit’s manifest power lies in man,
Or perhaps not—heaven has no cause.
A single heroic soul swallows the cosmos,
A thousand ancient temples adorn Min and Ou.
The western stele remains unerected, its record left empty,
The inner walls are still stained with dust.
To begin and complete is the work of a noble man,
Why need the transforming brush to urge you further?

**Answering the Liang Clan’s Inquiry on Reburial**

The ox sleeps where Tao Gong divined the grave,
A white crane immortal briefly descends to the mortal realm.
The heart’s sincerity matches the yin earth’s goodness,
How dare I claim heaven’s favor is mine alone?
If indeed the ancestors’ spirits are present,
They will see your descendants rise to high office.
