Alas, the departed flow like this river, and the barrier between light and dark remains. Relying on the merit of this morning audience, we seek to make it a bridge to liberation. May the Emperor’s hearing be moved; may your deeds be recorded, your sins erased, and the people’s sincerity be seen. Extending to the departed, preserving for the living, we pray that all in the underworld may ascend to the shore of the Dao.

Moreover,
The Emperor watches over you—let your heart know no duplicity, as if he stood to your left and right. All people possess constancy, cherishing virtue and honoring parents. We pour forth our crimson sincerity, lifting our voices to the vault of Heaven. We reflect: as children, we ever strive to honor our thoughts—yet the departed are gone like this river, while the living have failed to nurture them. Step by step, we prostrate ourselves, gazing toward Mount Tai to offer our devotion. Thrice we bathe, thrice we burn incense, relying on our revered masters to present our petitions. May the realms of light and dark alike receive the grace of deliverance. In the human realm, may the young grow strong, the old regain their vigor. May the dead be refined, their forms established, their spirits preserved. May all in the underworld attain the fruit of the Dharma.

**A Daughter’s Petition for Her Father**
In the shadowy night’s prison, we lament the nine hells’ depths of suffering. In the dawn’s courtyard, we later behold the five lights’ brilliance. We prostrate ourselves, pouring forth our sincerity, lifting our voices to the pure. We reflect: our father’s flesh has barely cooled, his voice still lingers—how could we, as children, ever forget the Way of serving parents? Fortunate that the Heavenly Worthy has stirred a heart of salvation, responding to cries for aid. Relying on the power of incense and cultivation, we dare express our private gratitude. We pray that the fifty-three years of floating obstacles and hardships may all be erased, that the hundred thousand *qi* of the formless void may be drawn forth. May all sentient beings attain birth and transformation, ascending together to the Great Dao.

Moreover,
The Dao is beyond words—through ten thousand million kalpas, its form defies description. Father, where have you gone? Fifty-three years passed like a fleeting colt. In quiet reflection, who could bear this? We reflect: our father met his end in the dust of this fleeting world. Though his clothing and food were sufficient to ease his brow, and no illness suddenly afflicted him, Heaven is high, Earth distant—who could restore his *po* and summon his *hun*? The moon is cold, frost bitter—who could not weep in sorrow? Relying on this merit, we make it a bridge. Having received deliverance, how could we forget our gratitude? We pray that the Three Officials may swiftly erase his name from the ledgers of sin, uprooting all evil. May the Five Emperors oversee his journey to the Jade Capital, his ascent to the Golden Gates.

Moreover,
Our father’s kindness remains unrepayed—we rely on the merit of deliverance to turn back the Emperor’s wrath. We pray for the stirring of compassion; having received mercy, who would dare forget the source? We reflect: our sins have accumulated upon our person, extending calamity to our father. Though we harbor private grief, we cannot voice it—we can only pray that the Dharma’s form may uphold us. Having bathed our bodies and refined our forms, we have already presented our petition to the Vermilion Mound. Laying bare our livers and gallbladders, we now inscribe these words in jade.

Longing deepens beyond measure, trembling and at a loss.

We humbly pray that ears may hear, sounds may resonate, eyes may see, colors may appear—awakening the mechanism of thought, knowledge, understanding, and enlightenment; that hands may move, feet may dance—attaining the joy of unfettered freedom. May all those in hidden suffering thereby ascend beyond.

Moreover,
As a woman who has taken her path, I once made a plea for his return to peace. The boundless heavens offer no solace—how can I bear this grief of losing my sole support? Bowing low, I pour forth my foolish heart, lifting my gaze to beseech your august presence.

Reflecting, I recall my father’s words were simple, his learning modest. Measured by human affairs, he did no wrong—why then was he forsaken by fate? Six years of confinement left his limbs as though shackled, bringing the twin fiends’ nightmare to his very marrow, or perhaps the Three Corpses’ calamity arose from his torpor. His illness has reached its crisis—who now knows the plan to restore his rest? Here I weep, barely fulfilling the duty of a filial child. Grieving and harboring resentment, I can only lay bare my plea.

Fortunately, in the long kalpas to come, the teachings will endure, so that beneath King Yama’s throne, grievances may be unbound and enmities released. Perhaps through wondrous power, a method to revive life may be offered.

We humbly pray that his marks of excellence may flourish, his karmic bonds from past lives be severed. May all his sins be uprooted, his chronic afflictions and accumulated misfortunes swept away. May he swiftly regain his form and wander the Great Void. May all, in darkness and in light, together attain liberation.

Prostrate, I beseech the August Emperor: though He has not absolved, how could one fail to offer reverence with a humble heart? Without a father, what refuge remains? The youngest daughter’s sorrow is unbearable. Bowing low, I pour forth my sincerity; lifting my gaze, I implore the lofty ear of Heaven.

I recall how my father’s untreatable abscess brought him to death, his flesh not yet cold. Perhaps he appeared in a vision of light, his countenance filled with sorrow—who could cast him aside, unmoved? Who would not plead with deepest urgency? Like Ye Chang, though innocent, he suffered the weight of public judgment; like Zeng Shen, falsely accused of murder, he endured suspicion that could not be dispelled. For three years, grief has weighed upon me; for nine springs, resentment has lingered in the Yellow Springs. Had I not served him with utmost devotion, who would deem his deep wrongs redeemable? I humbly pray that the karma of many lives be pardoned, that he not be bound and pressed into the ranks of ghosts. May the enmities of past ages be released, and from this moment of awakening, may all be aided by the power of the Dao.

Again, prostrate, I reflect: my father’s loss is grievous—how can one endure the sorrow of his death? Though his daughter was wed to a neighbor, she could not fulfill her wish to return and bring him peace. Bowing low, I offer my earnest devotion; lifting my gaze, I disturb the vault of Heaven.

I recall how my father attended to his duties with diligence and managed his household with frugality. Though he had but slightly expanded his fields, he widely extended the intentions of our ancestors. Though his family faced many hardships, he strove to repay the debts of his sons. His kindness remained unrequited, yet my heart holds it deeply. I had hoped that in his later years, he might enjoy good fortune and preserve his life. Yet half his years passed like a bubble’s shadow. After twenty days of illness, the abscess had not yet burst, but his gall had already perished. At the end of four seven-day cycles, his soul might be summoned, but his spirit would not fade. To bow before the Vermilion Mound and cross the threshold of life, one must rely on the Daoist priest to present these words. By the grace of the vast celestial bed, may his past karma be forever absolved, and may he depart the winding paths of the Yellow Springs to awaken to the moment of liberation. Since there is a Heavenly Paradise, may he dwell in the realm of carefree wandering.

Again, prostrate, I lament: my five viscera are torn asunder, yet I must sever my love for my late father. Though a hundred bodies could not repay him, the youngest daughter’s sorrow is unbearable. Bowing low, I pour forth my sincerity; lifting my gaze, I implore the lofty ear of Heaven.

I recall how my father, though his children’s marriages were settled, endured such hardship. His burden never lightened from his shoulders, and illness clung to him like an abscess upon his back. The stars dimmed and fell, breaking my heart like the fleeting lotus of a dream. Days and months passed, and in the blink of an eye, sixty days slipped away. I long to sing the *Summons of the Soul* like Song Yu, yet dare I present the plea of Ti Ying to redeem a crime? I humbly pray that his name be erased from the Black Register, that he may soon receive the Golden Register and White Tablet. May he bow before the Vermilion Mound and be delivered through the fires and waters of the ritual pools. May all who have sunk into suffering together ascend to joy.

**Petition for the Blood Bowl to Deliver the Mother**

Prostrate, I reflect: the Eighty-Four Thousand Kalpas of the Feathered Realm stretch boundless, without shore. The Fifty-Three Buddhas of the Blood Bowl vow to deliver all beings. If one can create such karmic conditions, the merit is inconceivable.

I recall how I, born a woman, once honored my mother’s example. Yet my love for her was like the magpie’s, and I could not repay her as the people of Juqiong did. Forty-nine years have passed—where now is her voice and form? One hundred and six days have flown by like the months and years. How could she not think of me? I must rely on the power of the Buddha to awaken the dream beneath the scholar-tree and enlighten her to past causes, that she may gladly attend the feast of reeds. By this, may the later fruit be perfected, and may she reverently welcome the Three Realms. May all in the ten directions be illuminated, and may the eighteen layers of hell be shattered, each prison opened. Without passing through a hundred million kalpas, may all beings in every kalpa attain deliverance. May her true body be purified, so that no orchid-scented waters are needed to wash her tassels. May her Dharma-eye be opened, so that no golden needle is required to scrape away her film. From this moment onward, may all be made new. May she receive life and nurture, forever free from suffering.

In the court of King Yama, may she indeed receive the grace of Kṣitigarbha Bodhisattva. In the Tuṣita Palace, may she join her palms and listen to the teachings of Lady Māyā.

**Petition for the Husband and Daughter**

Prostrate, I reflect: the Most High cherishes life, yet why was his life not granted its full span? Since ancient times, death has been inevitable—yet who pities this departed soul? Beating the earth, I hear no response; calling to Heaven, I make my plea.

I recall how I, knowing my fate to be meager, never found a proper match. I think of my daughter, her mouth still smelling of milk, her ignorance profound, and my husband, whose topknot of hair had barely been tied in gratitude—why was his illness incurable? After only eight days, he perished. What could be done? I am left with lifelong regret. O soul, where have you gone? Forever you return to the endless night.

With utmost sincerity, I offer this blood-written plea, hoping that my feelings may be considered and my sins redeemed.

Reflecting on the weight of merit and virtue, the transmission of talismans is inscribed in golden registers and white missives. Surely father and son ascend together, their names etched upon the jade chambers and cinnabar terraces. May all good destinies share in this grace.

**Prayer for Husband and Child**
Prostrate, I consider: earth stretches far and heaven endures long, yet none hear the suffering of the wronged. Frost chills and the moon gleams—who knows the sorrow of the widowed? I dare not speak at length, yet fear I overstep again.

I recall how I, born through kalpas of suffering, endured these two losses. The joy of our family’s rise faded like ephemeral blossoms; the hope of a full life shattered like a broken mirror. Since length of days is fated, what can I do? I know not the cause of good or ill that brought me to this extremity. Though living and dead are sundered, by merit and virtue one may yet ascend. Let me but express my love for my child and convey my longing for home. Though his voice and form seem present, I can only imagine them in the empty courtyard. Where has his soul gone? I pray he may be delivered to the Crimson Mansion, that even the hidden and shadowed beings may together return to the boundless Mahāyāna.

**Prayer for Father from Child**
Prostrate, I reflect: the August Heaven above watches over all and has not forsaken me. In serving the dead as the living, a filial child and obedient descendant offers all. Deeply ashamed of this humble petition, I fear it borders on excessive reverence.

I recall how I, fortunate to dwell among human relations, barely upheld our family’s legacy. Knowing the source of a spring’s flow, how could I not strive to repay its origin? Moved by the scene before me, I express my longing and remembrance. Though my forebear’s remains rest in the grave, all was guided by the divine. Seizing this opportunity, I humbly accept this ascension and dare not forget to offer thanks. Though his spirit does not return, may it wander the dim and silent realms. Virtue is hard to repay; with this simple rite of supplication, I look up to the Great Dao and extend blessings to the underworld.

**Prayer for Parents**
Prostrate, I consider: how vast is Heaven above—how dare I not offer my utmost loyalty? Even a child in arms loves its parents—who lacks this filial piety? Ashamed to repeat myself, I have already stated my plea.

I recall how we, in deep reflection, ever bear the difficulty of serving the dead as the living. In our conduct toward others and ourselves, we find no virtue or filial piety to praise. Thus, we turn to the Emperor of the Eastern Peak and Mount Tai, praying that all beings may together return to the Great Dao. May all men and women devote themselves wholly, all be protected and delivered, and all attain longevity. May spirits hear these hidden words and ascend, spared from suffering.

**Prayer for Father and Ancestors**
Prostrate, I reflect: the grace of Heaven and Earth is profound, and I bow in gratitude for their nurturing virtue. The crisp air of frost and dew—who does not feel the sorrow of offering mugwort? Humbly, I pour forth my sincerity, prostrating before the Azure Heavens.

I recall how I, bound by worldly dust, possess awareness of blood and breath. I cherish the toil of my father and grandmother, mindful of the teachings like those of Kaoshu. In life, I served them; in death, I buried them—this may be called filial, yet I strive to offer my utmost sincerity. Rising early and retiring late, I do not shame my origins. How shall I repay them? Last winter, I vowed to hold the Great Yellow Register Fast, performing rites of thanksgiving and expressing reverence through posthumous offerings.

I pray that their yellowed forms be purified, their jade eyes refined, and their true precepts followed in the School of Purity and Light. May the Southern Palace’s flying crow and the Northern Bureau’s spirits ascend, their talismanic commands fulfilled in speech.

**Prayer for Ancestors**
Prostrate, I reflect: human relations are rightly ordered, deepening the heart of filial piety. The Dao of Heaven is constant, and we truly bear its nurturing virtue. I prepare the sacred altar and humbly invoke the true healing.

I recall how we, bound by worldly duties, live as descendants. The merit of ancestors and the virtue of our lineage protect us, their descendants—how fortunate! With sincere hearts and earnest intent, we place our trust in the spirits without doubt. Let me briefly express my plea and perform this special rite. Ashamed of my repeated petitions, I yet hope for a fraction of private grace.

I pray that the Nine Dark Realms open the way, holding the texts of the Yellow Register. May the Eight Radiant Scenes shine forth, and may we behold the golden-purple visage. Further, I beseech blessings upon our descendants and grace upon the living.

**Prayer of the Perfected One Seeking Forgiveness**
I have heard that humans, as the most enlightened of beings, knowing their faults, must correct them. Spirits leave no trace for judgment—how can one call them wrong? Yet why do they act wrongly? It is because they rely on humans to act, and thus cannot escape the flattery of ghosts.

I, your servant, hold a sacred office in the Golden Towers of the Nine Heavens. Since the Kaiyun era, my shrine has received offerings. From the Kaixi era onward, my temple has been honored with imperial decrees. Though I possess the numinous power of grass and trees, I know not the wonder of ascending as a saint or perfected one. Thus, I have observed fasts, upheld precepts, accumulated goodness, and built merit, advancing to the ranks of immortals. I have been entrusted with duties in the unseen realm, performing my tasks with care—how slight my virtue!

Yet I fear that in using medicine to save lives, I may not distinguish the properties of *aconite* and *cinnamon*, *magnolia* and *poria*. In using literature to exhort the world, I may not recognize the errors of *Lu*, the foolish, *Hai*, or *Cheng*. Or in divination, I may fail to deliver the oracle. Or like the wild goose, I may not find rest. I may pray for clear skies, only to bring rain, turning crops to ruin in the summer heat. Or I may pray for rain, only to bring drought, incurring blame from the Wind Earl. Some say good and evil have their retribution—yet why does good not bring good, and evil not evil, like the vanished state of Guo? Or that fortune and misfortune move people—yet why does fortune not bring fortune, and misfortune not misfortune, like the horse of the old man of the frontier?

Some die young, not by fate, misled by shamans’ words into slander. Some live in poverty and hardship, blamed on ghosts’ deceit, unable to rise. Some village festivals involve offerings of meat and wine, violating the precepts. Some communal feasts borrow kitchens, offending the fast. Thus, *chimei* and *wangliang* seize the opportunity to ride their *men* carts, while the sins of *taotie* and those who pray with peach wood have no escape.

In deep anxiety and fear, I tremble uncontrollably. Fortunately, the Most High has sworn a vow of compassion, and the Great Dao provides rites of repentance. Humbly, I stand before the sacred presence and confess my failings.

I humbly beseech the renewal of the old and the proclamation of amnesty for calamities. May past transgressions of reckless words be redeemed, and future diligence in service be demanded, that all may be pacified.

In governance, I dare not stir up trouble, nor seek merit through toil and hardship. In personal conduct, I dare not hold office without earning my keep. To succor the living and deliver the dead, to aid the upright and expel the wicked—I shall exert myself in practice and repay with utmost loyalty.

Moreover, I hope that my person may enter the radiance of the precious pearl, and my every step fulfill the Three Thousand Precepts. Though my virtue lacks the fragrance of millet and rice, may my sacrifices endure for a hundred generations. The devotion of your servant is boundless, earnest to the utmost.

**Thanksgiving for the Bestowal of the Title "Perfected"**

I prostrate myself before the majestic lineage of the Great Dao, to which my heart wholly turns in reverence. From the vast expanse of the Great Luo above, the Three Realms all bear witness. How profound is the Celestial Emperor’s favor, and how unfailing the wisdom of the divine beings!

Reflecting on my joy, which shook the earth, and the grace that descended from heaven—though ranks are apportioned with distinction, I know my sixteen-character title exceeds all bounds. Where rites grow excessive, disorder follows; I deeply fear the full measure of the thousand-two-hundred-part cycle. Yet through broad consultation, all concur it is both fine and fitting.

Thus, I offer the reverence of fasting and purification to express my private gratitude. I humbly pray that in receiving emoluments, I may benefit the people, and that my yang merits be made manifest. Show me the path of illustrious virtue, and bestow further honor upon my yin office.

**Again,**

Heaven’s constancy favors those with virtue; without virtue, one cannot move Heaven. The sage does not claim to know the divine, yet only the divine may enter the sage. With sincerity, I bow and lift my gaze to the azure vault.

I recall that my homeland lies beneath the shade of mulberry and elm, bound in communal kinship. The title "Spiritual Relief" has endured for over four hundred years, and its boundless benevolence has been revealed to all under heaven. The people, content in their sufficiency, cherish it without end. Thus, the favor bestowed upon the master has grown ever greater. Gathering the feathered and robed, I prepare humble offerings, pledge alliance with the Three Realms, and dare not betray the grace of former days. Through the grand Rite of Thanksgiving to the Heavenly Net, I may perhaps express this heartfelt reverence. I further hope for your radiant presence at the ritual seat, that blessings may be conferred upon all people.

**Prayer for the Protection of Crops**

Heaven does not speak, yet its goodness responds—when it is said to rain, rain comes in its season. When people desire and follow, blessings sought arrive in full. With sincerity, I bow and lift my gaze to the azure vault.

I recall that my humble abode shelters beneath the vast turning of the cosmos, like a swallow nesting in a tent’s eaves. Should I ever fail in reverence, I shall surely correct my faults. My plea is not for external gain but only to avert disaster. Since I have already received abundant grace, I still pray for its fulfillment. Having been touched by this favor, how can I ever rest in ease? I shall ever hold the fear and trembling of one who has received much. I beg for your compassion and dare not forget to repay your virtue.

I pray that the young grow strong and the old remain vigorous, that men and women alike may thrive. May the lowlands know no dampness, the heights no drought, and may all be filled with abundance.

**Again,**

The people regard food as heaven; I deeply fear that Heaven’s heart may send down reproach. Even when the Xia prayed for grain from the thousand gods, they still hoped the Emperor’s wrath might be turned away. Below, I lead the people’s sentiments; above, I appeal to Heaven’s hearing.

I recall that we, who look up in service and down in nurture, are many in number. From spring plowing to autumn harvest, the year’s labor nears completion, and we hope for abundant fields and plentiful crops. Yet if pests or minor blights arise, how can we atone through our offerings? Only by removing harm can benefit flourish; only by doing good can auspicious omens descend.

May the wind blow gently, the clouds gather lightly—thus shall the vegetables and salted offerings be presented. May the harvest be reaped in abundance, the grain piled high—thus shall the millet and rice be fittingly offered.

*Xu Xian’s Florilegium, Scroll Ten Concludes*
*Xu Xian’s Florilegium, Scroll Eleven*

**Proclamation for the Establishment of the Luotian Jiao Ritual, Issued by the Golden Portal’s Conferral of Immortal Writs**

From the Three Heavens’ Gate, the Perfected of the Golden Portal’s Palace, charged with the authority of the Heavenly Mechanism’s Secretariat and the office of Wenchang’s Minister of Fortune, whose radiance ascends to the heavens and whose auspicious responses manifest among men:

Now, man is the spirit of the myriad things, his nature endowed with the Five Constants. Fathers, in their kindness, instruct—how could this depend on the carving of wheels? Sons, in their filiality, admonish—how much more do they honor the towering trees of their forebears. Elder and younger brothers, like the *chi* and *xun* flutes, take turns sounding the harmony of the household’s zither. True friendship, rooted in heartfelt exchange, is praised like the clans of Xiao, Zhu, Wang, and Gong. When righteousness runs deep, it is revered like the bonds of Xing, Jin, Ying, and Han. Such is the foundation of moral instruction, the unshakable pillar of human relations.

Let none oppress the weak with strength, nor overwhelm the few with the many. Let none neglect kin for strangers, nor allow the lowly to surpass the noble. When the cap of the superior and the sandals of the inferior remain in their rightful places, when names and ranks are preserved, then the scales of justice will be balanced, and great peace may be hoped for. Then shall culture and renown flourish—could this be mere jade, silk, bells, or drums?

To propagate transformation and uphold the current, one must embody Heaven’s Dao to bring blessings. To observe customs and examine mores, one must apply effort in human affairs. As for the promotion or demotion of the unseen and the manifest, rewards and punishments must be certain. Among those who oversee the city gods and earthly spirits, some may quell disasters of water and fire, some may bring rain and sunshine in due season, some may settle the wild geese in peace, and some may cause the tortoise and milfoil to speak. Where virtue is worthy of praise and merit is recorded, they shall be publicly recommended and duly promoted. But if they are dull-witted and unresponsive, if they fawn like false ghosts, if they cling to corrupt customs and are called licentious, if their foul deeds are notorious yet they refuse to reform—then they have brought calamity upon themselves, and disaster shall befall their persons. This office, having investigated and verified, shall on that very day enact thorough reforms.

Apart from the memorial already submitted to the True Bureau of the Three Realms and the City God, this proclamation is hereby issued for public notice.

**Proclamation for the Jade Palace’s Conferral of the Immortal Tablet and the Grand Offering of the Luo Heaven**

To the Three Heavenly Gates, the Perfected of the Jade Palace, who currently hold office—once appointed as Recorder of the Thunder Bureau’s Mysterious Ministry, once as the Supreme Minister of Longevity in the Southern Culmen—harmonizing the myriad transformations and governing the care of all living beings.

Wind to nourish the people, rain to nourish the people, regulating cold and heat, yin and yang in balanced order. Clothing to clothe us, food to feed us, ensuring mulberry and hemp, grain and millet bring abundance and prosperity. When forms are knit, men chant reverence, women chant devotion. When spirits descend, the moon becomes the essence, the sun the soul. Each has its office, each its appointed duty, fortunate to attain liberation through karmic conditions. Embodying the Dao and virtue to shed radiant light, enabling Yang Zhen to transmit purity and integrity through his lineage. Thus, Bi must surely regain his rank as lord and marquis—what wisdom may grasp, folly cannot, for what is granted by man is also granted by Heaven.

As for prayers for rain or sunshine, one may move unseen through heaven and earth. The Minister of Agriculture oversees the harvest, the Director of Silks manages hemp and flax—each holds their rightful post. The Market Official regulates commerce, the Artisan Inspector refines materials—none neglect their duties. Yet if one follows deviant paths, failing to reflect on the root, allowing demons and malevolence to arise and inflict harm, this is the bitter brew you have brewed yourself—though you may repent, can it be undone?

Whether the spirits of *chimei* and *wangliang* run rampant, spreading poison, or the monstrous *taotie* and *taowu* perpetuate treachery and deceit, this office shall never conceal or tolerate such acts. By law, we shall act, ensuring that in the yang realm, the guilty face the punishment of dismemberment, and in the yin realm, their forms are shattered to pieces. We shall sever them with the wind-knife, strike them with the thunder-axe—what man may kill, Heaven shall condemn. Whose fault is this, if not your own?

Moreover, this office has sworn to aid the upright and eradicate the perverse, to succor the living and deliver the dead. Now, filial sons and obedient grandsons, having turned their hearts toward the Dao, first enable their countless ancestors—from parents to great-great-grandparents—to ascend according to their vows. And for the lonely souls and stagnant spirits of the Five Ancient Paths and Three Wretched Realms, though long sunk in suffering, all shall partake in the grace of Heaven.

Apart from the memorial already submitted to the True Bureau of the Three Realms and the City God, this proclamation is hereby issued for public notice.

---

**Proclamation of the Bestowal of Honors and Transmission to the Upper Mystery Gate**

We humbly consider: the Nine Qi abide within the Cavernous Mystery, guarded by the divine. The boundless multitudes of the Ten Directions arrive upon the void. They attend our august presence, bearing witness to the Great Dao. Incense rises in clouds, thick and fragrant; lamps intermingle with the radiance of stars and moon. From afar, we govern the Nine Heavens, solemnly conducting the Three Offerings. With utmost reverence, we serve the Celestial Emperor—how awe-inspiring!

The divine, vast and responsive, fulfill their duties. When merit is complete and virtue perfected, all receive deliverance. The realm is at peace, the people prosperous, rejoicing in great harmony. Let all officials under your charge attend diligently to their posts.

We invoke the numinous waters of the Most High, recite the jade texts of the Primordial Beginning. The Five Emperors attend the Perfected, the viscera grow cool and clear. The nation is at ease, the people cultivate simplicity, and all demons withdraw in silence. Ghostly spirits vanish, malevolence subsides. Let this be made known—may all take heed.

Thus, the proclamation is issued.

---

**Notice to the Patron of the Retreat, Drafted on Behalf of the High Priest**

It is said: there is life, and there is death—life and death follow separate paths. Yet to serve the dead as one serves the living is to unite death and life as one. As descendants, how can one lack humaneness? Moreover, this body, this form, was not born among the mulberry trees, nor were your father and mother ever cast upon the waters. If you understand this, how can you not reflect?

Staging colorful performances in the hall—today’s so-called "old vegetables"—how countless they are! Weeping in grief by the roadside—today’s so-called "Gao Yu"—are there none among them? One should sip bean broth and drink water, fulfilling the joy of filial devotion. Why must one, like a dried fish tied with rope, harbor endless regret?

Better a cup of spring wine than slaughtering oxen for an extravagant burial. Though you may grieve that the wind and trees never cease, rejoice that heaven and man have this meeting. By observing the retreat and upholding the precepts thus, one may revive the dead and restore the bones—those who accept their lot.

If ancestors cherish their grandsons with love, how can descendants lack the awareness to reciprocate? Uncles are praised—why wait for the day of gathering pearls? Nephews are not fools—why must they ride in procession on horseback to care for their orphans? Ever mindful of their elder brothers’ lineage, they shelter their descendants; ever pitying their younger brothers’ early deaths, they nurture their offspring.

Whether in the joy of wedding cups or the sorrow of a broken lute, whether in the oaths of shaved heads or the delight of raised brows, as husband and wife, predestined by past lives—through life and death, true affection is revealed. These are the greatest of human bonds, upon which the teachings of propriety depend.

Though still in infancy, none fail to love their parents. We rely upon the Supreme Emperor, who possesses the virtue of cherishing life. Vastly opening the path of deliverance, both the living and the dead benefit. Now, in observance of the Zhongyuan Festival’s continuation of the Xuandu tradition, we reverently join the assembly in establishing the karmic conditions for universal deliverance.

Let good men and good women each awaken their virtuous hearts. Whether near or far, all shall be naturally unobstructed. The merit is extraordinary—merit beyond thought, inconceivable.

We look up to the retreat’s patron, who deigns to attend the ritual seat. Though you have turned toward the Dao, you also possess a filial and obedient heart. The earth has no prisons, yet Heaven has its halls—separated by no more than a foot’s distance. Good deeds accumulate like mountains, evil runs deep as the sea—even a slight deviation may lead to vast disparity.

Those who do not understand me—what do you seek? You must seek within yourself. I, so-and-so, a humble student of the Heavenly Abode, presumptuously preside over the mysteries of the Dao. Were it not for the presence of the sacred and the drawing of lots, how dare I affix my name and title? Though I lack the capacity to deliver others, I only follow the Dao in my conduct.

Should any in this altar fail in loyalty, the luminous statutes shall impose their penalties. Without addition or omission, let none act with deceitful intent—only with a sincere heart and upright will may merit be fulfilled and virtue perfected.

Thus, thus.
Know this, know this.

Amidst the myriad generations of ancestors, names inscribed in the registers of immortals, each spanning five kalpas of opportune convergence. The six relations and nine clans, names recorded in the realm of longevity, where eight thousand years pass as spring and autumn. This is hereby respectfully reported for your collective understanding.

Thus, the decree is issued.
**Decree to the Faithful Assembled at the Altar**

Now, as the season of early autumn arrives and the Zhongyuan Festival draws near, when the White Emperor governs the turning of time and the Earth Official conducts his reckoning, Buddhists on this day offer the Ullambana basin, while Daoists establish the Mystic Capital Offering. Though our gates differ and our households diverge, the principle remains one while the rites vary—each striving to transform the people and perfect customs, each holding filial devotion and ancestral reverence in mind. At such a time, how can one not reflect?

Vast is the heavens, its qi pure and clear.
The grasses [sway] softly, the people hushed and still.
Gazing at the bright moon over Dongting, why wear sorrow like a sash?
Singing of the crisp wind at Red Cliff, why weep and wail thus?

I turn to my shadow and sigh,
my heart grieved, my breath heavy.
This body, this hair and skin—bequeathed by my father and mother—how shall I repay them?
The fields and homes, the clothing and food of my parents—accumulated virtue of my ancestors—how shall I repay them?

My ancestor, the Grand Mentor Xie, his admonitions—what day dare I forget?
My uncle, Minister Xue, his parted wealth—his kindness was not small.
The line *"Ponds grow grass"*—this is why Xie of Linchuan dreamed of his brother.
The passage *"Offering water-plants"* in the *Spring and Autumn*—this is why Han Changli longed for his elder brother.

Yet when the younger sister of Yuanwai passed, why begrudge the carved stone?
When the younger sister of Huang the Grand Historian died, why resent the shattered wall?
Striking the basin and singing, how hard to sever the love of Zhuangzi’s garden.
Swearing vows upon the boat, how could she break the pact of Gong Jiang’s alliance?

What heart is this? What heart indeed?
They are human, and I too am human.
Pondering this, what can one say?

The long night stretches on—deceitfully performing the rites of summoning the soul and calling back the spirit.
The Great Lofty Realm is distant—yet fortunately, there exists the method to revive the dead and restore the body.

You, such people, should vow to cultivate all good fruits and plant this noble cause. Moreover, as the kalpa nears its end, twelve myriad souls shall be chosen to fill the ranks of immortals. Now, with merits and virtues fulfilled, I wish to deliver over seven hundred names—men and women of this assembly—so their souls may ascend to the Realm of Non-Action, their wheel of rebirth unbroken.

Yet if any harbor deceit within while feigning purity without, forsaking righteousness and forgetting gratitude, corrupting customs and undermining teachings, scorning the Three Treasures and neglecting the Five Constants—how could such people escape karmic retribution?

Some, once poor and later wealthy, now shamefully conceal their past.
Others, once rich and later poor, now resent their forebears.
As for their ancestors, their clans, their fathers, their mothers, their uncles, their elder brothers, their wives, their husbands, their younger sisters, their sisters-in-law—abandoning mourning rites, treating kin like strangers, as if deaf and blind. Worse still, they share no more care than horses and oxen, utterly devoid of sorrow, content to herd with deer and swine. Thus, the Yang and Ruo’ao clans, left with no lineage to rely upon—do they not feel shame before the sons of horse-doctors and field laborers?

Only I open the gate of expedient means, establishing for you this assembly of equality. Without obstruction, let us together plant the seeds of good affinity.

Let those already born and yet unborn never forsake compassion, adapting to circumstances and responding to transformations. Let those with kin and without, with equal loving-kindness, turn strangers into family, together attending the feast, ascending as one into the Dharma-realm. May your hearts open to awakening, free from greed, anger, and delusion. Let the Dao harmonize with nature, guiding all—from ghostly realms and human paths—back to the immortal Way. These words are no falsehood; do not treat them as mere commonplace.

**Notice of Appointment to the Hall of Masters**
With reverence serve the Dao, ever mindful of the solemnity of fasting, purification, and ablution. Let those who lead by virtue be your teachers, each fulfilling their duties in conduct and demeanor. Vast and ever-present, majestic in spirit—though unseen and unheard, beyond naming or discussion. Be earnest in devotion, respectful in chaos, ever vigilant in your actions. According to the golden registers and their jade precepts, the system of ranks and duties comprises six disciplines. The three grades of examination you know well; let sincerity be your measure. As one asks the servant about plowing, consult the maidservant on matters of knowledge. No bribes shall suffice—only those of proven ability share the carriage, only the worthy the same boat. Attend to your duties, uphold your stations.

This is addressed to you all—such is your own cultivation. You must refine your bearing to inspire admiration. Those who heed this shall enjoy blessings; those who hear it shall ascend [to higher realms]. Let unity of virtue prevail; even a single lapse cannot be tolerated. Should any transgression arise, the responsibility for correction rests with all—three admonitions followed by three bows. Reverently observe the prohibitions, receive the registers, and restore the images. Sever wrongdoing, continue merit—neither reward too lightly nor punish too severely. Do not fret over undeserved gains, nor claim unearned success. As the saying goes: "Without forethought, it begins with you, and returns to you." Merit and virtue are of utmost weight—here they begin, here they end.

Let all who hear this take heed and comply in body and spirit.

**Notice**
The establishment of teachings and statutes serves as guidance for all. In this world, offices and duties rely on those of trusted heart. Though we appoint from among the known, true talent must first be tested. Gather all without discrimination—cast aside neither a copper coin nor an inch of iron, nor neglect the worthy. Banish the wicked as one wields a sharp plowshare; recognize the dullness of a leaden blade. The six offices and six duties demand unity of heart and purpose. Let no past grudges linger, nor old missteps be repeated. Who is without fault? Yet those who correct their errors are true. The state upholds justice, even as it allows redemption. Ever vigilant, ever consistent—depart with announcement, return with report, as dutiful as to one’s parents. Report by merit, test by word, for the proper order of ruler and subject is thus.

**Proclamation for the Yellow Register Universal Salvation Retreat**
Vast and unfathomable—such is the sage, such is the divine. Radiant and manifest, ever present to left and right. Uprightness is their virtue; salvation their work. Looking up, we revere the boundless compassion of the Most High; looking down, we heed the decrees of our office. With utmost solemnity, we undertake this great endeavor as witness.

Long have we gazed upon the walls of Confucius’ temple; now we borrow the mat of Chen Fan. The spirits declare their return, wishing to enter the gate of cultivation. The retreat orders them; special mats are prepared. Let their faces bear joy, not the distress of the oppressed. Summon them, and they come—let them advance in succession, filling the hall to its narrowest corners. Welcome the familiar as old friends, the new as if long known—this is a once-in-a-millennium gathering. Let the distant come, the near rejoice; why distinguish between this realm and that? Some discuss the seasons’ cold and warmth, others the bonds of kinship—laughter fills the hall, hands join in song. What are ghosts, if not kin? All are human here.

Spread the mat and share the meal; coarse grain is no shame. If private devotions to the Buddha arise, let them be conveyed. Should one not know where to dwell, is that not neglect of their abode? Choose guests with courtesy, deeply grateful for the love of the host, the Sage of the Eastern Way. Those souls selected for salvation shall together journey to the Western Kingdom of Vaisravana.

**Notice for the Altar Wardens**
Prostrate before the three tiers, symbolizing the Three Powers, ascending and descending with solemnity. The eight directions correspond to the eight trigrams, arranged with strict precision. Stars form the weft, constellations the warp; heaven has its gates, earth its doors. Crimson cords stretch seventy-two zhang, charting the lunar cycles’ flow. Eighty-one poles are gathered, uniting the transformations of yang’s mysteries. The characters on the banner are as large as bushels; the floating canopy’s structure rises like a chamber. Light pierces the gates of heaven; incense strikes the pillars, filling the air with fragrance. Thunder Lords drive back intruders; Wind Lords wield their mighty fans. The gods, silent and steadfast, uphold the tilting; the Emperor, vast and majestic, presides above.

Should any demonic forces—be they *kuí*, *xū*, *yù*, or *kuáng*, or the malevolent *chīmèi wǎngliǎng*—dare to act with deceit or spread filth, they shall be met with the unyielding law of the Mysterious and unforgiven. Like shadows caught, voices seized—let them be snared as in a net that leaves no rabbit free. Sever their forms, cut off their heads as an axe levels a wasp’s waist. Exterminate their leaders, root out their followers without mercy. Let the frost’s night be as still as withered orchids; let no wildfire rage to consume both thistle and lingzhi. Transform sand and gravel into southern gold, turn tiles and stones into harmonious jade. In the center of the sacred scriptures’ target, let no beast remain unscathed.

Yet how could the gods of the altar, who pluck cicadas with ease, deem their arts too meager? Those with office and duty, with the weight of words, must hold no bias, no private grudges. Guard the Dao with severity, manifest the altar’s clarity. Let ghosts be ominous, immortals auspicious—so it is said of the desire-realm’s flight through the void. Let heaven be free of haze, earth of dust, as we raise the hidden words of the Great Brahma.

In conducting the retreat and practicing the Dao, boundless merit is accrued. Saving the living, delivering the dead—this is the great affinity. Let all faithful men and women attending the assembly offer utmost reverence and devotion. Should any willfully violate the retreat’s precepts or transgress its prohibitions, they bring calamity upon themselves.

How could one dismiss the talismans as mere ornamental writings hung upon the wall? These are the very duties of the altar’s overseers. Thus it is, thus it is. Reverent! Reverent indeed!

**Proclamation to the Generals and Officers of the Altar:**

We have heard that the merit of courtly worship, traced to its origin, began with Attendant Kang of the Capital Bureau. Through such acts, one repays the toil and kindness of parents, thereby attaining the Dao—such filial piety and righteousness defy full expression. Later generations established rites for the living and the dead based on this precedent, and when examining merits, none surpass this as the foremost principle.

Moreover, every person possesses understanding: who is without parents, without uncles and aunts, without siblings, without spouse, without children or nephews? Who does not hold their own body and limbs in highest regard? The living all wish to fulfill their natural lifespan without harm, while the dead all long to return to life, for dry bones to rise as flesh. Without this merit, none can be delivered.

Today, on behalf of [Name] and the assembly, we conclude the retreat. Previously, on the journey of courtly worship, we prepared the yellow registers and petition slips to deliver both the living and the dead. The night-long fast and rite were held to cultivate and exalt this merit. The details are manifold, and without you, Generals and Officers, who would bear witness? Without you, who would transmit the talismans and circulate the decrees? Without you, who would present the memorials and convey the petitions? Without you, who would summon the wandering souls and restore the scattered spirits?

You, Generals and Masters, stationed before the altar—if they serve as the charioteer, then you are the left; if you serve as the charioteer, then they are the right. Equal in merit, one in body, each must attend to your duties with reverence. Boast not of your goodness, nor claim undue labor. Let the living each fulfill their lives, ascending together to the realm of benevolence and longevity; let the dead each find their rest, wandering together in the heavens of bliss. Both living and dead receive this grace, and all will raise their hands to their brows, declaring: “This is the merit of you, Generals and Officers!”

On the day your merit is recognized, your names shall be inscribed in the Upper Clarity. These are the secret words of the Most High—there is no falsehood in them.

Should there be those who are inauspicious, disobedient, reckless, irreverent, unrighteous, treacherous, or deceitful—who willfully violate these precepts, defiling virtue until their transgressions reach Heaven—you shall, in accordance with the Mysterious Law, utterly eradicate them without exception.

Thus, exhort yourselves, Generals and Officers! Be diligent and steadfast!

**Proclamation.** Again...

The Palace hereby establishes the Zhongyuan Mysterious Capital Retreat and Offering, and all commissioned generals and officers must conduct themselves with lowered voices and solemn reverence, adhering strictly to the precepts. All proclamations to be issued shall proceed as follows.

Prostrate, we acknowledge that the Dao abides in the Great Void, formless and ineffable. Yet when summoned from one’s own primordial destiny, it arrives without delay. The Perfected Ones enforce prohibitions—without proper ritual, it will not act. The talismans of the Numinous Treasure universally proclaim the Nine Heavens. The Primordial Beginning’s *Red Writ* unfolds its myriad regulations. It spreads the five qi of the Five Dragon Divine Waters, coalescing them into numinous power. Wind gathers and smoke summons the Four Spirit Fire-Horses from the four directions, arriving like fine rain or dense mist.

City Gods, attend in reverence: I command the Demon Kings to guard my chariot, raising the Great Unity’s mighty standard and brandishing spears at the Fiery Gate. Let the *xi* and *wangliang* lurk in concealment; let the *chimei*, *shaokui*, and *xuyu* be swept away, and the mad *xuyu* subdued. The maidens of Wu Gorge shall find no place to cast their covetous gazes. The followers of Chiyou shall ultimately be reduced to withered bones.

To all assembled: heed this proclamation, and do not invite defilement to entangle you in guilt. Should any act thus, none shall escape destruction. Reflect thrice—though repentance may come, what use is it then? Uphold the Mysterious Law, and consign the wicked to the crooked and perverse. Sever heads and dismember forms, all to be boiled like dragons. Seize by sound and shadow—none shall slip through the net. Those who hear this tremble in terror; those who see it quake in submission.

To break one’s word is to forsake trust; guard the Dao with severity. Let the Ten Mysteries unfurl and the Eight Effulgences shine forth. May the Three Realms assemble in unison, and the host of Perfected Ones oversee the deliverance. Strike left to the left, strike right to the right. You shall serve as Upright Generals of Dharma Protection. Neither lag behind nor rush ahead—you shall act as Impartial Envoys of Retreat Supervision. Attend to your duties with reverence, unite your hearts as one, and remain steadfast from beginning to end. Such merit is of the highest order, sufficient above to offer to the Seven Ancestral Shrines, and below to deliver the multitudes from the Three Wretched Realms.

We further pray that Heaven, attaining the One, may be purified, and Earth, attaining the One, may be pacified. In harmony with the divine, the demonic path shall ever be inauspicious, while the immortal path shall ever be auspicious. Thus shall I endure long—heed these commands without error or deviation.

**Proclamation.** Again...

Beneath the Three Heavenly Gates, the appointed officers of the Golden and Jade Towers of the Perfected Ones’ Palaces, having received the reverent investiture of jade seals and been favored with the Master’s grace, now stand in awe. All eyes behold them with astonishment, for their solitary traces seldom stir such wonder. Though they attend to their duties with diligence, their merit must be weighed by the sincerity of their own hearts. Ten eyes gaze upon them, ten hands point toward them—none can escape the impartial judgment of right and wrong, good and evil.

To walk alone is difficult; to clap with a single hand is impossible. They must rely upon the strategies of civil and martial deployment. Next, to all the generals and officials arrayed around them—radiant to left and right, resplendent in might and spirit—who emerge from being and vanish into non-being, who appear before the eyes only to suddenly linger behind, whose past glory is recorded in the annals of history and whose literary brilliance is still heard today: now, their heroic souls exhaust their utmost loyalty, upon which Heaven and Earth depend for their very standing.

When the talisman arrives, receive its command in silence. Craftsmen adhere to compass and square; physicians distinguish ginseng from poria. First take the long, then the short. Servants till the fields; maidservants tend the hearth. They offer their strength; these offer their labor. Each cherishes the joy of the mulberry’s shade—how could it be said that their sustenance is without benefit? In rank, they respond to all things like the resonance of the Yellow Bell, every movement producing sound. They dispel entanglements like a blade newly sharpened on the whetstone, every use free from obstruction.

Let none treat one another as distant as Chu and Yue, for they must rely upon the loyalty of heart and mind like Zhang Liang and Chen Ping. Exterminate demonic forces, and the city gods shall heed their commands. Rescue and deliver all beings, embodying the Supreme’s love for life. Should any hidden spirits dare violate these prohibitions, they shall be judged according to the statutes and publicly subjected to the prescribed punishments.

Those beyond the bounds are called traitors, and the *Yu Dian* provides for their governance by the Ministry of Justice. Those who defy and resist shall face the executioner’s blade as recorded in the *Book of Zhou*. Guard the village gates, pacify the altars of soil and grain, and ensure that every person attains their desires, fulfilling the wish for ease and contentment, that every household enjoys abundance and prosperity.

You shall not boast of your virtues or display your toils—it is I who shall assess merit and dispense rewards. Offer the finest grains of ritual with reverence; do not bring shame upon the divine. Before the incense and flame, first pray for the longevity of the Son of Heaven. Each shall attend to their duties and not neglect their posts.

**Proclamation.** Again...

Let cinnabar tablets, cinnabar, green lingzhi, and red arrowroot be gathered by physicians. Great beams, small rafters, refined eaves, and short ladders rely entirely on the craft of carpenters. Having refined what is selected, each shall be measured according to its material.

Proclamation to the Generals of the Upright Altar and the Inspectors of Ritual Offerings: harmonize like sounds, seek out like kinds. Let your forms be truly upright, your voices truly clear. Serve as guardians to the Four Numinous Beasts, repelling enemies from a thousand *li*, striking terror into the hearts of foxes and jackals. Display the might of wolves and tigers blocking the path, leaving cunning hares at their wits’ end. Embody the power of tigers and leopards in the mountains—be virtuous and invincible.

When employed, act; when matters arise, command—abide by my prohibitions. Should any subordinate official dare to summon malevolent spirits, recklessly indulge in madness, or willfully defile, they shall be cut down and eradicated like foxes and rats clinging to a banner’s tassels. Some shall be released, some seized, like chicks within a cage—judged with weight or lightness, with impartial right and wrong. Do not let sacred fungi mingle with rotten weeds, nor auspicious birds with common feathers.

The celestial statutes are most precise—report any violations at once. Now seal the mud pellet, spread the ritual mat, and reverently welcome the imperial carriage in its stately procession, receiving the Emperor’s graceful arrival. Some shall raise the great banner before and plant the tiger standard behind; others shall wield the white tassel in the right hand and brandish the yellow battle-axe in the left.

Let none transgress these commands; align your hearts as one, and in your efforts, go beyond the measure. When merit is weighed for reward, no stinting shall there be. When virtue and achievement are complete, your names shall be inscribed among the stellar generals of the Southern Palace. Longevity, prosperity, and peace shall answer in part the hopes of the eastern host.

By this proclamation, let all be made known.

**Notice.**

Moreover, in the administration of ordinances: Those who cultivate hidden virtue shall surely receive manifest reward; those who practice open virtue shall surely attain illustrious renown. Now the Nine Heavens’ Two Perfected Ones have ascended to the ranks of immortals, their offices arrayed among the Nine Heavens. Can the virtue they hold in human hearts be measured? You, officers and generals, may see them with your eyes, hear them with your ears, and engage them in speech—no doubt remains, no uncertainty lingers. Therefore, you must unite your hearts and pool your strength to assist in the completion of this work.

Should there be treacherous and deceitful men, ghosts or goblins, inauspicious and perverse, insolent and irreverent, defiling themselves with filth—know that I shall execute Heaven’s punishment through you. The lightly guilty shall be seized and brought before the Celestial Pivot; the heavily guilty shall be dismembered and beheaded. Thus shall Heaven attain unity and clarity, Earth attain unity and tranquility, and the gods attain unity and numinosity.

When the day of merit arrives, I shall memorialize the Celestial Court on your behalf, that you may be appointed to the scattered offices of the immortal bureaucracy—how could you remain mere banners and drums for mortal eyes and ears? Yet if the left fails to strike on the left, or the right fails to strike on the right, and you disobey these commands, the fault shall be yours alone. The Perfected Ones, as celestial generals, shall have cause to reproach you—this is the hundred-times-tempered steel, daily sharpened to slice a thousand oxen, sheathed and stored, yet not my shame.

Let all take heed to these words.

**Notice.**

**Notice to the Five Marshals of the Boundless Realms—Wei, Zhao, and Diao**

Behold: bright moon and white dew, the passing of time—who among us is without the sorrow of withered grasses and desolate winds? Let all beneath the August Heaven and Earth, all gods above and below, offer reverent sincerity, upright and balanced, and by this karmic connection, cultivate merit and virtue.

Now, within this Daoist sanctuary, rites are prepared in purity. First, we address the pressing concern of those departed souls who have fallen into the three wretched paths, the bitter destinies, with no means of escape—how then may they ascend? Though officials exist to oversee such matters, do they bear no burden of reproach?

We summon you, warriors fierce as *pixiu*, to render service swift as hawks and hounds. On my decree, come forth at once. Soar through the void, transform at will, emerge from nothingness into being, and traverse every shadowed yin realm, every place of gathering. There, you shall summon the souls to be delivered at this assembly, escorting them here. Proclaim to them:

The primal might of tigers and leopards cannot long endure in confinement—thus sings the *Zhao Te* of Huainan’s Xiao Mountain.
The belly of the river fish is no place for lingering delay—thus intones the *Diao Hun* of the Changsha Grand Tradition.

Whose ode shall we raise to praise the banners of the learned?
Who shall sing the verses that revive the bleached bones of Xianyang upon the battlefield?
Who shall loose the bound spirits of Changping, their nineteen years of woolen garments now worn to naught?
Did Su Wu truly receive the wild goose’s message? A thousand years later, the marble pillar still stands.
Was Ding Lingwei truly a crane? Yet Zhang Han’s longing for water shield and perch stirred his heart, just as Tao Yuanming grieved for his pines and chrysanthemums.

O souls! O souls! Return to your former abodes.
The hour is now! The hour is now! Do not let it slip away.
From this moment onward, all shall be made new.
Look up and receive compassion; look down and find pardon.

The golden rooster is set before the gate, its augury already declared.
The courier stars streak past—no wolfish hearts, no wild ambitions remain.
The stubborn and unenlightened shall be transformed, their debts repaid like oxen at the butcher’s block.
The jailers shall follow the writs of judgment.
Though bound in fetters, they are not guilty.
Yet let the sight of their tattered robes shame the people—hold them no longer; release them all.

The Dharma’s gate offers expedient means—who would scheme like a thief to cross its threshold?
The immortal path is free and unbounded—who would feign the folly of Zu and Yu to deceive their way?
The world is vast—where else would these souls wander?
The scholar Niu lost his way in delusion—was Bo Ji truly a seductive phantom?
The old man drunk on the road—did Fu Qi dare stir mischief?

Let none be trapped in the schemes of Chen and Cai, nor hindered by the obstructions of Zang and Cang.
Should any petty ghosts act recklessly, you are to obey the Mysterious Law, seize them, and bring them forth.

Go forth and heed these commands.
When the day of merit comes, your names shall be inscribed in Heaven. You shall serve as attendants across the Three Realms, welcomed by the Five Emperors—this is no humble station.
The Nine Dark Realms shall turn back their souls; the Seven Generations shall emerge from the long night. Boundless deliverance shall follow.

This decree must be obeyed without delay or disrespect.

**Notice.**
*Concluding the Address to the Assembly for the Blood Pool*

In reverence, we acknowledge the vastness of the Feathered Realm’s pool, spanning eighty-four thousand leagues—boundless, shoreless, without edge or bank. At the Assembly of Universal Light, the fifty-three buddhas, embodiments of great sagacity, great vows, great compassion, and great mercy, have expansively revealed the Dharma-gate of delivering the living and ferrying the dead. How could we fail to cultivate the karmic causes of past and future?

We humbly reflect: born as women in the world of Jambudvīpa, during the time of bearing sons and raising daughters, how could we avoid the defilements that foul heaven and earth? Unrepentant, we tread upon thin ice. Truly, we fear that on the day of Gengshen, our transgressions may be recklessly tallied. Beginning in the year of Guisi, we seek to absolve our faults—whether to repay a mother’s kindness, cultivate our own blessings, aspire to be born in the Pure Land, or prepare a bridge for others. Fortunate that the measure of merit has come full circle, we pray that both the living and the dead may benefit.

On the third day of the third lunar month, golden figures appeared in the auspicious meanders of the Qu River. Like the Buddha Śākyamuni, who manifested upon Vulture Peak to expound the Dharma and vow to deliver all beings, we now earnestly invite four or five virtuous masters to establish a night-long Daoist ritual. Before the Mirror of Karma, in the halls of King Yama, we wholeheartedly prostrate to the Master Who Saves from Suffering. Within the Alms Bowl Hermitage, beside the table of Rājagṛha, we bow in homage to the bodhisattva Maudgalyāyana.

We make offerings to the worthy sages of the ten directions, welcome the eight compassionate lords, and dedicate the merit to the assembly of holy beings. We traverse the heavens, earth, and watery underworld, clearing paths for all. Summoning the departed souls, we guide them to recognize the white clouds. With mantras, we offer sweet dew and food; with lotus lamps, we illuminate the ninety-nine layers of hell’s darkness; with orchid-scented waters, we wash clean the sixty-six joints, bringing cool purity.

We separately prepare a nighttime feast for King Yama of the Scorched Face, universally bestow alms upon the asuras and all beings, and broadly extend relief to the orphaned souls, the stagnant spirits, and those trapped in the five sufferings and three evil destinies. May all sentient beings attain perfect enlightenment. After receiving the precepts, they shall be granted certificates as proof.

We pray that the departed may wander the Western Regions, beholding Avalokiteśvara in the lapis lazuli gardens; that those who preemptively cultivate merit may live as steadfastly as Mount Tai, attending the Queen Mother’s banquet by the Kunming Pool. May the sun of the Buddha shine ever brighter, bringing joy to humans and gods.

**Notice of the Establishment of the Blood Pool Ritual Altar**

In reverence, we call forth wandering souls and restore lost spirits—especially on the third day of the third lunar month, the anniversary of the first crossing at Sizhou. For the benefit of beings, the thousand-handed, thousand-eyed Avalokiteśvara manifests in boundless freedom, vowing to become the incarnation of the Savior from Suffering and the King of the Scorched Face.

All disciples of the buddhas, if you wish to know the causes of past lives, sit again, and I shall tell you: swallowing charcoal and blackening the body is folly, deceit. Offering uncut jade is sufficient—why go further? Those who gnashed their teeth to pieces did so to establish martial merit; those who swallowed their tongues to death did so in shame over state affairs. Though their names are recorded in history, indelible, they dared not harm the hair and skin bestowed by their parents.

By the Miluo River, the waters stretch dark and vast; on Mount Shouyang, the winds blow cold and fierce. Eight thousand *li* to Chaozhou, cast adrift in lands of miasmic mists and poisonous vapors. Nineteen years upon the sea, stifled beyond the northern snows and desert sands—buried grievances unvoiced, yet their integrity stood tall. The Earl of Lu appeared in a dream to King Zhao of Yan; the descendants of Panhu were named by Emperor Gaoxin. Lord Wei transformed into a crane; the virtuous sister became a cat. Though they possessed human hearts, they were not spared the destinies of beasts.

For me, girded with gold and robed in purple—what need had I for a bamboo bowl and gourd ladle, unable to bear such hardship? To hasten as a menial, to serve as another’s slave; to pore over classics and histories, to act as a scholar’s scribe—what difference lies between noble and base?

Traveling at dusk through rain, at dawn through clouds, I recall the dreams of Wu Mountain. Greasing my chariot, feeding my horse, my soul follows the wanderings of Pan Valley. Upon the One-Sandal Ridge, lightly I tread—where do I go? Before the Jade Coffin Hall, proudly I stand—where do I turn? Buried alone by tigers, devoured by serpents, like the ghost of Ruo’ao, without refuge. The son of Yuling did not die—who was he, enduring suffering age after age? Now, dead at last, all forms are empty.

Eighty-four thousand kinds of creatures, wriggling and fluttering; three hundred sixty classes of beings, feathered, furred, scaled, and shelled. I have heard of the myna bird reciting the name of Amitābha Buddha; of the clam transformed into Avalokiteśvara. Sparrows gather to chant sutras; geese listen to the Dharma.

Tonight, the patrons of this ritual, led by so-and-so, have established a night-long Daoist ritual in the Hall of Remaining Blessings within the Eastern Palace of Spiritual Salvation. We separately prepare a measure of food and widely open the Dharma-gate. Whether sentient or insentient, what distinction is there between early and late awakening? Since you have come to attend, why ask further?

The noble one contends with none; the humane bears no lingering grudge, nor gathers factions to cast out factions. Let none exalt themselves or compare self and other. Each erects their own barriers—free from anger, free from delusion, free from preference, free from aversion. The ghost of kinship looms large, while other ghosts shrink endlessly, locked in the snail-and-clam struggle of mutual devouring. You dwell north of the sea, I south—like horses and cattle, our winds never meet. Yet across the three thousand great worlds, where are the seventy-two ranks of men?

Excellent, excellent. Thus it is, thus it is.

Fields know no starving poor; the Buddha holds compassion. In murky waters, wash your feet; in clear, wash your cap-strings. Whether in purity or progress—both are fitting. Cold becomes clothing, hunger becomes food. Is this not joy? Is this not delight?

Leap beyond the Gate of Ghosts, recognize the path to heaven. Do not let precepts and disciplines instead plunge you into confusion. Receive, uphold, and practice—you should heed this well.

Thus the proclamation is posted.

**Proclamation at the Dismissal of the Ritual Assembly**

The ghosts of Lü return in words—knowing death, yet not knowing life. The fast unifies them. Do not fear scarcity, but fear falling short. Now we prepare a single mat, summoning the ten directions. They come, drifting and carefree, vast and present as if here. Whether in thought or thoughtlessness, neither thought nor thoughtlessness—neither non-thought nor non-thoughtlessness.

If sentient, if insentient.
If neither sentient nor insentient.
If old, if young—each in due order of honor.
If noble, if base—make no distinction of high or low.

I extend compassion, wishing you to generate a heart of purity and five-colored clouds of radiance.
By night, you must listen to the Dharma spoken by the Feathered Forest Guards of the Southern Palace.
The eight-flavored celestial banquet—you must hear the scriptures expounded by the Queen Mother of the Western Realm.

Why wait to be clad in creeping fig, only to be torn asunder?
Why wait to feast on chrysanthemum blossoms, only to wither away?

Take refuge in the Treasure of the Dao, escape the ghostly bureaus.
All equally receive the kindness of deliverance, forever severed from the suffering of sinking and drowning.

A single sprinkle of sweet dew—
already abundant, already rich, already sufficient, already moistening.
Ascend together to the Azure Heaven of Origins,
without prejudice, without insistence, without rigidity, without self.

**Meritorious Dharma Utterance for Universal Deliverance**

We gaze upon Mount Hu, praying for longevity as to the Southern Mountains.
We pay homage at the Peak Court, bowing before the Purple Palace to inscribe our names.
On the third day of the third month, the sky is bright and the qi clear—
the six senses and six perceptions are cleansed of dust and defilement.

Plant good fruits upon the assembly of the Spirit Mountain,
give birth to golden lotuses in the pond of the Feathered Realm.
Those who hold the grasses to summon souls and offer prayers for the departed—
may they behold the visage of Māyā.
With flowers tucked behind their ears, those beauties—
may they wish to journey to the Land of Bliss.

Let this serve as a bridge and a permanent tally.
Thus the document is issued.

**Proclamation to the Departed Souls of the Southern and Northern Temples**

Where men are bright, ghosts are dark—yet when ghosts return, their spiritual souls descend to earth while their ethereal souls ascend to heaven, and heaven may be reached. Following the rites and canons, we broadly establish vegetarian feasts, seeking to forge karmic bonds with all beings. We wish to borrow but a square inch of ground, for who would say the waters of a well or valley cannot sustain a school of fish? Fortunately, the mountains of pine and cypress suffice to shelter flocks of crows—none shall be excluded.

We make this special provision in advance: holding orchid grasses to dispel misfortune, scattering flowers in the grove to exalt the Great Brahmā. Lamps and candles glow in the moonlight of night, while banners and pennants dance in the morning breeze. If we would aid these souls—how could we not proceed by this Way?

Let them eat of rice and wear of brocade—shall they not know warmth and fullness? Let them taste our horses and anoint our chariots, that their wandering wishes may be fulfilled. How could we bear even one to weep in solitude? Thus, though they come from a thousand *li* away, the distance is not far. The lairs of dragons and serpents shall directly ferry their boats; the passes of tigers and leopards shall unlock their chains.

Let each rest in their place—what doubt remains? Though the dreams of skulls are not the spring and autumn of the human world, if liver and gall are lost, it is as if one’s own kin have become strangers. All are neighbors, all are kin—what fences exist? What divisions of "you" and "me"?

We issue this proclamation first—let there be no clamor. May the Earth-Lord Bright King temporarily serve as a bodhisattva exhorting virtue. Above, the August Heaven; below, the Empress Earth—truly they hear the words of filial sons and obedient grandsons. In the southern fire marsh and northern water pool, they shall naturally attain the wondrous refinement of form and essence. Without distinction of noble or base, all shall be enabled to ascend.

Thus the proclamation is posted.

**Proclamation to Wandering Spirits**

We declare: the life you were given was never truly yours—from this moment, let death return to life.

The Dao is fundamentally non-action; henceforth, turn your hearts toward the Way. Seizing the moment of awakening, the karmic conditions of past lives, the merit defies all measure.

The immortals know the greatest joy. To the east lie the caverns of Cloudy Mount and the chambers of Green Mound; to the west, the celestial city of Tianyong and the terraces of Dark Garden; to the south, the mountains of Qingdu and the palaces of Flowing Fire; to the north, the courts of Supreme Mystery and the halls of Purple Void. From present to past, from ancient times to now, countless millennia have passed. Though this realm and that may differ in strength, they lie hundreds of millions of miles apart—ordinary folk cannot reach them, the vulgar cannot hear of them.

This is the land where the great sages of the ten directions wander and rest, the gathering place of the lofty perfected ones of the Three Realms. Its governance is effortless, its people pure and simple. Clothing and food arise naturally; forms and faces are upright and proper. There is no noble or base, no rich or poor, no right or wrong, no self or other, no taking or giving, no love or hatred.

That which is born is born, yet never ends. That which takes form takes form, yet never exists.

Through countless generations it endures, through kalpas unending it remains. The sun and moon shine without cease, wind and rain follow their proper measure. The four seasons proceed in constant harmony, the five grains ever ripen in abundance. Rare blossoms and sweet herbs bear names each distinct; numinous mushrooms and auspicious grasses defy all description of their forms. Those patterned in five colors are not few, nor are the nine-hued phoenixes with three feet. The tortoise called *Ben* lives yet a hundred years; jade hounds bay at night, golden cockerels herald the dawn. Cloud-wreathed mountains stretch boundless, rivers flow vast and deep. Though set within this very cosmos, it presents a scene of another kind—dim and distant, beyond all bounds, a primal heaven not yet divided, swirling in primordial chaos.

One might call oneself a man of Fuxi’s age, yet never suspect one has lived to the kalpa’s end. To be born here, to grow here—could one not rejoice? Could one not delight? You, departed spirits, who would inquire of that other world: I have heard the wondrous Dharma, and it may serve as a bridge to this place, a means to bind karmic ties with all beings. Thus we establish the Mysterious Capital Offering, the ritual precinct solemn and still in the boundless void. We open the summons to the Celestial Physicians, the divine tigers perform the lads’ dance. We enact the Hunyuan writ, repent toward the directions, receive precepts and transmit talismans. We make universal offerings to the Ten Kings, brightly repaying the Three Realms. We proclaim the image-texts, intone the scriptures. Lamp-light pierces the realm of darkness; the unhindered feast of measured food is universally bestowed. Bathed in the Flower Pool, we wash away the floating veils of past years. We open the roads of the underworld, and now discern today’s journey. Turn not back—step firmly forward. Five *zhang* below the earth, a pearl the size of a millet grain hangs in the empty mystery; nine gates open in the heavens, leading straight through the Great Floating Li Soil to the First Azure. The words above are true—hear them well.

Thus the proclamation is posted.
Again...

Holding fast the Golden Deity’s seasonal rites, the Lesser Howl enacts its authority. The Middle Prime is the time for reviewing the registers of life; the seventh day is the assembly for celebrating birth. According to the seasonal customs of Jing and Chu, this legacy endures—stirring the hearts of all beings as wind and landscape shift. Leaves fall in the courtyard: we sigh at the fleeting passage of days and months. The dew’s chill sobers the air: we lament our forms and shadows, mourning ourselves. What has gone cannot return; what has passed cannot be pursued. Were we to compose a "Summons of the Soul" in the manner of Song Yu, we fear the soul might not come when called. Were we to emulate Jia Yi’s "Lament for the Dead," our sighs would be in vain, for none would understand us.

Now, wishing to inquire into the methods of life, we first enter the Assembly of Universal Deliverance. The Most High possesses the scriptural hymns for rescuing the suffering and delivering the departed; the *Lingbao* holds the rites for reviving the dead and restoring the living. These are the essential ford for transcending the world—here one may seize the moment and awaken to the Dao.

At the outset, what is called "life"? What is called "death"? Yet at its ultimate, there is neither death nor life.

Now, the patron of this fast, [Name], has conceived this aspiration and wishes to open the gate of expedient means. If you spirits possess awareness, you may tread the path of return. At the sight of Mount Xian’s weeping cliffs, your souls shall ascend here. The rightful community is restored, and no soul shall fail to descend. The feast of this fast transcends the dusty world: to the east lies the Wilds of No Purchase; to the west, the Land of Silence; to the south, the Courts of Kunlun; to the north, the Palace of Primordial Mystery. Yet all these lie but within the space of a single thought—go straight, and you reach the First Azure Heaven. Coming and going freely, unobstructed and unbounded, countless bodies gather, entering a millet-grain pearl suspended in the void. We summon the Five Directional Lads, who hold lotus blossoms to guide the way.

Let this admonition be heard and known.

Thus the proclamation is posted.

After the court’s dismissal, the notice is posted below the realm.

Li, the Daoist officer, burns the memorial tablet—this is the occasion for Lady Zhang’s release from her cup. When the Merit Officer recites the scriptures, it becomes the path for none to transcend.

Records of events and transmissions of the numinous resound through past and present.

Alas, you who dwell in the six dark passes of the underworld and the three wretched destinies of suffering! Through endless nights, you have not glimpsed the light of sun or moon; through kalpas of many lives, how could you know the misty radiance beyond the world?

Tonight, at this very hour, come hasten to the Dharma assembly. To the left and right, compose yourselves. Though there are distinctions of rank, there is no division of noble and base. From Yue in the south to the Hu in the north—why separate you as "us" and "them"? From dawn to dusk, what difference is there between new ghosts and old? Since we now share the bond of those who meet as friends by the roadside, there is no need to follow banners and divide into factions like cattle.

I have true words—you should listen closely. Those who gather ferns for food—how do they compare to those who feast from the celestial kitchens? Those who stitch lotus leaves for clothing—how do they compare to those robed in cloud-silk? Though Su the Academician possessed talent, he still suffered hunger and cold. How could the aspirations of Wang the Top Graduate not lie in warmth and sustenance?

Now, take this single drop of pure soul-water and transform it into the gate of sweet dew for the great thousandfold world. The Floating Li Soil lies directly before the First Azure Heaven, from the realm of form to the formless realm.
