Mount Tai is but the Eastern Marchmount’s palace—enter the Small Heavenly Gate to reach the Great Heavenly Gate. Do not stray onto the path of delusion; together, return to the Great Dao.

Alas, you lonely souls—alive, you drift like floating clouds; dead, you rest like fading shadows. Why then cling to the burdens of mouth and body? Is it not because your hun and po have yet to find their home, and so such thoughts arise?

One sip of jade nectar stirs a hundred emotions; when the dark frost is pounded away, the cloud maiden appears. I ponder the intent of this verse—could it be that rich flavors fail to satisfy the mouth? Woven from mist and purple silk, the robe brings only toil without joy, the face left unadorned. I savor the meaning here—could it be that light warmth fails to comfort the body?

The affairs of celestial immortals I dare not discuss. As for the spirit way, it cannot but rely on mortals to act: offerings of grain must be made, silks and cloth must fill the sacrificial pits. Yet are these grain and silk truly what the spirits depend upon?

Thus Confucius said: *"Eating coarse rice, drinking plain water, resting one’s arm as a pillow—joy lies therein."* Zilu said: *"I wish for carriages and horses, light furs, to share them with friends until they wear out, without regret."* Mencius said: *"With five mu of land, plant mulberry—those of fifty may wear silk."* Again he said: *"With a hundred mu of fields, do not rob them of their seasons—an eight-member household may know no hunger."* These words of the great sages, inscribed in books, were set forth for humankind.

The starving man beneath the mulberry tree wished to become a warrior. The affection for the coarse robe nearly cost the master his life. Straw sandals and hempen cloth—how frugal! Lotus garments and chrysanthemum meals—how pure! For clothing and food are what all people depend upon; not a single day can they be lacking.

How bitter it is—endless sands without resentment, yet dim and desolate. Cast among blades and arrows, heaven and earth grow sorrowful. Bones become the soil of a foreign land; souls become ghosts of a neighboring realm. With no one to offer mourning or sacrifice, what is to be done?

Duke Cheng of Wei dreamed of Kang Shu, who said: *"They vie to take my offerings."* The ghosts of Ruo’ao, if not fed by sacrifices, will their fate not soon perish? And consider the mantis seizing the cicada, the hound Han Lu snatching the swift hare, the chicken pecking at insects, the hawk striking at mice and sparrows—all rush for food, harboring no malice. The golden-robed lord, the black-clad master, the loom weaving new brocade, the powder passed down from the Qin Terrace—though called unknowing, each still seeks honor and nobility.

The bee’s flower, the fly’s buzzing pipe, the mosquito’s drone, the louse’s gnawing, the ant’s devotion to the pot, the moth’s flight into the flame—born from womb, egg, moisture, or transformation, all cling to life. There is no other reason: each values its own nature and life. Since you know your nature and life, why not turn your hearts toward the Dao?

Tonight, eat my dharma-food, wear my dharma-robes, heed my dharma-words. Let all lonely souls within the three thousand great thousandfold dharma-realms—whether with masters or without, with thought or without, with form or without, of the ten kinds—abandon all craving and attachment, cast aside all doubt and suspicion, dissolve the distinctions of self and other. Thus, thus, all shall be delivered, bound straight for the land of rebirth.

Roam south to the wilds of Wangmai, rest north in the silent lands of Moyi, wander west into the dark realms of Xijia, pass east through the primordial radiance of Hongmeng. Above, no heaven; below, no earth—carefree and at ease, is this not joy?

Let this proclamation be known to all. Thus posted.

**Proclamation for the Establishment of the Ullambana Assembly to the Lower Realms**

Descendants of Lü give birth to sons, and sons in turn beget descendants—how dare they forget the nurturing grace of generation upon generation? Men become ghosts, and ghosts again become men, yet they cannot escape the suffering of endless cycles of rebirth. Of all meritorious deeds, none surpasses this.

In this world of three thousand realms, there are patrons—so-and-so and others—who, on this auspicious day of the Earth Official’s Great Amnesty, reverently establish the Assembly of the Heavenly Worthy in the Mystic Capital. Since they earnestly honor the departed and cherish the distant, how could they fail to extend this kindness to others? They summon all ten kinds of lonely souls to deliver them from every suffering and hardship. Though the cold are clothed and the hungry fed, why do they remain as fleeting as foam, as transient as rest? Why do they still come?

Let each abide in their place. Do not rely on strength to bully the weak, nor raise the armies of Jin and Chu. Do not harbor grudges to seek revenge, nor nurse the hatreds of Wu and Yue. Do not treat fellow passengers as enemies, nor wield spears within your own home. Do not let two tigers fight where the terrain cannot sustain them, nor imitate insects that devour one another and bring ruin upon themselves. Rather than clinging to "you are you and I am I," why not break down the fences between us all?

When one speaks of form as non-form and emptiness as non-emptiness, the other shore is seen to be utterly free of wind and waves. Only filial children can bestow blessings upon all, and only the benevolent do not shrink from such offerings. Though this begins with us, how can we bear to leave you behind? In the realm of darkness, a single somersault will lift you to rise upon the Terrace of Fragrant Mist. Recognize the hundred-step stairway—step firmly upon it.

Follow the Mystic Precepts and do not stray onto the path of delusion.

**Proclamation to the Lower Realms**

For the living, food that pleases the mouth may be fine millet or coarse fodder; garments that adorn the body may be humble hemp or noble fox fur. None can do without these. But for ghosts—how can they partake?

Alas, you foolish and bewildered beings, drifting through the four modes of birth! The six paths hear no sound, and without sound, there are no mouths—though food exists, how can you eat it? They see no form, and without form, there are no bodies—though clothing exists, how can you wear it? Why then do you suffer the torments of sinking and drowning? It all arises from the mind of craving and attachment.

When the Duke of Jin took the voice of an ox, it was because sound implies a mouth, and a mouth seeks food. When Master Sheng transformed into a pig, it was because form implies a body, and a body clings to clothing. Pressed by hunger and cold, how can one attend to propriety and righteousness?

Now that you have come here, what will you do? Do not wield a dull hammer to strike a sharp awl. Each person has their shortcomings—do not weary of old fists or sate yourself on poison. What do the noble contend over? Do not let Han Lu and Dong Guo dogs exhaust one another before you, nor let the yellow sparrow and mantis devour each other behind you.

Only when one is like Lu Kang and Yang Hu, who reconciled the enmity between Wu and Jin, or like You Yu and Zi□, who turned Hu and Yue into brothers, can you cast aside grudges. Why not stop your carriage, share a mat of grass, and walk together? Walk together, but let the elder go first.

Let those who come, come; let those who go, go—each to their place of rebirth. I extend the kindness of warmth and fullness; you dispel the state of freezing and starvation. The divine lamps shine like the sun, their light witnessed by all in heaven. The Dharma-water rains down like a shower, its moisture universally bestowed upon the void. Cultivate these merits to build this bridge.

Take each other by the hand and ascend the jade steps—whether to advance or linger, whether to hasten or delay. Inscribe your names in the Golden Register—who will be first, and who will follow? For now, act as the King of Equality and attend the Unobstructed Assembly.

Let this proclamation be issued without clamor.

Thus posted.

Amidst the boundless expanse of layered gates, in the endless night’s profound obscurity, before recounting the thousandfold vastness, let us first speak of the six realms’ boundless expanse. The Four Formless Realms remain formless, yet their emptiness is but delusion. The Three Realms’ sentient beings never escape illusion’s transformations. The oranges’ playful elephants—are they free from deceit’s guile? The lotus’s lingering rings—can they sever the longing of affection’s thoughts?

Across all dharma-realms, those called celestial immortals still possess human hearts—how much more so the spirits of the Dao. Ancient sites in mountains and rivers, sacred altars and spirit shrines: when bells resound and apes cry, the plain temple is revered above the Southern Marchmount. The stone engraved with the iron horse’s dream—did it not fulfill the "Yellow Ox’s" prophecy? Though called divine powers, they still crave blood offerings.

Among those with eyes and ears, who willingly lends their sight and hearing? Among those with mouths and bodies, who willingly endures hunger and cold? Do they not take each step in thought, free from desire? Those who returned jade to their lords—where is that jade now? Those who died presenting their kingdoms—how foolish were those kingdoms! The horse of Chaoyang would not advance, though eight thousand *li* lay ahead. The paper of the sea would not nurture, though nineteen years passed in reckoning. Spears were not yet rested when spears were lost; oars were not yet struck when oars vanished.

Though souls and spirits do not rot like deadwood, fame and merit are but empty blossoms. Some care not for wealth and rank, some shut their ears to right and wrong—rinsing in streams, resting on stones, angling for the moon, plowing the clouds. The elixir crucible remains, yet its furnace smoke has turned to ash. The meditation cell endures, yet its skeletal image has crumbled to dust.

Then there are the nine schools—physicians and diviners—and the hundred arts—actors and entertainers: those who mock dwarfs escape not Confucius’ condemnation; those who take sorcerers as omens avoid not Duke Mu’s folly. Risking life on perilous poles and leaping swords, gambling existence on danger. Divining with turtle shells and casting tiles—why not heed their own omens? Scheming with traps and snares—why rely on force? Competing for profit with brush and coin—how does it compare to boats and carts?

Though born alike, who knows how death differs? Some face battle and sacrifice themselves for their country: tongues may sever, yet eyes still glare; entrails may be devoured, yet bodies do not yield. The flames on Yan Mountain blaze fiercely—not for one snake alone. The waters of Miluo River surge boundlessly—not to stand apart from the crowd. The crow, unaware of fleeing, met its minced fate here. Without distinguishing suspicion, this scale led to its boiling. Those who imitated the bear’s roar—had their plans exhausted. Those who captured snakes—few remained.

Wealth harms the self; boasting of gold’s abundance is vain. Beauty injures the body; the sorrow of moth-eyebrows is futile. All flows on thus—what words remain? Alas, you multitudes, failing to find your place of rebirth, why enter the realm of death without guilt?

As for those who gape awaiting food—shame on their bloated bellies. Those naked and bare—deeply ashamed of their tattered quilted robes. Mouths shrunken—worse than a crow’s peck. Necks elongated—nearly like a crane’s form. The women of Xia Mountain often awaken as apes. The villagers of Fu Village—how did they transform into tigers? Some born from wombs, eggs, moisture, or transformation; some clad in scales, armor, or feathers. Though lacking human hearts, they possess hearts turned toward the Dao—these are the ghostly and beastly realms.

All you named and nameless kinds, mastered and masterless souls—why have you come? There is reason. Do not let the low surpass the noble, the distant surpass the close. Do not let the strong bully the weak, the many oppress the solitary. Rather, like fish moistening one another with foam, do not imitate beasts that fight when cornered.

The Southern and Northern Seas—are their customs truly so different? The people of Qin and Yue—are their hearts not equally sorrowful? When punishments are discussed, even strangers meet as new friends. Act now as the King of Equality and attend the Universal Salvation Assembly. To be clothed and fed—is this not joy? To be joyful—is this not delight? To bathe virtue and cleanse the body—is this not purity? To advance—is this not progress? To ascend the Dao’s shore, to climb the immortal steps—these are no empty words. Each should listen carefully.

Thus posted.

**Notice for Wandering Souls**

What makes humans spiritually superior to all things? Knowing heaven and earth’s nurturing power, they must also feel the sorrow of frost and dew. How does creation bring forth all beings? Though it shows no favor in sun and moon’s illumination, can it lack the thunder’s wrathful might?

Today, the Daoist Liang, in reverence, recalls his father’s labor in building the house, cherishes his grandfather’s sharing of sweetness, his grandmother’s nurturing kindness yet unrepayed, his great-uncle’s exhorting words not forgotten. Recently, at the White Water Plain, he consulted the Green Crow divination to arrange burial rites, expressing filial devotion and pledging posthumous cultivation to bestow blessings. Unforeseen, this day has come—fearing to betray that earlier vow, he respectfully chooses this season of orange and tangerine, offering simple sacrifices of grain and fruit.

He respectfully invites the officiating officials to array solemn dignity, first purifying the sacred altar, then summoning the underworld. As for the rebellious dogs, stubborn and fierce, harboring treacherous schemes, embodying inauspicious omens and malevolent hearts—those who stealthily spy on lofty walls like rats, or presumptuously peer at grand tunnels like mantises, unaware of departure or approach, as foolish as dull steeds—let the multitude’s wrath brook no tolerance. The divine might blazes forth; relying on the Mysterious Law, they are delivered to the Demon-Quelling Star.

As for the pitiable wandering souls, the forsaken lingering spirits—having come to this assembly, what will you do in this hall? Form joyful bonds; do not harbor notions of self and other. Past enmities—let them all be resolved. Today’s heartfelt words—let them be briefly exchanged. Whether once old friends or neighbors bound by trust, whether decaying with orchids or enduring like metal and stone—use this to plan for yourselves; I will not betray your hopes.

Hungry ones—provide them food; why remain gaunt and self-aware?
Cold ones—clothe them; let none stand naked at my side.
With the waters of Canglang, wash your feet.
With the purest of springs, cleanse your hearts.
May you awaken and ascend, while the myriad beings, lost in delusion, stumble and fall.
Step upon the jade stairs, where clouds rise in towering peaks, and gaze afar at the radiance of the golden palaces.
The immortal source stretches boundless—is not the journey forward a joy?
By this decree, let all take heed.

Moreover, the forms of yang and the statutes of yin—originally no different in law, for they stem from the same source.
Heaven and hell—how far apart are they? The difference lies not beyond the span of a heart.
Daoist schools speak of their existence;
the discourse of the noble declares their absence.
Today, the female disciple of the Dao, so-and-so, grieving for the Old Lord so-and-so, whose life and death follow their appointed time—no untended carbuncle goes unhealed—yet on the dark paths of the netherworld, she remains troubled by idle words.
Without the aid of incense and cultivation, how can liberation be attained?
Thus, she invokes the Supervisor of the Fast, the Valiant General of the Straight Price, the hounds and falcons, bidding them trace the path and point the way.
Let malevolent foxes and wicked crows take flight and soar far away; let the moon’s clarity dispel the lingering miasma.
Above, let it reach the Three Realms; below, let it descend to the Nine Springs.
All imprisoned souls—look up and obey this decree of amnesty.
Demand their bonds from the Ox-Head jailers; let no venomous harm arise, as from a wolf’s wild heart.
The punishments of grinding pestles, boiling cauldrons, and scorching fires—what code prescribes them?
The tortures of fire beds and iron registers—what scripture records them?
Kneeling, they find no ground to voice their grievances; crying out, they see no path to heaven.
Let all be released without delay; none shall be detained.
Let lingering spirits, forsaken souls, the beings of the Four Births and Six Paths—all gather at this Dharma assembly to forge good karma together.
Do not let the lowly envy the noble, nor the strong bully the weak.
Do not divide self from other, for how can I alone escape?
Do not let north remain north and south remain south, never to meet.
Let joy abound—those near rejoice, those far draw close.
Come with light hearts: the first call, the last respond.
Shoulder to shoulder, they laugh and speak; hand in hand, they sing and chant.
Let each arise in compassion—this is called equality.
Wash your cap-strings in purity or your feet in filth—who can speak of the world as a muddy mire?
Feed the hungry, clothe the cold—was the vow of a lifetime ever merely for warmth and sustenance?
Having returned to the Great Dao, one may inquire of the art of living.
Emerging from the gates of the dead, you cross into my realm.
The time is now—do not let it slip; how can you fail to seize this moment of celestial celebration?
O souls, go forth—this path leads to the discourse of the Dao’s emptiness and formlessness.

Moreover—alas!—death is but a return.
To return is to return to the Dao.
The Dao takes formlessness as its essence.
Dim and silent, vast and obscure—I cannot force a name upon the departed.
Thirty-seven springs and autumns—what stirred the longing to return, only to halt here?
Tracing the cause, there is reason indeed.
Recently, a woman’s illness did not abate; perhaps swayed by reckless, deluded words, her heart grew troubled, her mind confused.
Bewildered, as if in dream or drunkenness, like scorching winds spreading poison, like barbarian mists burying all in dust.
She turned back, yet unexpectedly, the twin demons of illness struck at her vital essence.
With the woman’s untimely death, her grief and lament deepened the abyss—thus, no remedy could prevail.
The spirit realm offered no response; beyond Yellow Mound Mountain, her destination remained unknown.
Darkness gave way to light in the blink of five days.
Day after day, month after month—ever more distant.
Without the power of the Dao, who would guide her delusion and lead her to this return?
Frost chills the moon, a lone crow cries mournfully—at this moment, in this sorrow, what will you become?
Though endowed with the vigor of heroes, though your heart be as wood or stone, how can you remain unmoved by the sighs of orphaned children and widowed wives?
You must awaken compassion, bear witness to devotion, guard and shield, and never indulge in wayward paths that breed defilement.
Let the departed soul return to the Dao—no greater merit exists.
As for the paths of heaven, humanity, spirits, the Dao, hell, ghosts, and beasts—desolate and distant, bound to the Dao—
seen, they have no form; heard, they make no sound.
Yet why must you cling to thoughts of greed, anger, and delusion, to the worldly pursuits of the mortal realm?
Having come here, what will you do?
Clothe yourselves in these robes—no more cries of cold.

Feed, and you shall be fed—no more wails of hunger.
From this moment forth, may you awaken to understanding.
The East cannot be relied upon; the South cannot be your abode.
The West’s shifting sands, the North’s flying snow—none can be dwelt in long.
How can you not turn your heart toward the Dao?

May you, the departed spirits, together ascend the shore of the Dao.
Even the joy of wind-dancing in the rain-altars cannot surpass this.
Know this.

Thus, the proclamation is posted.

Moreover:
Life and death are the constant of day and night, stretching from ancient past to present without end.
Ghosts and spirits follow the Dao of yin and yang, sighing at the boundless cycle of beginning and end.
Yet from a single thought of good or evil arises the divergence of suffering and bliss along two paths.
Deluded in mind, ignorant of principle, they chase shadows and lose the true.
Fortunate to encounter this sagely age, where rites of universal deliverance are established,
where the profound teachings of the Dao are expounded,
where reverence for ancestors is displayed,
and where the benevolence of aiding and saving is magnified.
Such great virtue as this is rarely met.

You wandering souls, adrift in the hidden traces of the netherworld,
lingering in the long night—
though your sorrows are myriad and vast,
know that the Six Paths stretch boundlessly before you.
Those who cling to delusion are ensnared by things and burdened by them.
Those entangled in attachments bear the weight of form upon form.
Sunk in the dark and desolate paths, cast into realms of desolation and chill,
some wail beneath the stars and moon,
some howl amid wind and rain.
Unheard by those who mourn and offer sacrifices,
unclaimed even in burial—
at the ends of the earth, to whom can you turn for refuge?
Beneath the slanting sun over fragrant grasses, you long in vain for home.
To speak of this is to feel deep sorrow and compassion.

Yet by the golden register rites performed in the light,
the Supreme Lord opens the cinnabar mausoleum of the Dao,
revealing the wondrous Dharma of rebirth for you,
bestowing the good cause of deliverance from death.
Cleanse your thoughts, purify your hearts,
and all shall heed the precepts of the Nine Perfected.
Warm your robes, fill your bellies,
and together escape the afflictions of the Five Sufferings.
Forever attain freedom, with no more entanglements.

Thus, the proclamation is posted.
*End of Scroll Eleven of Xu Xian’s Literary Works*

**Scroll Twelve of Xu Xian’s Literary Works**

**Memorial for the Restoration of Lingji Palace**
We observe that the construction of this palace has proceeded for six years. Recently, the eastern pond has dried up entirely, no longer offering the joy of divine and mortal communion. The roof lacks over ten thousand tiles. Those near and far who seek the spirits’ favor have voiced no small distress—surely none lack the heart to give generously.

We recall that building a house takes three years, yet still invites the criticism of passersby. A mountain built to nine *ren* may yet be undone by a single basket’s shortfall. Our Perfected One is both enlightened and efficacious. Let all of like mind remain steadfast to the end as at the beginning. Though this palace nears completion, the eastern pond’s desiccation gives cause for concern, and the western inscription shames us with thoughts of leaking roofs.

All around, in the ten directions, are benefactors. Above, within three feet, divine presences watch over us. Do not say that sweet wine is not offered in thatched halls—after libations, perhaps cassia wood may yet mend the medicine chest. The sum still lacking is precisely what is needed. Thus, we make our plea.

To count grains of rice before cooking betrays the narrowness of our own vision. To donate a million in wealth reveals the heart of a true elder. Since this is a plea for divine favor, why not give with joy? Let none hesitate in their generosity.

**Memorial for the Construction of the Divine Kitchen**
Long has the stone statue of Xu stood as a folk shrine—what is this if not a thousand years of virtuous governance? When Magistrate Chen wished to oversee the local altar, he sought to etch his name for ten thousand generations. His intent was not in the butchery of offerings but in the solemnity of sacrifice.

The new temple stands grand, a majestic sight in the land. Yet the old kitchen, exposed to wind and rain, totters on four crumbling walls. Though rise and fall follow destiny, creation lies in human hands. Once, we built by the roadside; now, we raise beams with purpose. Through the counsel of the worthy, the divine kitchen is complete.

How splendid! How magnificent! Soon, its grandeur will be fully revealed. Here, the distant may drink; here, they may feast. Thus, the temple’s majesty shall be restored.

**Memorial for the Construction of the Hall of Remnant Blessings**
We recall that the five aggregates are empty—who perceives the three thousand great worlds? All buddhas arise from the work of the mind. To accumulate goodness is to build the Hall of Remnant Blessings, exhorting others to do the same. This blessed land is a pure realm indeed.

We have seen that in the first year of the Xinglong era, this luminous pavilion was raised, yet it lacked the Zhongtong paper currency. Those who gave willingly found the Buddha’s witness. In this life, we forge karmic bonds for this life—even a single coin carries its own merit.

Axes fell trees, saws shape them; tiles must be broken, walls painted. Through diligent effort, we await the day bricks are polished into mirrors. With this humble memorial, we knock upon the gates of the noble.

**Memorial for the Construction of the Hall of Remnant Blessings, Requesting Monks as Fundraisers**
We recall the thousand hundred million transformation bodies, each dust-mote realm attaining buddhahood. In the ten directions, every land is a bodhimandala. Do not dismiss what is seen as weightier than what is heard—one must act here to achieve there. All beings may be liberated, yet one must not forget one’s native soil.

Our great master was once the monk of Ao Peak. The Hall of Remnant Blessings has long held the incense of Hu Rock—whether form or formless, it meets the conditions for enlightenment. Through exhortation and alms-seeking, we have gained the aid of celestial musicians.

Having already hung lamps and tempered the arm, why shrink from cutting flesh or offering the body? Let us repair the eastern wall and western wall, lest wind and rain intrude. The scale shall be grand, the renewal complete. From beginning to end, it shall be a boundless field of merit.

Henceforth, let this karmic bond be fulfilled.

**Memorial for the Construction of the Hall of Remnant Blessings**
We recall the alms-seeking across the ten directions—from the southern continent to the northern, years of cultivation. From the eastern land to the western, all are seeds of the Buddha. Do not bark at the old bhikṣu for finding it hard to beg—know that resolve makes deeds easy.

The elder scatters gold, known as the great *āraṇya*. The Hall of Accumulated Goodness and the Hall of Remnant Blessings are the bodhimandala of Dingguang Yuan. We must establish present and future causes and effects. From this point onward, renewal is our aim.

Vows as deep as cranes’ crowns and reeds’ knees. Beams like phoenixes, eaves like halos—how grand the scale! Thirty thousand loads of earth, great the expense. Thousands of donors, vast the merit.

Many have cast forth their treasures, bursting the bag of illusions. No longer shaken by wind and rain, even dragons and devas praise this work. With this humble memorial, we knock upon the gates of the noble, awakening the bodhi-mind—great compassion, great joy, great giving. Countless are the marks of virtue and fortune, boundless and immeasurable.

From beginning to end, may it be completed. With raised hands and lifted brows, we shall gaze upon it in reverence.

**Memorial for the Restoration of Ciji Palace**
We recall the stored essence of the Bohai Sea, manifesting the thirty-two marks of Avalokiteśvara Bodhisattva. In Tongcheng, the incense of Ciji has burned for over two hundred years. Wherever it is established, the name remains the same.

This hall, now ruined and fallen—is its restoration in earth and timber difficult or easy? We look to the thousand benefactors for witness. Let not the mendicant’s vows be in vain; even a single tile or beam’s aid is welcome.

Fortunately, there is no division between this and that realm—let us forge bonds with ten thousand people. Meeting from a thousand *li*, let all give with joy, and it shall be completed this very day.

Phoenixes soar, halos fly—how grand the scale shall be! Cocks crow, dogs bark—may the teachings spread far and wide. This is the transformation of black cloth into silk, the return of auspicious omens once lost.

Through this offering, may it serve as a bridge across the sea of suffering. The merit is beyond measure, the karmic fruit beyond thought. Gold scattered upon the earth—we have heard of Sudatta’s path to buddhahood. Incense burned to heaven—may we emulate the blessings Hua Feng offered to the sage.

With this humble memorial, we knock upon the gates of the noble.

**Memorial for the Reconstruction of Wenxing Hermitage, Including Fundraising and Poetry Competitions**
We recall that Wenxing Hermitage plans to raise beams—its rise and fall are fated. A thatched hall cannot be a painted cake; without the right people, nothing can be achieved.

Though honored by your gracious presence, I have been remiss in offering proper hospitality—may Heaven and Earth bear witness to this heart.

Fine days and fair scenes, rare since ancient times, are now ours to share. Reopening the old shop, let us all share a smile. From all four directions, patrons of old return. Say not that this second coming costs no coin.

What is right today may have been wrong before—yet once a matter has passed, let no blame remain. Neither ill-willed nor unkind, may we meet anew as if for the first time. No empty promises shall be made; only progress sought.

**Memorial for the Restoration of Qilong Temple at Aofeng**
We recall that Qilong Mountain, where banners flutter, holds a true dragon’s lair—its paths winding, peaks circling. To fish from the turtle’s head, one must first claim its crown; where earth is sacred, men are noble.

Though the grove lies shadowed, an ancient temple stands solemn still. For generations, this land has known blood offerings and incense smoke. Yet long has it lain in ruin—its rise and fall fated. Now, renewal is our aim, the divination auspicious.

Phoenixes take flight in radiant splendor, yet we seek no excess. Cocks crow, dogs bark—we glimpse the signs of peace. Gods and men rejoice alike; village and shrine rest in harmony.

Plans are made in unison, but strength must be shared. To raise even a single beam is no task for one timber alone. Beneath the shade of mulberry and catalpa, who would withhold aid? From the great households, middling ones, and humble alike—let each offer what they can.

Inscribe it boldly, repeatedly, without fear of ink. Let many copies of *Zhiyuan* be displayed, that we may await Heaven’s blessings of peace and prosperity.

**Memorial for the Restoration of the Boundary Bridge**
We recall that this bridge, standing for three hundred years, now crumbles—its fate inescapable. In the eleventh month, a temporary crossing was built; the people, unburdened, pledged their hearts.

Yet we must rely on the vows of elders, the surplus grain of monks, the spare wealth of donors—these will ensure lasting sufficiency. To cultivate good roots is to honor good fruits; such is the principle we follow.

Not for fame or profit, but to seize the moment. Travelers shall pass without peril, and descendants know boundless blessings.

**Memorial for Bridge Repairs and Fundraising by Lottery**
We recall the vast labor of mending western walls and eastern partitions. First the lesser, then the greater—let accounts of wealth and righteousness be clear.

Empty words avail nothing; only capital yields profit. A clever wife without flour cannot make even a simple cake. You have tasted the pear—now, in this time of peace, why refuse the passing note?

Thirty lots, no burden to carry, bought and matched in an instant. The first prize, truly refined, wins all acclaim in the poetry contest. Drawn by chance, some gain favor. Witnessed by kin, none dare act carelessly.

Here is the translation:

---

Present matters are neither false nor deceitful. Wealth in the world is ever in use and ever abundant. A thousand-character text bears a thousand-character mark—mutual agreement binds all. Among ten thousand people, ten thousand connections are made, and merit is fulfilled.

Casual village speech may stir the hearts of the young.

**Memorial for Road Repairs**
We recall the post road where travelers come and go, this thoroughfare spanning ten thousand li. The pagoda and bridge stand but a short distance apart, yet they mark the vital junction between two villages. Recently, floodwaters have risen, turning level paths perilous. Though all wish to do good, none can act alone. If we do not plan now, fertile fields may once more become seas—accumulation begins with small steps. Stone upon stone can form a mountain. In this time of peace, why refuse the passing note?

Thanks to the building of dikes, no detours will be needed. By joining in collective causes, we mend this shared ground. A steady step forward—this is the first stage on the road to the capital.

**Memorial for Dike Repairs**
We recall that where water has embankments, floods cause no harm. Where fields know neither drought nor deluge, abundant harvests may be expected—though mountains and valleys shift over time. Years have passed, and the balance of yin and yang has faltered, leaving many lingering ills.

Now we propose to rebuild the dike, restoring the old watercourse. Ten thousand oxen may haul the stone, but only thus can the waters be held back. Baskets of earth piled high will form a barrier strong enough to defend. Though we follow the land’s contours with wisdom, we must also contribute wealth and labor.

Let neither landlord nor tenant make distinctions—together, we secure blessings for generations to come. We humbly request your brush to set these words to paper, that contributions may rise like clouds.

What flows east must first flow west. What is done above will be mirrored below. To channel the Jing into the Wei, one must not shirk labor. To dredge the Huai and clear the Si, there is indeed a Way. Success is certain—granaries will overflow, and storehouses brim. The blessings are many: first wealth, then longevity.

**Memorial for Establishing a Merit-Gathering Ritual**
We recall that in the great Ming, under Heaven’s mandate, all who accumulate goodness receive the five blessings of peace. Now, in this age of prosperity, in towns and villages alike, let us together achieve good karma, each awakening to the task.

Nobles and commoners alike, we humbly request your great brushes. May abundant virtue and power be bestowed. As the people observe the three-month repentance rituals, may four seasons of fortune gather—may the aged flourish, may all prosper.

**Memorial for Prayers and Exorcism**
We recall that when baleful qi recedes and auspicious qi harmonizes, disaster is averted and hardship resolved. The Dao of Heaven is distant, but the Dao of humanity is near—thus we seek favor through offerings.

For the moment a thought arises, the gods take note—yet how can deeds be without consequence? The finest incense is not native to this soil. Wax candles and bright lamps may borrow light from neighbors. Let us uphold the peace of our village and hear the music of a well-ordered age.

Therefore, the noble-minded anticipate misfortune and take precautions. Do not dismiss minor ills as harmless—pray for their removal. In us lies hidden virtue from Heaven.

We pray that the sounds of crowing roosters and barking dogs reach all four borders, that the blessings of the *Hongfan* and *Guizhou* be bestowed upon the people. How could there not be ample grace?

If goodness exists but is not acted upon, what use is it?

**Memorial for Celebrating the Five Manifestations and the Buddha’s Birthday**
We recall that when a single Buddha appears in the world, all generations revere him. The three realms, filled with spirits, gather for the assembly of the Five Perfections. Ten thousand gods rejoice, and the four seas look to him in reverence.

We honor the Buddha’s station: through three asaṃkhyeya kalpas, he cultivated merit across ten lifetimes. On the eighth day of the fourth month, the *mingyú* plant blooms with auspiciousness, bestowing blessings from the nine heavens. Once more, the *udumbara* flower appears in splendor. With hands raised to heaven and earth, he vowed to deliver both saints and mortals.

We, your humble servants, owe boundless gratitude, for we know the source of our blessings. Whether registered households or water-dwellers, though the six senses and six desires remain unmastered, once a year we offer thanks. We prepare pure offerings, invite the World-Honored One, and bear witness together in awakening.

We pray that the Tathāgata may manifest—neither defiled nor pure, neither extinguished nor born. May all blessings reach the people—may they live long, prosper, flourish, and thrive.

**Memorial for the Blood Bowl Ritual**
We recall that women, with their five leakages, endure hardship in bearing and raising children. A mother’s tenfold kindness cannot be repaid, even if one were to grind one’s bones to dust. Some mothers are still living yet lack proper care; others have passed, their merits unfulfilled. How can we not reflect on this? Let us return to the root.

As children, we must recognize the bonds of heaven and earth. By the true person’s wisdom and the Supreme’s expedient means, we establish the Blood Bowl Assembly. Following the repentance rites, we perform the twofold ritual—benefiting both the living and the departed, broadly delivering all beings.

The Savior from Suffering, the Heavenly Worthy, is none other than the compassionate patron. Do not dismiss this as unrelated to monks and Daoists. The Tathāgata Śākyamuni declared himself the reincarnation of Laozi—his merits are inconceivable.

There is no evil in that realm, nor weariness in this. Without concern for gain or accomplishment, we build this bridge, that all may cross and receive its seal as proof. May all turn in reverence, and may every assembly be complete.

From the Floating Black Soil emerges a pearl of millet, no farther than five *zhang* away. The Feathered State’s pond transforms into a lotus throne, ever present within the heart’s square inch.

To express sincerity, we humbly submit this account.

**Memorial for the Daoist Master’s Enthronement in the Luo Tian Jiao Ritual**
We recall the imperial decree of jade, bestowing honor upon the master. Two silver seals—what joy to receive such imperial favor! Never before has this been seen; today, we witness it.

Though will can pierce stone, the body feels light while the feet grow heavy. Reborn, one is not worth half a coin. Yet we hear that good deeds multiply—why not pray for blessings like those of the Kaiyuan era, when bells were cast?

Let no restraint be placed upon the Zhongtong-era literary drafts, that they may ever exist and ever endure, bringing all things to their proper beginning and end. I shall strive to ascend the pole’s summit, while you need not forge iron to serve as a threshold.

Back and forth across two thousand *li* of roads, this karmic affinity is no mere chance. Up and down through seven or eight years, this merit surpasses all former merit. If a virtuous thought arises, let joyful heart follow.

**Memorial for the Daoist Master’s Investiture of the Immortal’s Tablet**
We humbly observe that the Perfected One of Numinous Aid has received the Celestial Master’s provincial document, graced with imperial favor, bestowed a noble title, and exalted in incense and devotion. Though the matters be vast, the rites must not be lacking. With this humble memorial, we reverently knock upon the gates of the lofty, praying that this opportunity of karmic affinity be fulfilled—thus shall boundless merit be attained.

For four hundred years, the temple’s offerings have endured. Mount Ao’s numinous manifestations, the celestial seal of sixteen characters, and the phoenix-script forming its chapters—all evoke the sighs of yellow-haired children and white-haired elders, the reverence of officials and gentlemen. Joy abounds, and multitudes come forth. Thus we extend our sincerest entreaty, for who could doubt the efficacy of seeking the divine?

Half the face, all familiar—yet at the first word, speech falters. This affinity cannot be without cost.
The Dragon-Tiger blessed land lies no less than twenty stages away. Musk and rare incense are no mere pinch of dust.
To seek affairs beyond heaven, one must spend wealth of the world.
Those who have set their hearts, and those who have not—each must regard with true sincerity.
When the hand is ready to extend, let it extend; laugh at those who hesitate and falter.
We dare not demand beyond what is due, yet neither shall we let past opportunities pass again.
We pray that names inscribed may ascend the Buddha’s pagoda, rising layer upon layer, ever higher.
Gazing upon the sacred, emulating the divine—Mount Song’s majesty—we call ten thousand years, ten thousand years, and again ten thousand years.
Below, we cultivate human affairs; above, we repay the nation’s grace.
Though these humble words lack refinement, let them not be refused on account of their plainness.

Moreover:
At the outset of affairs, all follow with eager steps. Yet when faced with wealth, some falter in speech, hesitating to speak.
The glory of this document shines resplendent, revered as if the divine stood at our side.
Who lacks joyful intent? Each harbors a heart of offering.
Though past contributions were not few, they were spent as swiftly as they came.
If the plea for alms cannot be repeated, with whom shall we consult? Why have we come, if not for this? The urgency is plain.
Those who once pledged must not betray their word. Those who now inscribe need not speak of later regrets.
When good deeds are completed, all blessings shall follow.

The Daoist Liao, who befriended Han Yu, learned goodness from others.
Mengchang Jun was not reproached by Feng Huan—no debt shall remain unpaid.

**Memorial for the Celestial Master’s Bestowal of Registers and Establishment of the Jiao Ritual**
To ward off disaster and repel calamity is the unerring duty of the divine.
Upright and enlightened, seeking blessings and invoking favor—this is the common desire of all.
Though the Creator leaves no trace, the Dao’s methods hold their own karmic affinity.
Reverently, for the Numinous Aid Perfected, we receive the Great Cavern’s precious registers—above, to announce to heaven and invoke the sage; below, to protect the nation and pacify the people.
We must rely on rites to dispel demons and guard the true, paving the path to attaining the Dao and ascending to perfection.
Great virtue cannot be surpassed; the common folk, too, shall benefit.
The dragon steeds’ saddles remain secure; their gallop unerring.
Breaking reeds for rafts, the cost reaches a hundred thousand.
Black oxen and yellow calves, each abide in their tasks.
May the Nine Virtues of the Great Norm be fulfilled, and the blessings of the Nine-Five sovereign descend.
Let all pour forth their hearts to accomplish this good work.

**Memorial for the Sculpting of the Perfected’s Return to the Dao’s Attire**
We reverently observe that the Numinous Aid Perfected, having ascended to immortality, must be adorned in immortal raiment.
Of old, the robe and tablet were the state’s favor; now, boots and bamboo hat suit the times.
Neither can be discarded—let both be preserved. Yet neither can remain unchanged; thus, we establish a new hall within.
To sculpt the image of the Dark Sage is to embody the Dao itself.
A crown of azure clouds, a fan of soaring feathers—blending the true form, no human effort alone could achieve this.
Should there be benefactors moved to assist, their merit shall be beyond measure.
Once, in the Yuan and Ming, the true body manifested, its purple-golden form witnessed by all.
In an instant, it vanished without trace. Why cling to a clay semblance? Yet in adorning the spirit to resemble the spirit, it must meet the mark.
Of old, serving in Jin, he wore the robes of Liang’s favor. Now, enshrined in Lu, he dons the cap of Zhang Fu.
Though imitating the fashions of the age, he cannot escape external judgment.
Having received heaven’s favor, his attire must accord with the Nine Chapters.
This requires human contribution—could it be without cost? Let benefactors and patrons each bring forth treasures, pledging their hearts to accomplish this.
The merit shall be great. Reverence lies herein, not in mere appearances.
We reverently pray for the present emperor’s longevity, equal to heaven’s.
May all under heaven bow to the imperial crown, and the myriad lands offer tribute.
May the hundred barbarians don robes and sashes.

**Memorial for the Sculpting of the Divine Image at Qingkou**
The two peaks stand majestic; of old, Mount Ao claimed six lands, gazed upon by all four directions.
Now, the new mound at Qingkou is dismissed as a humble shrine. Yet the divine, vast in power, remains indestructible in true form—this image is but clay, shaped by human hands.
Empty words suffice not; let donations be recorded to clarify.
Consider the artisans—no two possess the same skill. Having stepped forward, let them extend their hands.
All who tend the incense and flame are disciples—let none speak of great or small.
At the ripening of the rice harvest, we shall surely see sevenfold, eightfold abundance.
Though these words are coarse and unrefined, let the good work be completed.

**Memorial for Robes and Attire**
The divine is the people’s lord—how could it not think of them?
Clothing is the body’s adornment; it must befit its wearer.
Moreover, the Numinous Aid Temple is known as the shrine of the upright god—its patched robes are unseemly.
Zixia’s shame at his frayed sleeves deeply shamed Confucius’ sageliness.
The years have passed—why not renew the boots and bamboo hat?
Circumstances demand it; let none say the cap and shoes are misplaced.
In times of war, millet and beans are set aside; sable and cicada ornaments come from the mirror’s reflection.
Adapting to the times—who could say it is wrong?
We have disciples with whom to speak; let great benefactors arise with joyful hearts.
A hundred expenses are not easily met—a single coin is still an offering.
Every stitch and thread requires effort. Gathering fur makes a fur coat—let all contribute their strength.
With the five colors displayed, may abundant blessings extend to the multitudes.

**Moreover:**
Crimson shoes and star-crowned caps, bowing to the Han gods.
The Celestial Master’s favor—Zhang Fu’s cap and Confucian robes.
This is no land of Yue; to imitate foreign dress would be to don outlandish garb.
Ashamed of the worn, quilted robe, we seek brocade to cover it.
Why wait for cold to seek furs? Let us play our part as the scene demands.
Though a hundred holes and a thousand tears cannot be mended, a thousand stitches and ten thousand threads still require effort.

"To say it comes again is not worth a single coin.
This time, a [missing character]—how could it be for great expense?
Since it is neither ghostly tribute nor heavenly rain, let it bring joy to gods and delight to men.
Moreover, in this year of joyous counsel and abundant harvest, why empty the mushroom stores or overturn the granaries? How could we follow the waves of others?
We must dust our caps and straighten our robes—blessings never tire of cultivation, and words leave no room for later dispute.

**Memorial on the Replacement of the Perfected One’s Stolen Robes**

Prostrating, we, descendants of the Five Dynasties’ marquises and kings, revere integrity as divine and honor our thousand-year parents.
In our village, without cause, the sacred headdress was violated. Due to the poor who disregard righteousness, even the virtuous have lost their dignity.
They stripped the robes from the seat of honor, stole the implements of incense and flame, daring to deceive in the unseen darkness—calamity follows those who ignore past ruin.
Though they claim the dead of night knows no witness, they cannot escape the disaster of a single dawn.
All who tend this shrine must not bring shame upon the gods.
Let us unite in a plan to cover the loss; do not惜 the expense of undoing this wrong.
May the hopes of the multitude be fulfilled as in the old statutes.
With brushstrokes swift as clouds, success shall come in no time.
Let there be awe-inspiring might and a model worthy of emulation—gazing upon it, none shall forget.
He who accumulates good meets with fortune; he who accumulates evil meets with misfortune—retribution never fails.

*Xu Xian’s Florilegium, Scroll Twelve Concluded*
*Xu Xian’s Florilegium, Scroll Thirteen*"

**A Letter to Lord Dongcun**

The gods manifest their presence, proclaiming themselves through writings. Yet I, unworthy, cannot compose verses like those for the zither-playing spirit of Xiang, nor dare I presume to expound on the *Yi* as one buried in a tomb. My leaky roof brings shame, and my well-bound view leaves me ignorant. Though I may possess a single scrap of foolish insight, it remains beneath even the lowest of the Three Histories. Now, having been judged in the monthly critiques, I pray you will not fault me as the *Spring and Autumn* does.

The illustrious title of Lingji has endured over four hundred years, its transmission long unbroken. Master Tongzhen underwent ninety-four transformations—who dares claim to understand them? Though fame and profit may differ, literary merit naturally invites public consensus. It is only because Heaven does not withhold the Dao and Earth does not hoard its treasures that the Yellow River yields its chart and the Luo River its writings. Knowing the gods’ designs, one acts upon the slightest sign. The sage does not speak of the uncanny, and the nobleman harbors no doubt. Seen, it is invisible; heard, it is silent—without sound or scent. Vast beyond containment, minute beyond division, it transforms and thus endures. In essence, the upright depend on humanity, while fortune and misfortune, regret and shame lie as plain as the palm. The ancients were swayed by the winds, yet they were people of harmony and humility. In this degenerate age, where are the gentlemen of ritual, righteousness, integrity, and shame? This too is the concern of those who grieve for the Dao—how could I be merely playing with words?

Since the matter is just, why seek others? Empty words are no match for practicing the Dao. To sacrifice to what is not a ghost is flattery; to fulfill one’s intent without self-deception is true sincerity.

I reverently consider your esteemed self, who unites the manifest and the hidden, delving into the profound and the obscure. Your towering presence stands like a thousand-fathom cliff; your vast qi sweeps away armies with a brushstroke. Long have I admired the talents of the Prince of Teng, and now I rashly replace the request for the Luo Chi Stele with this plea, fearing even Bo Jie might pale in comparison. Fortunately, like Su Zizhan, I dare not refuse outright. Relying on your magnanimity as an elder, I am honored by the elegant verses you bestowed upon my daughter and grandson. With the temple’s renovation, the inscribed stone shall be glorified, becoming a model for all ages and a crown for the four directions.

Words cannot exhaust speech, nor speech exhaust meaning; I merely convey this sentiment in brief. Greatness is called sagely, and sageliness divine. One must repay virtue—pray refine my words, and do not leave them unpolished like a neglected wall.

**A Petition from the Villagers to Lord Dongcun**

This ancient temple has gathered four hundred years of dust. Now, a new stele of one thousand and eighty characters stands, its grandeur resplendent. Though its prose may be heard, like the Dipper and the Literary Star, its influence does not extend far. A journey of a thousand *li* is still possible—how could I have imagined the separation of Shen and Shang, spanning years? Never did I expect today to renege on my earlier words. Once, I sent a cage of geese, not the brush of Wang Xizhi; now, I offer no roasted meat to summon the Master’s carriage. Without the medicine of your counsel, I can only grope blindly. Yet I trust in your lofty virtue, which does not forget friendship in poverty.

You have deigned to favor me with fine words, not dismissing the crude as one might reject blue paired with white. I am ashamed of my vulgar speech, which risks confusing purple with vermilion. Permit me to ask: how might I inscribe my gratitude upon the river of your kindness?

I reverently consider your brilliance, which exhausts the workings of creation. Your learning spans heaven and humanity; your writings surge like vast waters, as profound as Han Yu and more majestic than Sima Qian of the Han. Your renown has long been established, and your accolades are many. The Old Man of Stone Islet is no debt collector, and the Woodcutter of the Mountains only knows how to recite poetry. Though you have bestowed the diligence of a letter, I cannot help but cherish the affection of the *tiepao*. If you show kindness to one like me, who would dare forget the source?

I, a latecomer to learning, am ignorant, my childish mind and meager wit adrift in the illusory and indistinct, devoid of radiant and heroic talent. Yet I have already benefited from your nurturing breath and sheltered beneath your canopy. With respect, I arrange these wild geese in formation, reiterating my private plea. Dare I trouble your carriage to grant this heart a fraction of solace?

**A Letter in Reply to Lord Dongcun**

Some ask: The *Record of Lingji Wang* is the king’s own account. If the king has already recorded it, why does he still eagerly seek others? What is his intent?

Among all things between heaven and earth, none is more numinous than the dragon. Its breath transforms, its wonders unfathomable. Yet what it relies upon to manifest its divinity is the cloud. Without clouds, the dragon cannot display its numinous power. This is the meaning behind my request to you.

With respect, I write to express my gratitude.

When Zhang Lu entered the pass, he was already dead. When Ying Hou was at home, Tao Zhu concealed himself. Though Fan Li changed his name repeatedly, his true visage remained that of Gao and Kui. I know not what books Qi studied, but Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang have transmitted this Dao since antiquity. In haste or turmoil, one must hold fast to it. The subtlest essence lies in grasping the mean—this is the heart behind bequeathing teachings to the world. One must fulfill the rites of honoring parents and serving superiors; for a thousand years, its wonders remain unfathomable. Ninety-four transformations may be heard of, but without form or sound, I dare not answer the questions of ghosts and spirits.

To take righteousness as profit is not the way of the bandit Zhi. Though I possess only mediocre talent, I harbor a lingering attachment. Though the Three Rivers do not respond, a single phrase suffices to convey understanding. Why have you come? There is a reason.

One chaste woman shames the wanton crowd; one capped man humbles the topknotted nine. Though this is the way of the world, does it still possess humaneness? Not illuminating the *Great Learning*’s generation of wealth brings deep disgrace to the *Spring and Autumn*’s forthright brush. In this age, who but you could shoulder this task?

Though one may not be human, humanity is not heaven. One must act in accordance with words, and speak in accordance with deeds.

I reverently consider Lord Dongcun, whose virtue bridges the hidden and the manifest, whose Dao spans the obscure and the luminous. On paper, his clouds and mist evoke the structure of Liu’s bones and Yan’s flesh; in his breast, the vast ocean contains nothing but the sentiments of Zhou and the thoughts of Confucius. Thus, he recognizes today’s right as yesterday’s wrong. Never has there been a case where A approves and B disapproves.

A single word carries weight, upheld by the consensus of many. Why seek outward traces when this is already a matter of subtle influence?

Lord Beishan has sons, grandsons, and generations beyond. —*Postscript by Lord Xiecun*

I am honored by your fine composition, which brings radiance to our ancestral shrine. Reading it again and again, I am suddenly struck with insight. Truly, this is what is meant by "whether wise or unwise, the difference is as great as thirty *li*." If I were to compare myself to Master Tongchuan, that would be beyond my daring. Yet I shall place your *Dao Inscription* by my seat, so that future visitors to this hall may read these words, envision your person, and—like seeing a tiger’s pelt—know the roar that once shook the mountain forests and made all beasts tremble.

—*Letter from Lord Xiecun*

Recalling the poems you sent in your sleeve, I remember our brief encounter, where I glimpsed but half your refined bearing. Now, entrusted with composing this record, I behold once more your extraordinary presence—fivefold in its brilliance. Though I long to sweep the path before your gate, I have not yet had the chance. To hold the whip as your attendant would be to gaze upon a towering mountain. My heart holds you in deepest esteem; to know you is to meet Jingzhou—just as I have always wished. Not to have encountered the Lord of Lu is simply fate.

I reverently consider Your Honor, the Prefectural Judge, a man of our native district, advanced in years yet blessed with the vitality of Shao, his hidden virtues sustained by divine favor. His mastery of the classics and cultivation of conduct embody the legacy of the Duke of Zhou’s descendants. His wealth enriches the state, surpassing the achievements of Yan Ying and Guan Zhong. His learning and scholarship surpass others, while his official rank is but a secondary matter. Having found joy in the company of Lord Dongcun, why should he not also wander the wilds?

I dare to bow in humble reverence, grateful to have found recognition in this day. The inscription for the shrine is elegant, its thousand words a testament to refinement. Already, it circulates among the people, its praise enduring for generations, all flowing from your breast. This is no mere carved dragon on paper; even my crude and rustic talent cannot grasp the force of your acclaim. The numinous responds, and the village shrine shines with splendor.

A gentleman forgives others’ shortcomings; even the smallest gift carries weight, its intent as light as a goose feather yet spanning a thousand *li*. I humbly present this pair of carp as a token of my diligence. Your words are worth a thousand gold, and in return, I offer this white goose in gratitude. Deeply ashamed of my rough draft, I can only hope for your indulgence. I respectfully command my ink-brush scribe to gaze upon the thousand volumes of history.

—*Letter on Behalf of the Local Community from Lord Xiecun*

Han Zihua was praised by the Duke of Holding the State—this is the honor due a scholar-official of our time. Xie Lingyun never dreamed of Hui Lian; how then could he have composed those sublime lines by the pond? Where there is such a brother, there must be such a spirit. I revere the man, and thus I revere the deity.

You have deigned to lower yourself to one so unworthy, a great talent bestowing favor. Long have I cherished your humility and virtue, never daring to forget your kindness. All say that the elder brother’s judgment is sound, yet it is truly your guidance that steers us. After three years of offering, I lament my own inadequacy. Even a meager meal, once spoken of by you, gains weight. How dare I borrow a thousand gold? Yet your praise, like the words of a trusted friend, moves me deeply.

I reflect that I have idled in my village, retreating to the mountains. Though poor, I have never changed my countenance. Wherever my gourd bowl has taken me, I have never presumptuously presented my name. Long have I basked in the lingering grace of your river’s bounty, bound as we are by the vines of kinship. Beyond this, I have no other requests, nor do I know what else to do.

I reverently consider you, a man of renowned lineage, a rare talent of our age. Your breast brims with noble aspirations, your brush flows with vast and boundless prose. I am deeply ashamed that my humble gate cannot accommodate a carriage of four horses. I respectfully offer this letter with utmost reverence, though I am unworthy even of its paper and ink.

Your many acts of kindness have long been known, and I have heard of your reputation as a military adjutant. The temple’s renewal is entirely due to the efforts of Governor Yu. This fulfills my wishes, and I offer these words in gratitude.

—*Letter to Commander Zhang*

I prostrate myself in awe, considering how your bold arguments in the court have secured a legacy for a thousand autumns, and how your strategies within the command tent have earned you the enfeoffment of ten thousand households. Your exemplary conduct endures, ensuring prosperity for generations to come. I dare to present my name, seeking an audience before the court. Your humble inquiries are without shame, and the songs of the people attest to your virtue.

I reverently consider Your Honor, the Commander-in-Chief, whose *qi* is vast and unyielding, whose nature embraces benevolence. You govern the people with a parent’s heart and judge affairs with divine clarity. Grievances vanish like thorns cleared from the thicket, while virtuous deeds flourish like sweet pear trees. Your upright governance stands unmatched among the seven prefectures of southern Min. Your noble bearing soars above all, and all gaze upon you as the North Star among men.

Your learning is as deep as the ocean, your scholarship as splendid as the blossoms of the literati. Your brush wields creation itself, conjuring wind and clouds upon the page. Long have I heard of the two peerless talents in your court, and I rejoice in this gathering of shared culture. Your legal acumen rivals that of Cui and Du, still praised to this day. Your critiques of Ou and Yu leave no room for reproach. The gates of your hundred-*li* city stand magnificent, and the thousand-year-old ornamental pillars are renewed.

How admirable! How excellent! I prostrate myself in reflection: I am but a child from a remote village, born in a thatched hut, yearning to behold the pure light of wind and moon, having once sat in the lingering glow of rivers and streams. Now, in this village of accumulated virtue, there stands an old rain-praying temple, repeatedly blessed with divine responses, known as the Spiritually Efficacious Shrine. Its characters are as large as pecks, boldly inscribed and doubly cherished. The inscription is on every tongue, and gratitude for your virtue must be sung anew. This is a grand affair for all time—who would dare to diminish its legacy?

These empty formalities in a wooden box are hardly worthy as gifts. May heaven bless and support you with abundant fortune, fulfilling the sincerity of my prayers. With humble shame and heartfelt gratitude, I offer these words.

—*Letter Requesting an Inscription for the Temple Gate from the Surveillance Commissioner*

The arrival of your carriage in the eight prefectures brings added luster to this blessed land. The Surveillance Bureau, with its solemn governance, extends the protection of heaven itself. I am deeply moved and will never forget this kindness.

I reverently consider Your Honor, the Surveillance Commissioner, a rare talent of our age, a great virtue for our time, whose nature is pure and conduct meticulous. Long has your family been renowned as upright officials in Chengdu. Your heart is upright and your intentions sincere, having personally received the teachings of Master Yichuan. Your lineage has long been praised for its purity, passing down exemplary conduct to your descendants. You uphold the customs of Guān and Luò, making ritual and righteousness the model for Ōu and Mǐn.

Your calligraphy stands unmatched through the ages, your brushwork sweeping away a thousand armies. Lu, Luo, Wang, and Yang are hardly worth mentioning; Ōu, Yu, Chu, and Xue cannot compare.

Truly, your brilliance shines like a beacon, and I humbly submit the sincerity of my earnest devotion.

I reflect with reverence upon myself—a rustic commoner, a humble scholar of the fields, narrow in vision and small in stature, my person attached to ignorance and folly. Yet daring to lift my gaze toward the axe and halberd of your judgment, I venture to present this petition with bowed head.

Here in this village of accumulated virtue stands the ancient Lingji Palace, a sanctuary revered by all the people, where prayers for rain and sunshine have long been answered. I now seek but three characters, though they weigh more than a thousand gold. If you would graciously bestow your wisdom early, my joy would be boundless.

Your Honor’s gate is vast, and I dare once more to present this humble offering, hoping it may reflect even a glimmer of your luminous example. I, a mere well-frog with a child’s understanding, have consulted the oracle again, my ignorance twice over. I can only hope you might deign to consider it, though I cannot fathom the depth of your judgment.

On behalf of the villagers, I present this petition for the Dao’s flourishing at Lingji Palace:

Lingji Palace—what a sacred precinct! A thousand years of incense smoke.
The marketplace of blue cloth teems with people; generations of noble lineages gather.
With shared devotion, we seek to adorn it.
May its august title be honored in the two capitals, its glorious name resound across the four directions.

The writings of later masters that transform the present—those of Wang, Yang, Lu, and Luo—cannot surpass this. Ascending to transcendence and verifying sagehood, the teachings of Zheng, Qiao, Wo, and Quan—might one yet hear of them? With the heart set on carrying forth Heaven’s transformative work and the vow sworn to benefit beings and aid others, compassion manifests in myriad forms, and merits defy measure.

The rites of spring sacrifices endure in their observance, yet the duty of morning and evening devotions may lack its proper steward. Thus, through inertia, we have failed to seek out worthy ones. Who, dwelling alone, might deliberate on the Great Harmony? He who takes goodness as his teacher—such as you, sir, a man of aligned resolve and disciplined conduct—commands the collective plea of the multitude, not for my own sake or out of personal favor.

Should you deign to grace us with your presence, the grandeur of the palace gates would shine all the brighter. Again, as iron is transmuted into gold, we rely deeply on your refining power; as a brick cast forth draws forth jade, we bear the weight of your parallel prose. In my recent memorial, I humbly presented a stern simplicity, my gaze fixed on the smallest details, and now, with reverence, I sweep clean my humble abode, awaiting your arrival with hands clasped, ready to receive your radiance with offerings of incense and flowers.

A thousand-year-old marble pillar, home to the swift-returning crane of immortals. A ten-thousand-year Dao sanctuary, a land as majestic as the Six Turtles of antiquity. From all directions, the gaze of the world turns here; by the strength of the many, it is sustained. To manage it, to cultivate it—whose effort is this? From beginning to end, though it be my heart, it is the heart of the villagers. Yet I fear this flourishing may not long endure, and so, with earnest devotion, I make this plea.

Human sentiment is thorough: they say the plum blossoms by the stream are worth a thousand gold. To see this good work completed would surpass even the wealth of Yangzhou’s ten thousand taels. I humbly request that the High Master Tang Zong establish ten thousand assemblies to assess and honor this endeavor. As the assembly of gods and humans spreads its blessings, your name shall suddenly emerge, summoned forth. With respectful deference, I await your esteemed presence.

I hold in deepest reverence so-and-so, a master whose learning flows from profound sources, whose Dao towers like a mountain peak. You grasp the subtlest mysteries of the profound and empty, embodying stillness and non-action. Your mind upholds the strictness of ritual purification, and your strength bolsters the gates of the teaching. From the first, I trusted in your conduct among men. In this age, who but you could lead?

Now, for the sake of Lingji Palace, we propose a Universal Salvation Assembly, to be held on the fifteenth day of the seventh month, with a special ten-day observance. Three days of fasting will precede the nine-morning rites. Externally, the teachings will be expounded; internally, the *Scripture of Salvation* will be recited. I implore you to condescend to your exalted station and preside over the ritual proceedings. Should the souls of the departed find deliverance, this assembly will indeed be illuminated. Looking up, I rely on your wisdom; bowing low, I risk overstepping.

I, dwelling in rustic obscurity, am but a latecomer—what do I know? Though the Dao may be beyond my hearing, the virtuous delight in doing good. Should you graciously descend from your carriage to take your seat, I shall sweep the mat to welcome you. A single boat can ferry one sixty *li*; I shall forever prepare incense for your long journey. Countless people turn to you in devotion—joining the multitude, I shall not be cast aside.

---

On behalf of the villagers, I petition for the appointment of a lecturer to the capital:

For long have I admired your noble character, not merely for a day. Recently, in discussing the mysteries, I presumptuously knocked at the gate of your cinnabar chamber. You deigned to respond with humility, casting your gaze upon me. Since our parting, this sentiment has never waned.

Now, for the construction of the ritual platform at Lingji Palace, I have conveyed this humble request, entreating you to condescend to travel here. When it comes to the literary style of canonical texts, you are the one the people have chosen. In this age of moral exemplars, who but you could lead? Should you apply the fasting rituals as your own practice, enabling our deities to merge with the likenesses of immortals, how profound would be the blessings bestowed!

The timber of the ridgepole and the pillars of the threshold are not your proper place. Like the vastness of rivers and seas, all streams—even the muddy and stagnant—find their way to you. Should you deign to descend to the altar, I shall hold the broom in readiness.

---

A petition for the appointment of Yue Shan as temple warden:

I, who respond to circumstances to benefit beings, have yet to open the gate of skillful means. Pushing aside the grasses to seek the mysteries, I long to behold the depths of true emptiness. You have graciously taken notice of me, yet for long I have proffered my card in vain, blocked by the long barrier of the western gorge, gazing toward the northern mountains in yearning. Now, having received your command, how dare I not act as your humble disciple?

The divine and enlightened cannot be fathomed—this petty one shall do his best to respond. Were it not for the precepts of the Venerable Master Chonggui, how could Pang the Layman display his spiritual powers? The *Record of the Pavilion of Great Compassion* is now complete; I dare to reveal my Eastern Slope visage. The Dao sanctuary of Caoxi is no small matter—why not take up the Sixth Patriarch’s koan?

Why has the Venerable Master Chonglin come? The Old Man of the Stone Path will surely not cast me aside. Fearing that the golden root may be misaligned, I still seek the jade axe to refine it. Should you deign to take this as your topknot and robe, it would be like moving an old case to this neglected thatch. I offer but a cluster of gourds and a bolt of fine silk, a modest gift like the purple bamboo shoots of Huzhou.

May your Dharma eye open wide; my private sentiments need not be spoken.

---

A congratulatory address on the appointment of Lu Shan:

...

Humbly I recall how the Venerable Master of Guangshun once ascended the jade steps of the imperial court, and how the Xiantong Chan Monastery was graced with the honor of a golden edict and inscribed plaque. What has shone before must surely return in its beginning.

With reverence I consider the Venerable Master of White Deer: the world is drenched in his Dharma rain, the fragrance of compassion fills the rocky cliffs—truly a superior person, blessed with the marks of virtue and power. I look up in awe at the boundless imperial favor, and bow in respect at the proclamation of his appointment. Scaling the lofty peak, he climbs the clouded heavens of the ten thousand years; his voice resounds, "Long live the Emperor!" The great monastery adorns the blessed land of the Three Mountains. His renown weighs upon the Three Mountains, and with a shout he scatters the wild foxes beyond the fields. Joyfully encountering the pair of deer before the hall, the monastic groves stand in solemn order, and the neighboring villages gleam with splendor.

I reflect that I, in my humble station, am without ability, though our bond has long been established. How dare I, with my insubstantial and empty words, venture to approach the wondrous workings of his spiritual powers? Though his name is inscribed upon the pagoda and his writings manifest in his deeds, yet in advancing step by step, his strength surpasses others. Since the Dharma’s cause is no mere accident, the Dao sanctuary will naturally flourish. I now offer my congratulations, never forsaking compassion. Having been graced by the honor of his elephant-borne visitation, I pray he may reveal the sacred auspice of the green waterfowl. For a thousand years, this temple must receive the Buddha’s confirmation. With a single wisp of heart’s incense, one ought to join palms in reverence.

My written words are few, but my devotion runs deep. I humbly present this verse:

Upon the rain-drenched peak he sits,
His wondrous powers vast and deep.
Rejoicing in the weight of royal grace,
His name soars high o’er Ten-Thousand-Year Peak.

*Postscript of Inquiry by Zhou Shenggong*

This Zhou Shenggong is the finest of our hometown—a true "white eyebrow." The common saying goes, "Scholars are often ill-fated," and indeed it rings true. His four-six parallel prose seeks some response, yet this is beyond even my three-fold experience; I can only reply with a few brief points.

All mountains, forests, rivers, valleys, and hills are called *shen*—spirits—yet the Three Offerings hold particular solemnity in the rites. When yin and yang, wind and rain, brightness and darkness lose their proper rhythm, the Six Excesses are already warned against in medical texts. Ashamed of my lack of the mountain spirit’s efficacy, I have instead invited the river demon’s mockery, left speechless and unable to defend myself. What transgression have I committed? One may discern the truth in the end, yet might not misfortune and fortune still confound?

The tortoise does not weary of me; I rely solely on the first divination’s omen. The horse cannot speak, yet I dare inscribe the words "peace" in seal script. The yellow bear appears in dreams—dreams born of thought. The red snake transforms into a shadow—yet even shadows may be doubted.

These are all the toils of spirit and thought, leading to calloused hands and feet. The illness, long afflicted by frost and dew, is a calamity without cause. The moon now lodges in *Dou* and *Niu*—whose fault is this? If Heaven wills it, what can be done about fate?

As the common saying goes, "The Master does not speak of the uncanny." Yet I have humbly received the brush’s sharp reproach, and deeply shamed the wooden scholar’s lack of letters. Forty-nine years of delusion—only now do I glimpse Qu Boyu’s regret. Twelve thousand breaths of *qi*—yet never have I heard the name of Wang Shuhe.

I would rise from nine deaths to a single life’s remnant, to master the teachings of *Yongming’s* seven charts and eight wrappings. Though a hundred medicines have been tried in hope of some chance success, if even one man remains unhealed, it is called your failure. Why must you overturn and declare, "There is that which stands with Heaven and rains upon Earth"? The people of Yue revere reproach; the people of Chu revere ghosts—can their customs not be changed? The *Wei* stomach’s obstruction, the *Qi* stomach’s blockage—what use are those physicians?

Having not repeated the same error, I may yet take one more journey. I regularly rub the jade sphere—who reads the *Heji Jue*? Moreover, the parallel prose of four-six rhythms is itself a masterpiece.

Having consulted the oracle again and again, I glimpse at last the true circumstances. There comes a time when no medicine is needed—yet how can brush and ink suffice to record it? Your prayers have been long and earnest; what more could you say? Since your kindness has pressed me so, I cannot refuse.

Though the broken-arm prophecy proved true, it did no harm to the benevolence of Yang Shu. Though the lost-axle kin were unharmed, it did not diminish the renown of Lou Shide. Twice have I sent letters of mourning, each time shamed by your commands. Though a gentleman may grieve in adversity, must he assume the guise of a petty man?

If Heaven does not summon me back, what can Bo Liao do against fate? For great renown must endure the trials of the altar’s pit, and dark spirits often rise in the wake of lingering illness. What crime has the crane committed? When will the phoenix’s forging be complete? Even a lame tortoise may yet take one more journey; the bedridden rooster can still reconnect. Yet I fear the mockery of “washing feet and losing wealth,” and dread the reproach of “extending a hand only to cast the next stone.”

Since you have spoken to me so earnestly, I recall your footsteps with joy. To repay enmity with virtue—even the old fist finds no surfeit. Strive in learning and conduct, and may you never forget your former steps. To share another’s sorrows and rejoice in their joys—even Confucius and Mencius found this hard to articulate. To drown where others drown, to hunger where others hunger—this was the vow of Yu and Ji.

To skillfully save all things is to waste nothing. To speak when one can speak is to never misspeak. To seek a six-foot vine, one must first cultivate three-year mugwort.

My reply, written in haste, still harbors the fear of a bladder’s rupture. I would rush out in bare feet to welcome you, yet wait for your carriage’s arrival. I raise my whip in gratitude—why hesitate to follow in your steps?

**Marriage Proposal for the White-Water Bridge Alliance**
I recall that our two families, having borne sons as clever as paired fish, now unite for a second generation. The oracle confirms the harmony of the mating phoenix. The Zhus and Chens share an old bond; the Zhengs and Weis renew a long affection. We pledge by the green mountains and swear by the white waters. The karmic cause is now fulfilled—how fortunate we are!
